CONDOLENCE MOTION

Honourable Gill James AM

[12.00 p.m.]
Mr ROCKLIFF (Braddon - Premier) (by leave) - Honourable Speaker, I move -

That the House expresses its deep sadness at the death on the 24 May 2025 [Bookmark: _Hlk215576282]  of the Honourable Gillian Hilma James AM, the first woman Deputy Speaker of the House of Assembly from 1979 to 1980; first woman Minister of the Crown from 1980 to 1982; and a member of the division of Bass from 1976 to 1989 and 1992 to 2002; and places on record its appreciation of her service to this state; and, further, that this House respectfully tenders to her family our sincere sympathy in their bereavement.

It is a great privilege for me to be standing here today to celebrate and honour the life and contribution of the Honourable Gill James AM, Tasmania's first female Cabinet minister in the history of our parliament. 

In doing so, Speaker, I acknowledge her son, Ken, in the Chamber with us today. You have our deepest sympathies. How proud you must be of the contribution of your dear mum. For all the words we can use to describe her - commitment to community; work ethic; but also, clearly, a trailblazer and a leader and a role model for women in Tasmania, women in politics and women in our community - I have no doubt that the contribution of your dear mum has inspired many to be not only standing for politics but contributing to their community.

What an outstanding achievement we speak of today, and one that will never be surpassed.

Gill passed away on 24 May this year at the age of 90 and, again, to her family and friends, my deep condolences. Not only was Gill a trailblazer for women and a person who was deeply respected across politics, she was a friend, an advocate for workers and veterans, and a quiet achiever.

Since the 1960s, Gill was an active advocate for ex-service personnel, appearing before the Veterans Review Board and Social Security Appeals Tribunal to secure what they were entitled to. 

Gill remained a champion for her community her whole life, serving not just as a member for Bass for 23 years, but also as patron of many well-known organisations including the Tasmanian chapter of the Vietnam Veterans Motorcycle Club, the Launceston Art Society, the Northern Tasmanian Netball Association, the Huntington's Disease Association and the Vietnam Veterans Association of Australia Launceston sub-branch.

She also helped establish Launceston's first shelter for homeless men during the 1980s and was awarded the National RSL Certificate and Badge of Merit and made a life member of the RSL in 2003. She was appointed a legatee for Legacy. 

Gill was made a Member of the Order of Australia in 1990, received Honourable for life in 1997 and was later introduced into the Tasmanian Honour Roll of Women.

Gill was born in Launceston and educated at St Helens Primary School and Launceston State High School. She was married in 1961 and had one son, her beloved Ken, who we also honour today. I understand that even today, Gill is the longest serving female member of the House of Assembly since women first won the right to stand for parliament in 1921.

Prior to entering parliament, Gill worked in the offices of former president of the Senate Justin O'Byrne; and former deputy prime minister, Lance Barnard.

In 1979, Gill was the first woman to be appointed as a presiding officer - Deputy Speaker. Then, in 1980, as I said, she became the first woman to become a Cabinet minister, with the responsibility for public and mental health, consumer affairs and administrative services.

A few years ago, in 2022, the member for Launceston, Rosemary Armitage spoke very eloquently about Gill in the upper House to mark International Women's Day and had this to say: 'Gill has been regarded as one of the state's best grassroots politicians, and despite coming from a family of Liberals,' I understand, 'formed her own Labor-aligned views.' She also said that, after so much work in her community for veterans, Gill became known as 'Mrs Fix-It', and that 'her hard work at the coalface getting positive outcomes for the people who needed them made her a force to be reckoned with. Quiet, unassuming, not necessarily high profile, but very well loved'.

I also know former Speaker, Michelle O'Byrne, held Gill in very high regard, saying she 'remained glorious in her later days defending and advocating for those who needed her'. Michelle and I spoke about politics a lot, but also about people who influenced our careers, and certainly Gill was one who had a big influence on Michelle and her aspirations.

Mr Ferguson, a member for Bass, also paid tribute to the loss of a 'true old-school advocate for workers and veterans', and reflected that the 'whole community has lost someone special'. Very kind words, Michael, thank you. I can imagine Gill being here today in this parliament, still very much holding to those values, still fighting for people and injustice, and still giving her all to help others.

I have been a student of politics for many, many years. I often sat at school and read newspapers - editorials, opinion pieces - and I developed a passion and love for politics and an interest in all sides of people's views and what drives them to parliament. I cannot remember many, as I read through newspapers and things at the time in the Tasmanian State Parliament in the era of Gill James, but for some reason I remember Gill James. She was always there, speaking very passionately, but always in those pages surrounded by community and surrounded by the people she was advocating for so strongly. That's my memory of Gill, as a student of politics and it is etched in my mind. Notwithstanding, of course, her retirement and other areas of Gill's contribution, I was made aware of her trailblazing for women in politics and her very strong grassroots advocacy as well.

Having entered parliament in 2002, just after Gill's contribution, I know that, should Gill have continued in parliament, I would have learned a lot about grassroots politics from her as well. She may well have had some empathy for me, given her 'supposed' family background of Liberals - maybe. Nonetheless, we need more Gills in parliament and in public life. 

Can I say, Ken, it's lovely to have you here today. You will listen to people speak eloquently and true as to their views of how Gill influenced the community and indeed individual lives as well. When you do so, I know you will do so with a great deal of deserved pride and admiration for the contribution of your mother in this place and, most importantly, in her local community.

Again, my sincere condolences to all those that knew and loved Gill. Vale, Gill James.

Members - Hear, hear.

[12.10 a.m.]
Ms FINLAY (Bass - Deputy Leader of the Opposition) - Honourable Speaker, today we honour the life and the legacy of Gill James AM, a woman who made history in this parliament, and did so with a spirit that was as cheeky as it was courageous. Whenever Gill was in a room, everyone knew about it. Her smile, her colour, her energy could not be ignored. Gill, as she was known to many of us, had a number of extraordinary achievements: breaking barriers for women in politics, championing veterans, and helping establish Launceston's first homelessness shelter for men. She was honoured as a Member of the Order of Australia and inducted into the Tasmanian Honour Roll of Women. 

Perhaps her greatest legacy is the path that she paved for others, showing that leadership could not only be bold, but it could be glamorous and deeply human. Gill was a trailblazer, and that word's been used so often today and in recent conversations. It's used throughout every excerpt describing the most wonderful Gill James, and it's true. I think it's true that Gill was as cheeky and glamorous, she was utterly fearless, and she never lost her fire. The first woman to serve as a Cabinet minister in Tasmania. Just imagine that - imagine what that would have been like. It says so much about who she was. The first woman Presiding Officer - a woman who made history and absolutely did it in style. 

Premier, you mentioned the fond relationship between former Speaker, Michelle O'Byrne, and Gill James. I was in attendance at the funeral, and I felt the warmth and the deepness of the relationship. I know one of the things that I learnt that day about your mother was that she was often seen driving around Launceston in a sports car. That's something that Michelle referred to on the day. If I have my memory correct, something that I remember is that not only did she drive around in a sports car, but often she might actually relieve the ministerial driver and drive the car up and down the highway. I love that and what it says about a person.

Not only did she make history, but throughout her entire life, she remained active, right into her last years and her last weeks. She continued to defend and advocate for those who needed her. She was an incredible storyteller, and she continued to tell stories with that great sparkle in her eye. One of the things that's been recounted is that even in her final weeks she was delighted by the outcome of the then-federal election and still debating the politics with passion. I feel sad to say, Premier, but she was fiercely Labor. She may have grown up in a Liberal household, but there was no way she was aligned with your side of the house.

Honourable Speaker, it's actually a privilege to stand and speak to this motion on behalf of the Labor Party. It's an honour to be able to do it in the presence of Ken and it's something that for many of us, has actually provided the opportunity for us to be here. Today we honour one of the greatest true believers, Gillian Hilma James. She led a life of service, particularly to ex-service personnel, and again right up until her last moments, she was honoured and thanked. There was an immense amount of gratitude by many of the ex-service men and women that she had supported and their families.

Born in 1934 to a conservative-leaning family, Gill's childhood growing up in the north-east in St Helens and then being in Launceston, exposed her often to social injustices and disadvantage. That's what ultimately led her to devote her working life to community and to Labor. Gill's first job in politics was at the ripe old age of 19 as an electorate secretary to the Labor Senator Justin O'Byrne. In 1963, she joined Lance Barnard's office, initially as Mr Barnard's personal secretary, before becoming his head of office and most trusted confidant. Gill was by his side as he rose. As Lance became the president of the Tasmanian branch of the ALP, the Deputy Leader of the Labor Party, and ultimately the Deputy Prime Minister in the Whitlam government, you can only imagine the extraordinary conversations and opportunities that they had as they worked side-by-side and the decisions, the impact that Gill was party to during those history defining years for our party and for our country.

In Gill's words, reflecting on her experiences with Lance Barnard, I'd like to provide some quotes direct from Gill. We travelled all over Australia, more than Gough Whitlam. Lance set out to bring Labor to government. We went to every tiny outback town imaginable. I was the scout who found out how people really felt about things. They wouldn't tell him.

Then, once in government, Lance took on 13 ministries and Gough 14. People were constantly shuffling in and out of the office to get things signed and to get things done. That Labor government changed more in a short time than was changed before, or has been since and Gill James was there. She was a part of it, and no doubt she was leading it.

The incredible Gill James then set out about making her own history here in this place. Gill was first elected to the Tasmanian parliament in the 1976 state election as the first woman ever elected in Bass. She was the only woman elected to the Tasmania parliament in that 1976 election. She recalled later that one of her big responsibilities upon being elected was to record key points in the debate for the Premier because this was an era before Hansard and she was the only member of caucus proficient in shorthand.

On the Parliament House website there's a photo of this Chamber taken in November 1977, and you are immediately drawn to Gill's bright, smiling face, dressed all in white, amid a Chamber of men in tan and grey suits. That is the glamour of Gill James. I wonder if, when that photo was taken, Gill could have imagined a day like now, where women make up very close to half of all the members of the Tasmanian parliament.

Upon being elected, Gill quietly but defiantly set about breaking through the glass ceilings in this place and with the images that I have of her in her sports car, I like to imagine that she's not breaking through glass ceilings, but that she's smashing them through in her sports car. In 1979, she became the first female presiding officer as chair of committees before becoming Tasmania's first female Cabinet minister in 1980. 

She wasn't just eased into Cabinet with a junior ministerial role; she was loaded up with portfolios. Public and mental health, consumer affairs and administrative services, and as Tasmania's first female minister, the formidable Gill James' elevation to Cabinet naturally captured a lot of attention and with it, unexpectedly, some thinly veiled chauvinism. One of her political opponents described her appointment to the challenging Health ministry as a kiss of death. Premier Doug Lowe said that it would force her out of Cabinet within 12 months. Well, I'm going to quote Gill again -

I was delighted with the member's terminology that Mr Lowe, the Premier, had given me the kiss of death. I was kissed by many Liberals after the election and have received many calls from doctors in Launceston. I'm very glad to have the Health ministry and if none of the past ministers have made an impact, I'm about to start.

I love that, I love the cheeky, I love the confidence and I love that she was just in there to have a go. Gill remained in Cabinet for the remainder of the Lowe and Holgate governments before becoming a senior member of the Labor Opposition where she continued to make history in becoming Tasmania's longest-serving female MP.

In the 1989 state election, Gill lost her seat by only 150 votes, but rather than lament her misfortune in not being a part of another Labor government, she dusted herself off, kept working in the community and achieved one of the all-time greatest comebacks in Tasmanian politics, not only winning back her seat at the 1992 election, but topping the polls for Bass, and that's what I love about Gill James, the determination.

Gill described her comeback as going from a rooster to a feather duster and then back to a rooster again. She continued to serve in this chamber for a further decade, eventually retiring in 2002 after a remarkable 23 years representing the people of Bass. Never one to crave the spotlight, Gill always credited her electoral success to the work she did in community helping people. In a newspaper article in 1998, she said that she couldn't remember the last time she was interviewed on television and that she deliberately avoided the media. 'If they're chasing me or I'm chasing them, I can't be attending to the people who actually need me', she said. 'I don't need to make a big noise; I've got my grassroots support.' 

A very large part of that grassroots support, came from the extraordinary work Gill James did with war veterans, war widows and their families. Over a lifetime of public service, Gill will always be remembered as much for her work with servicemen and women and their families as her time in this place. With a husband and a brother who had both done national service and a stepfather who was an ex-army man, Gill's work with veterans began when she was in Lance Barnard's office. In 1967 she was appointed as an official representative before the Veterans' Review Board, and Mr Barnard's office became the place to turn to for vets and war widows in Launceston when they were needing help. 

Gill is thought to have assisted literally thousands of Tasmanian veterans and their families over the course of her career. Perhaps one of the most moving memories I have of her service was the Guard of Honour at the end of the service. It was moving, it was genuine and there was an absolute gratitude in that room for all that she had done for so many.

With no regard for electoral borders, she'd work with anyone who came into her office for help, poring over records for long hours and familiarising herself with legal and medical terminology. Some veterans would work with her for years in advocating for entitlements and pensions so that they could live the remainder of their lives out with dignity. She carried the responsibility seriously and personally. She described the veterans she represented as 'the people whose fates are still in my hands', and the community loved her for it.

Throughout her lifetime she was named patron and awarded honorary memberships and life memberships to many organisations, including veterans' clubs and associations, the RSL, the Launceston Totally and Permanently Disabled Soldiers Association, the Vietnam vets motorcycle club - so many organisations. She worked so broadly across the community.

The association's chairman at the time, Roy Johns, summed up Gill's approach when he said:

There are so many people who can't put their words together, who have a wonderful story but can't tell it. Well, she's been the one to tell the story for them. The widows, they worship her. She's a member of parliament, but that's not what they see her as. When the others are off socialising, she's in her office fighting for the rights of ex-servicemen.

Like so many working women, Gill balanced her public life with family life. I know that there is no greater bond than that between a mother and a son, and that must have been an amazing way to be raised and incredible to watch and be part of.

She loved yachting, she loved her cars and had a lifelong passion for speedboat racing, which I also love. In 1990, Gill was named as a Member of the Order of Australia and granted the right to use the title 'Honourable' for life. In 2005, she was inducted into the Tasmanian Honour Roll of Women.

Gill James will be remembered as an icon of this parliament, an icon of our community, a legend of the Australian Labor Party, a trailblazer for women in politics and a tireless advocate for the people across our Tasmanian community. On behalf of our party and grateful Tasmanians, we honour her lifelong service and her memory and offer our deepest condolences. In this moment, we know that she will be watching down, smiling cheekily and wishing us all well. Today, we stand to honour her life and her legacy. Vale Gill James.

Members - Hear, hear.

[12.24 p.m.]
Dr WOODRUFF (Franklin - Leader of the Greens) - Honourable Speaker, I rise to add my words of condolences to the family, friends and members of the Labor Party who knew and loved Gillian Hillma James - Gill James. I especially add our condolences, thoughts and full respect to Ken for all of his mother's life and what she contributed to the community.

She was the longest-serving woman member of the House of Assembly since women won the right to stand in this parliament in 2021. She was elected twice, as we've heard, from 1976 to 1989 and again from 1992 to 2002, but those facts hide the reality of what she achieved through her career. By her legacy she's shown what true community service looks like and we've heard a lot of examples. Gill was obviously a woman who never rested and devoted herself to standing up to injustice and calling it out and used her precious time on Earth to work for other people, and that is really evident in what she did.

She grew up, as we've understood, in a Liberal voting family, but in her teenage years she became lit with the passion of the policies of the Labor Party in the 1960s, which were really calling out injustice and crying out for change, so much so that she was successful when she was 19, just out of school, in getting a job with Labor Senator Justin O'Byrne. She left school, went to Canberra and went on the journey of federal politics, which is a massive step for a young Tasmanian woman from Launceston.

After the birth of Ken, her son, she was asked to work for a short stint with the deputy leader of the Labor Party, Lance Barnard, in 1962, but she was obviously so capable and effective - also maybe there was the Launceston bond, because I understand that Lance Barnard was also from Launceston; two Launcestonians in Canberra - and so respected that she remained with Lance Barnard until he left politics as then deputy prime minister of Australia in 1975.

Gill didn't stop there. She took the passion of the Whitlam era and what had been achieved at the national level and brought it to Canberra, and as we've heard, she stood and was successful in entering into Tasmanian politics in 1976. It was at a time when she used the energy of what was happening nationally with the feminist movement, with women, with the work that Julia Ryan did as the Labor first ever minister for women, and she came down to Tasmania and smashed through some of the many layers of glass that hold women back. There are still some more to smash and we're all doing our best to do that, but Gill has been there and was the first woman to become a Cabinet minister. As we've heard from Ms Finlay, the member for Bass, that would have been quite an awesome space to enter into and she didn't just take up a small job, she took up a massive job accepting the health and mental health portfolios as well as the consumer affairs and administrative services portfolios that we've heard about.

She also continued her work with the Labor Party and was for many years working with Lance Barnard when he was the deputy president of the Labor Party and then working here with the Parliamentary Labor Party when she was a member of parliament as the whip. She was also deputy chair of committees and held a number of other roles that were also firsts, so she was a woman who was not afraid of being out in front and she obviously carried with her a passion for why it was important to do that. 

She worked with Lance Barnard when he had the defence portfolio in Canberra from 1972 to 1975. In the contribution by Rosemary Armitage MLC, she talked about the fact that Lance Barnard had some Vietnamese orphans that he raised, so she was personally affected by returned servicepeople's experiences and by a connection with the Vietnamese community that galvanised her for the rest of her life in the work that she did for returned soldiers. 

She was a life member of the Vietnam Veterans Association and a patron of the Tasmanian chapter of the Vietnam Veterans Motorcycle Club. Maybe other members can confirm whether she ever got on a bike. I reckon she probably did because it sounds like that's the sort of woman she was. She was also involved in a whole range of things, the Launceston Art Society, the netball association in northern Tasmania, Huntington's disease and we've heard a range of other things. I suspect it doesn't tap the depths of the things that she was involved in because she was a giver, no doubt about that.

She completed her 23 years of service to Tasmania, 23 years ago and - so many firsts for women. She walked where no woman had walked before. She opened doors that we women in the Chamber follow and other women will follow. We will push through other doors because we have Gill James, with all her energy, passion and spirit, in our back pocket, all of us. A place like Tasmania is better thanks to people like Gill James. We are grateful for the service she gave to Tasmania and Tasmanians.

Ken, I want to say, on behalf of the Greens, we can see your mother was very special, and she gave a lot and I'm sure she gave a lot to you, too. Her legacy will be enduring for the better. Vale Gill James.

[12.31 p.m.]
Mr BARNETT (Lyons - Deputy Premier) - Honourable Speaker, it's a great privilege to rise and join the Premier, the Deputy Leader for the Opposition, the Leader of the Greens and others in this place to pay a tribute to Gill James, pioneering Tasmanian, a trailblazer in Tasmanian politics, and a leader in health, housing, community services and the veterans' community. I acknowledge that up front. I want to acknowledge Ken in the Chamber today and, of course, pass on sincere condolences and best wishes to Ken and family, friends, former colleagues and all the communities that she represented in the seat of Bass and elsewhere around Tasmania.

Gill James is an outstanding woman and I had the privilege of getting to know her during the 1990s, but more specifically in the early 2000s when we started the campaign to support Teddy Sheean for his Victoria Cross. It was in those early years where there was little confidence in our campaign and our quest for a VC and for justice for Teddy and I wanted to pass on a special greeting and thanks from Garry Ivory, the nephew of Teddy Sheean VC, because he not only approached myself - and we worked together - he also approached Gill James, after her retirement in 2002. After those many long years as the longest serving woman in this parliament and the first minister in this parliament in Tasmania, which was already outstanding, she acted as a volunteer and she helped us through the bureaucratic maze, as it were, through the various tribunals that we wanted to approach. Gill James gave excellent advice to Garry Ivory and, indeed, myself and others and I wanted to acknowledge that.

I got to know Gill James as minister for Veterans Affairs and parliamentary secretary responsible to the then premier Will Hodgman. She was always standing up for those doing it tough. She stood up for the vulnerable and, specifically, the veterans and I wanted to acknowledge that today in this condolence motion because of my personal contact and friendship with Gill. We would meet at the RSL, Tasmania Launceston branch meetings and events from time to time. She was a wonderful supporter of Legacy.

Many in this House know my grandfather flew in the First World War and did much to support and get Legacy established in Tasmania, particularly in Hobart as president, and had other roles. Gill was a wonderful supporter of Legacy, particularly in Launceston, and caring again for the vulnerable, for those who couldn't help themselves. Gill James had that kindness of spirit, she was caring and she was always reaching out for other people.

Of course, she did have that wry smile and, I would say, cunning wit to get the job done and to find the way through on behalf of her constituents. I think the word formidable has been used and I concur with that remark.

She received the national RSL Certificate and Badge of Merit, which is a great honour, and honorary life membership from the RSL. She was a great supporter of the Vietnam Veterans. I have had many interactions with the Vietnam Veterans, I still do. They are a wonderful organisation, and Gill James was often at those meetings, or she would be supportive in different ways, shapes and forms during the 2000s - again, post her paid career in 2002 - and she was doing this as a volunteer. She was an advocate for the veterans, standing up for their entitlements, for income support and protection, and she did it for the vulnerable.

I met with and worked with Gill James from time to time when I was minister for Housing for a couple of years and she was, as has been indicated, standing up for the homeless and vulnerable Tasmanians in and around Launceston, not just the women, but the men. I think that's been noted in the remarks today and I want to again pay tribute to that because - caring for the vulnerable, the homeless, she certainly stood out. She was a quiet achiever, certainly as minister for health and mental health, and consumer affairs - all portfolios which I have held or now hold, so I can relate to her determination to not just be a poisoned chalice, but to make a real difference. She was a big reformer in the health space and mental health reform in that role. That should be acknowledged. I wanted to put that on the record.

The other area where we came across each other from time to time is in the area of support for people with motor neurone disease. My dad, as people in this place and elsewhere know, had motor neurone disease. Mum was involved as patron for the Motor Neurone Disease Association Tasmania and Gill James would come and support our association, our support group in Launceston. I'm clearly very grateful for Gill James' support and note the wonderful care and commitment she's given her community. She was a trailblazer - it should be acknowledged - and I can say a wonderful tribute for her life and legacy. Vale, Gill James.

Members - Hear, hear.

Mr FERGUSON (Bass) - Honourable Speaker. I loved Gill James, she was a beautiful person, and it's a really important moment in this House for us as a collective, while not all able to speak, but for us as a collective, the members of the House of Assembly to pay tribute to such an assertive, remarkable, witty, kind, and successful member of this House. I'm very grateful for the fact that I, amongst others, knew her and appreciated what she brought to Tasmanian public life and to improving the lives of her constituents, with a primary focus on Bass, our northern Tasmanian community. She couldn't help but also be a force for good for people right around the state and, indeed, the nation. As a fellow member for Bass, I want to take this opportunity in speaking to this condolence motion. Thank you to the Premier for moving it. Thank you for the Deputy Leader for seconding it, or speaking to it, and others.

I want to thank the late Gill James AM for her service to this House and her service to the people of Bass and Tasmania over many years. It's been beautifully articulated by others already. I was very saddened to receive news in May of the passing of someone I encountered as a dear friend and former member of this House. Elected in 1976 - an altogether different era to the times that we're now living through. That was a groundbreaking moment, remembering it was within a year of the defeat of the Whitlam government, not just by the dismissal, but by the election that followed. That was a significant breakthrough moment for her personally and for her party here in Tasmania, which was elected in Tasmania despite the groundswell of Liberal support federally around Tasmania. Nonetheless, in Tasmania, Labor was returned in 1976 with Gill James' support. To note that she was the first-ever female Cabinet minister in our state is a tremendous honour for her and her side of politics. When I think of the achievements of Gill James, I immediately think of people like Sue Napier, who broke through the same kinds of barriers and achieved the same kinds of great successes as an eminent Bass politician who was also female. Notably, she was defeated at the 1989 election, where we saw the defeat of a Liberal government only by a single vote because of the Labor-Greens accord. That was the election where she was unsuccessful and many would have thought, 'Well that's the end of your time', but it wasn't to be.

The 1990 Queen's Birthday Honours List saw Gill honoured as a Member of the Order of Australia, and I note that that records it was not for service to the parliament but for service to the parliament and the community, a vital distinction, because the work she did as a parliamentarian, in my opinion, was the minority of the work she did as a public figure for Tasmania. As has been well described, for many years her work in the community, particularly but not only with veterans, TPIs and war widows, was something that was her life's work.

She was returned to this House in 1992 as, I think, a personal act of defiance against her defeat in '89. She topped the poll for Labor, and in 1992 to return for Labor at an election that the Liberals won was a big achievement. Remember, two sitting Labor members lost their seats. That's trailblazing if ever you heard of it. She was granted the right to use the title honourable for life in 1997 while still serving as a member of this House, which I think is a sign of the respect she enjoyed across the Chamber.

I would like to share the cheeky grin she always wore with that bright red lipstick, and she retired on her own terms in 2002. Few will admit it, but every member of this House wants to leave this House one day in just the same way - admit it. She retired on her own terms. She was the longest-serving woman of this House ever to date, and I personally suspect that that record will last a very long time - 23 years is a very long time. She was also inducted into the Tasmanian Honour Roll for Women in 2005.

I just want to speak for a moment personally. Despite our political differences, I can't remember us ever having a difference and we spoke many times. Our mutual love in particular for the members of the Good Neighbour Council of Northern Tasmania and enjoyment of the Friday get-togethers served as a wonderful place to really get to know each other well, as two MPs for Bass who did not serve at the same time. We would enjoy those times over some 20 years, and I can say with gratitude that our mutual respect ran deep and it always will. She was always beautifully dressed. I don't know so much about the sports car, but she had a lot of class and she wanted to look the part and she did. It was a way of enjoying life.

One of the major things we shared in common politically was our work as Health ministers. We both served as ministers for Health, and let me tell you, Speaker, Gill encouraged and supported me from the beginning. She was a terrific person and she was kind to me.

The Labor Party should be proud to count Gill James amongst its alumni. The Labor Party has lost a true old-school advocate for workers, for veterans, for the TPIs, for the widows and for the legatees, the children. She really was a quiet achiever. She was not a personality to seek credit, but she deserves all the credit we're pouring upon her life today.

That heritage that we reflect upon was adopted while working for other Labor luminaries including Justin O'Byrne, a prominent Tasmanian Senator at the time, and of course Deputy Prime Minister Barnard, who himself had had a long history in the electorate, following in his own father's footsteps.

Her funeral at Franklin Grove on 6 June was well attended. The tributes to her life were just as reflective, warm and uplifting as that beautiful autumn afternoon in northern Tasmania. It was beautifully presided over by Michelle O'Byrne, who was then our Speaker as well. The honour guard by the veterans was a very moving mark of respect and admiration that the veterans and that community in particular had for Gill. It really carried the honour and respect that the entire electorate and the broader community held her in and it was a beautiful display. 

Gill reached the glorious age of 90 and she kept that beautiful smile until the end. Her legacy is one of caring for people with a sense of true personal and lifelong dedication and making a difference in the lives of many Tasmanian people and their families.

As I close, I wish to pay particular thanks to Gill's family, because Ken, you and your late father and the late Peter, Gill's long-term partner, shared Gill with us, our Tasmanian parliament and our Tasmanian community. You may not have had much say in that, but you did it nonetheless, you shared her with us and the public. That is an extremely generous gift by the family and supporting, no doubt, Gill throughout all of those years. I particularly thank her family and offer my sincere condolences to them and all who loved her. Vale Gill James, may you rest in peace and thank you for your service to Tasmania. 

[12.47 p.m.]
Ms HADDAD (Clark) - Honourable Speaker, something that is lovely about condolence motions is hearing such beautiful tributes to members across the political divide. To hear such touching words from the Premier, the Deputy Premier and the member for Bass show the connections that we build in this place. Sometimes that's not what the public sees when they see us fighting on the news, but to hear what a profound impact a member of the Labor Party had on the work of two currently serving Liberal members is really meaningful and I wanted to start my contribution by recognising that.

I also want to pay my respects to Gill James and to her son, Ken. Thank you for joining us here today in the Chamber, Ken, to be able to hear the words of many of the current crop of Tasmanian politicians and how much respect we had for your mum, Gill. She was, as we've heard others say, the first woman Cabinet member and the longest-serving female member of this House, and I agree with the member for Bass that 23 years is a remarkable level of service and I dare say that record will not be outdone anytime soon.

Gill was the first female Cabinet minister, later to be joined by others in the Labor Party, including Judy Jackson and Fran Bladel, and they are women who served in cabinets at a time when women weren't really expected to be there at all. She worked in the old federal Parliament House as a staffer to Lance Barnard at a time when women weren't expected to even be in that building as staff, let alone as members. 

I will tell a very short story of an event I attended in this place last week, a reading of a children's book called A Loo of One's Own, and thanks to some of the other members who supported that event. That was the story about the fact that when the first female members of the Australian Parliament were elected there were actually no female toilets in that building at all, a really tangible sign that women weren't expected to be there. Yet while those first female MPs who were elected had no toilets to go to, there were plenty of female staff in that building at that time, including Gill James. 

Those elected female members, one from each side of the political spectrum in each House of the Australian Parliament, went to many of those female staff members and said, 'What do you do?', and the answers were extraordinary. They would go home in their lunch breaks, or they'd walk five minutes to a building nearby to use the bathrooms, so we're literally talking about not only smashing glass ceilings but smashing porcelain bowls as well with the women who served in our parliaments in those early days of women first being elected to represent our communities. I will finish that anecdote by saying it was an astonishing 30 years before a female women's toilet was actually built in old Parliament House. Luckily they did remember to build some when the new Parliament House was built.

I want to recognise that the stories those early elected women in the Labor Party shared with those of us who have followed in their footsteps are really profound, and many of us would not necessarily have the opportunity to even be serving in this place if it wasn't for people like Gill, who told us stories about - we all kind of cop a bit in this place from the public, on all sides of politics, but it was so much more visceral for those early elected women. These were women who, even in an environment with no social media, were really derided and not taken seriously, yet made an enormous contribution not only to their communities but to this parliament and to the ministries that they served in. Without the contributions of Gill and those who joined her later, Fran and Judy, many of us might not even have been here.

I do want to recognise the really special connection that many in the Labor Party had with Gill, particularly Michelle O'Byrne. I know that Michelle would have really liked to have been here for today and, indeed, intended to be here for today's condolence motion and couldn't be due to travel commitments. She sends her fond regards to everybody here, and I know has already done that personally with Ken and Gill's family, and presided over the funeral earlier this year.

I think Gill's life and her legacy in this place are marked by her tenacity. The member for Bass, Mr Ferguson, mentioned it as well, that not only did she return after losing her seat, but she topped the poll. I think that is a really telling return to this place, that she wasn't willing to go down without a fight. She returned to this place and served for a significantly long period of time after that - all up serving the people of Bass for 23 years. 

Not only did she make extraordinary contributions to the ministerial portfolios that she held, but also to her community around, as we've heard, housing - a particular passion; the rights of returning servicemen and women, their widows and their children; and to working people across not just northern Tasmania but across the state and the country.

We're very lucky to be able to stand in this place and share our condolences with Ken and Gill's family, show our respects to an extraordinary woman who gave a huge amount to this place and to our state.

Members - Hear, hear.

[12.52 p.m.]
Mr O'BYRNE (Franklin) - Honourable Speaker, it is wonderful to hear those contributions to Gill. She was a giant of the Labor Party and the Launceston community. On a personal level, she was a very good friend of my sister Michelle, and a mentor to Michelle and many other young female aspirants to either political office or to social justice and social change. She was a friend of my mother, and she was a much-loved member of the Australian Labor Party Sisterhood in Bass.

Gill was a fan favourite amongst the rank-and-file members, not because she sought political office for personal power, but because she demonstrated her values by seeking political office to lift other people up. Her involvement in so many community organisations in Launceston, and her quiet impact, is profound - and it is still being felt today. You can just imagine, her life is essentially personified by service to others.

To drive, as a young woman in the late 1960s and 1970s, putting her hand up to work in political offices, travelling to Canberra - Senator Justin O'Byrne, who at that stage was the leader of the Senate. Also working with Lance Barnard, a significant figure in not only the Labor Party but in Australian politics in the 1960s and 1970s, and being witness and being present at all of the - We've heard all the stories of the 50th anniversary of 'the Dismissal'. Well, Gill was there. She was a part of that, and no doubt those Whitlam years impacted her theory of change about how she would effect change. Her decision to run in 1976 would have no doubt been impacted by the events of the Dismissal; the events of the 1972 election of the Whitlam Government and the massive social and economic change that that triggered.

It's no surprise to me that when Whitlam made the decision to bring the troops home from Vietnam, at that time Vietnam vets were not treated very well. I'm sure that had an impact on Gill's role in working with veterans, particularly Vietnam veterans - that they deserved better than the thanks this nation gave those veterans when they returned from the Vietnam War.

She survived the 1974 Bass by-election, when Lance made the decision to resign from federal parliament to take up an ambassadorial role. That was a very tough time to be Labor in Bass. That was a moment where my mother, when she was handing out how-to-votes in the 1974 by-election, was struck by a Liberal voter saying, 'How dare you offer me this how-to-vote for the Labor Party.' My mother replied - and I'm sure Gill would have echoed - 'I dare. I dare offer you this how-to-vote card.'

All of us across this Chamber feel the benefit of someone who valued grassroots connection, who respected community, who was there when it counted, and didn't just turn up for the election but turned up every day of every week to represent her constituents.

Tasmania is a far better place for Gill's service. Vale, Gill.

Members - Hear, hear.

[12.55 p.m.]
The SPEAKER - I, too, would like to rise and honour the extraordinary life and service of the honourable Gill James AM, a woman whose service, vision and lifelong commitment to community has left an indelible mark on Tasmania. I also, too, give my deepest condolences and sympathies to Gill's son, Ken James, in this Chamber today, her family, friends, loved ones, the Labor Party, and all who knew and loved Gill.

My godfather was John Treloggen, who was Gill's brother. As their houses backed on to each other in Gascoyne Street, Kings Meadows, growing up I saw Gill on many occasions, as I did John and Elaine and their children Joanne and Richard. Gill and her husband, Peter, and Ken lived just around the corner from where I, too, lived in Kings Meadows.

It says a lot about Gill's humility as a person that before I could vote, I sadly admit, I never realised she was an elected member of parliament. That's because I just remember Gill as a very smiley, kind, witty, very classy and lovely lady, who was always very happy to say hello and who did drive a very nice sports car.

It was only much later that I gained a true appreciation of what a great trailblazer Gill truly was, and the immense and important significance of Gill's many firsts for women: that Gill still remains the longest-serving woman member of this House; that Gill was the very first woman presiding officer in Tasmania; and that Gill was the very first woman minister in Tasmania.

Gill's long-term advocacy, her life serving others, and her outstanding patronage of many, many organisations continued long after Gill's retirement, ensuring that Gill's enduring contribution to Tasmania lives on.

Gill James's life reminds us all that true service does not end when we all leave this parliament. Our legacy can endure, evolve, and it can continue to shape the world around us for the better.

All of us in this Chamber are a richer and stronger community because of Gill's service and all of her efforts. So today, I join with you all in honouring Gill's service, celebrating Gill's legacy, and I, too, commit to carrying forward the spirit of dedication that Gill so profoundly embodied.

Vale, Honourable Gill James AM. We here in this Chamber, along with your family, friends, loved ones and all who knew and loved you, will remember you.

I now ask all members in this House to please signify their support by joining me in standing.

Motion agreed, nemine contradicente.

Mr ROCKLIFF (Braddon - Premier) - Honourable Speaker, I further move -

That a copy of the foregoing resolution be forwarded to the family of the late Ms James.

The House suspended from 1.00 p.m. to 2.30 p.m.


MINISTERIAL STATEMENT

RA Rodda Museum of Pathology - Coronial Investigation into Unauthorised Human Remains

[2.30 p.m.]
Mrs ARCHER (Bass - Minister for Health, Mental Health and Wellbeing) - Honourable Speaker, I rise today to update the House and the Tasmanian people on the government's response to the coroner's record of investigation and findings in relation to human remains retained following coronial autopsies between 1966 and 1991 at the RA Rodda Museum of Pathology in Hobart.

I want to start by acknowledging the deep hurt that this has caused, especially for the friends and family of loved ones affected by these historical practices. These revelations have been both traumatic and devastating and this has been made very clear to the government, both firsthand through meeting with those impacted and through other members of parliament. 

As members are aware, the coroner's report found that specimens taken during postmortem examinations were retained and exhibited in ways that disregarded the dignity, autonomy and cultural beliefs of individuals and communities. The coroner confirmed that between the 1950s and early 1990s, 177 specimens of human remains were retained without lawful authority or consent. To the family and friends of those individuals, we offer our deepest and most sincere apology. This abhorrent practice represented a profound breach of trust and a failure to uphold the ethical standards expected of health and research institutions. 

It is important to state that these actions occurred prior to the Coroners Act 1995 coming into effect. There are rigorous processes that have been implemented by the state forensic pathologist to ensure that this will never happen again. 

It is also important to make clear for the record that the Department of Health has advised that the three current forensic pathologists employed by the department were not involved in these historic matters. 

Given the horrific and unacceptable nature of these circumstances, it is very clear that more needs to be done to ensure that everyone involved is held accountable.

The Attorney-General has already written to both the Director of Public Prosecutions (DPP) and Tasmania Police to make them aware of the coroner's report. I can confirm I have also instructed the Department of Health to undertake further work to establish, as far as is possible, the identity of any employees within the department during the period of the 1950s through to the early 1990s who may have been involved in the retention of remains without lawful authority or consent. 

While the coroner's report identified one individual, now deceased, as being primarily responsible for the unlawful retention of body parts and did not identify any others who may have been involved, it is important for the families that we do what we can to investigate further and if further information is found, to pass those details on to the appropriate authorities. As the coroner's report said:

It appears at this stage that now-dead forensic pathologist Dr Royal Cummings was the person who provided the large majority of coronial specimens to the museum. However, it also appears that his predecessors and successors also engaged in the practice.

Given these comments in the coroner's report, I have directed the department to review all available historical records and to engage with UTAS to establish any further information relating to the unlawful retention of specimens which can be provided to the relevant authorities for their consideration and potential action. 

I can also confirm that the government will facilitate a formal parliamentary apology on behalf of the state to appropriately recognise the immense harm caused. It is our intention for the formal parliamentary apology to be delivered in the first half of 2026, and I can assure everyone that the families of those affected will be closely consulted in its development. This formal apology is an important step for the parliament to acknowledge and apologise for the pain and trauma associated with these practices. 

It is important that we take care to coordinate and deliver this apology in a trauma-informed way, allowing families who wish to be present to be here and to allow members from all sides of parliament to participate. We will work with members of parliament to coordinate the timing and format of the formal apology and we thank those members from both Houses who have represented the concerns of the families and assisted families through the coronial process, which we know has been very difficult for families.

Honourable Speaker, as the Minister for Health, Mental Health and Wellbeing, I am very committed to ensuring the highest possible standards from my department. While we cannot change the past, it is vitally important that we learn from what has happened and ensure that we do all we can to hold those responsible to account, and that's exactly what we will be doing. I thank the House.

[2.36 p.m.]
Ms HADDAD (Clark) - Honourable Speaker, I move -

That the Ministerial Statement be noted.

I would like to commence by also expressing my sincere sympathy and condolences for the grief and trauma experienced by families affected by this situation. I acknowledge the unimaginable pain that these distressing historical practices have caused and the profound shock of learning that the remains of their loved ones were taken and retained without their knowledge or consent. This harm has deeply affected individual families and has been felt across the wider community.

Families have been in touch with many of us across this parliament to express their distress. Certainly, I've heard from people even as recently as this morning, not in the context of today's ministerial statement, but just in the context of the coroner's report being released, that they remain very hurt and shocked to learn through media reports and public notices that their loved ones' body parts had been taken and retained. I know that others have found it very meaningful to hear from those families who have undergone compounding grief and distress as a result of that discovery.

I wrote to the Attorney-General about this issue on behalf of several constituents and I'm glad to know that he, as we heard in the Minister for Health's statement, has written to the Director of Public Prosecutions and Tasmania Police about the coroner's finding and that the Minister for Health, who we've heard from in this statement, has instructed her department to conduct further investigations to ascertain as much as possible the identities of employees within the department from the 1950s to the 1990s who may have been involved with this unlawful retention of remains. I think that's very pertinent because, as we heard from the minister, the coroner's report names one past staff member who likely was involved with this, but it's inconceivable to think there wouldn't have been others who were also involved.

I commend the minister and the government for progressing to a parliamentary apology. That is a very solemn and right obligation that we have as members of parliament to express that publicly to families affected on behalf of the decision-makers at that time.

I want to close my contribution by sharing a little bit of one of the deidentified emails I received from a family member affected. I've removed his name and some relevant dates so he can't be identified. He wrote specifically to request a committee of inquiry into this matter and said that in his case, he was visited in 1976 by Tasmania Police who came to his workplace to inform him of his father's death. He attended the Hobart Police Station shortly after and was escorted to the morgue to identify his father's body, and during that time he was informed of his father's cause of death. He wasn't asked whether his father had thought about donating organs and didn't sign any consent forms for organ removal. His father had requested cremation in earlier discussions while he was still alive and respecting his wishes, all of his ashes were scattered at sea following the funeral service.

Fast forward to 25 January 2025, and on page 50 of the Mercury is a notification from the coroner listing 126 deceased persons who were identified by the title coronial project, indicating that organ specimens were removed from those deceased persons without coronial authority, without next of kin knowledge nor approval, forwarded to the UTAS Rodda Museum, labelled with the donors' identity and information and displayed for public view for the next 40-odd years. He said:

Recognising my father's name listed, I was shocked and disheartened. A feeling of hollowness pervaded me for some days. It was a second grieving period.

He goes on to explain that he knows many other families have been affected and describes it as an abhorrent abuse by medical professionals including, but not limited to, pathologists and others at all levels during organ removal and specimen delivery stage. He said the Rodda Museum management and staff, again, at all levels, as the receivers and catalogues of these body parts in bottles who signed specimens out to the Royal Hobart Hospital and who accepted the specimens at the Rodda Museum during the 30-odd year period with no questions asked.

He finishes by saying that those deceased people affected left behind mothers, fathers, brothers, sisters, daughters, sons, grandparents and grandchildren and we owe it to apologise to those families.

[2.41 p.m.]
Dr WOODRUFF (Franklin - Leader of the Greens) - Honourable Speaker, I want to thank the minister for Health for bringing this ministerial statement. It has been waited on for a long time by the affected families and I want to first recognise the families who were affected by what happened with the unlawful taking of parts of people that they have loved and describing them as specimens and putting them in bottles to be, as one family member put it, ogled at by other people for research or as a curiosity. People have spoken of their grief, shock, anger and trauma that they feel for not having this information and realising it was not something that only happened to them, it was systemic and it went on for decades.

The coroner has uncovered that, over 33 years, 177 individual samples of human remains were retained by the Rodda Museum at the University of Tasmania. That would be, I imagine, and people have talked about, extra hard to think it was a systemic practice. It wasn't an accident or a one-off, it was something that continued over decades. It was essentially normalised to unlawfully make decisions on behalf of grieving families, to take parts of the body of the person who died and to see it as an object for medical research without having a discussion with the families. It's utterly out of step with people's expectations today in Tasmania of how a grieving family should be treated. It is utterly out of step with the wider community and it sits particularly horribly with the families and other loved people of people who died.

It was quite clearly noted in Coroner Simon Cooper's report. He said:

The belated discovery that human remains were removed at autopsy and not returned to the body has been a source of pain and anger for many families.

The retention of human remains without family or coronial approval, or even knowledge, is a historic practice out of keeping with, and offensive to, contemporary standards and values. It is inconceivable to my mind that it would ever happen again although the fact that the practice continued for as long as it did and ended only comparatively recently is almost equally inconceivable.

It is important to make the point that the coroner's report noted that it was unlawful behaviour. It can't only be put down as an accident or a benevolent action. It was unlawful behaviour, as the report says:

There was no jurisdiction for a coroner to authorise the removal or retention of any part of a dead body for any purpose other than for investigating the manner and cause of death for that person. Similarly, any part of a dead body that had been removed and retained for legitimate coronial purposes should have been subject to a burial warrant in consultation with the family and replaced in the body prior to burial.

We know these things didn't happen. We obviously look forward to, and thank the minister for talking about making, a formal apology - something that has been discussed with all the families involved, so that people have time to know about that.

I end with saying it is a fair question that people want to have all the answers to the things that the minister raised. People want to have the confidence that can only be achieved by evidence that this could never happen again and that checks and balances are in place. That is important information for justice and healing.

[2.46 p.m.]
Mr BARNETT (Lyons - Deputy Premier) - Honourable Speaker, I rise to acknowledge the statement of my colleague and friend, the Minister for Health, Mental Health and Wellbeing, who's made this on behalf of the government.

I will also start by acknowledging the hurt, pain and suffering of many of the family and friends of loved ones affected by these historical practices. I acknowledge the trauma and the pain, and this has been made very clear to the government directly, to me and my office, the Minister for Health, Mental Health and Wellbeing, and to others. I acknowledge the feedback from members of parliament in this place: the member for Braddon who asked me the question about this not so long ago and the shadow attorney as well. Thank you for that, certainly to the family and friends of those individuals. Like the Minister for Health, Mental Health and Wellbeing, we offer our deepest and most sincere apologies.

As Deputy Premier, Attorney-General and minister for Justice, I've taken a series of steps to respond to these findings and to the concerns raised by families and members of this parliament. I've worked closely with the Minister for Health, Mental Health and Wellbeing, Bridget Archer. As the minister relayed, in the first half of next year the government will facilitate a formal parliamentary apology to the families of those affected by these practices.

In relation to the potential breaches of the law and questions of accountability, I have written to the Director of Public Prosecutions, Daryl Coates SC, and to the Commissioner of Police, Ms Donna Adams PSM, APM. In those letters, I've drawn the coronial findings to their attention. Any decisions about criminal investigation or prosecution are and must remain matters for those independent statutory authorities. It is not for the government to direct those outcomes, but it is our responsibility to see that the relevant bodies are formally aware of the findings. Additionally, as the Minister for Health, Mental Health and Wellbeing said, her department has been directed to review all available records to ascertain whether there are other individuals who can be identified as having engaged in these practices.

I've heard very clearly the concerns raised by the handling of individual cases associated with the Rodda Museum, acknowledging the distress caused by the late discovery of retained specimens and the processes which followed. These concerns are being taken seriously and I can confirm that work is underway to review the Coroners Act, including to identify any improvements relating to the provision of information and support for families, friends and others in difficult and sensitive circumstances. Throughout this engagement, I've remained mindful of the independence of the court and the coroner, while ensuring that legitimate questions concerning lawfulness of the retention of specimens, accountability and transparency are properly addressed.

I can inform the House that I've instructed the Tasmanian Law Reform Institute to review the Coroners Act 1995 and the Coroners Rules 2016; with a particular focus on:

Any desirable changes to jurisdiction, powers, practices and/or procedures of the coroner and the division that would better service the needs of people interacting with the Coronial Division and the needs of the community; and

any improvements to be made to the provision of information to and support for the families, friends and others associated with the deceased person who is the subject of a coronial inquiry, including, but not limited to issues regarding autopsies, cultural and spiritual beliefs and practices and counselling services.

The TLRI review provides an important opportunity to ensure that the lessons of the Rodda Museum matter are fully absorbed into the future coronial practice.

The Tasmanian Law Reform Institute (TLRI) process will also provide a pathway for affected families and indeed, the broader community, to share their experiences and insights. I'm acutely aware that for many, telling their story again can be retraumatising. For that reason, I've asked my department to liaise with the TLRI to offer assistance on how to best support those engagements.

The University of Tasmania has already issued a public apology, acknowledging the hurt experienced by families as a result of these historical practices. There is understandably an expectation that the state will also formally acknowledge this role. That's why the government will facilitate a formal statement and parliamentary apology for these historic practices, and I want to place on the record my deep regret for what families have endured. 

What happened was wrong - it should never have occurred. Families were entitled to trust that their loved ones would be treated with dignity, transparency and respect, and that trust was broken. For that, we are profoundly sorry. Thank you, Speaker.

[2.51 p.m.]
Mr GARLAND (Braddon) - Honourable Speaker, I rise today to welcome the update provided to the House by the minister for Health. However, on behalf of the families who have contacted me, I must also offer some reflections in reply. While the government has acknowledged what has occurred and the hurt it has caused the families and friends involved, what is not yet fully understood or recognised is that many of the deceased passed away in deeply tragic and traumatic circumstances. While all deaths are unfortunate, coroners only become involved when the death is unexpected, unexplained, or where the state or an individual may have contributed to the circumstances of that death. 

I've been rendered speechless by the harrowing stories shared with me by affected families - stories of loss, trauma, and lifelong grief caused by those deaths. For many, the wounds left by those deaths have never fully healed, and for some, the state itself was responsible for the failings that contributed to their loved one's death. To be wronged by the state decades later is almost inconceivable. These revelations have retraumatised these families, bringing with it the sleepless nights, grief, anguish, and a sense of torment - as if they had not suffered enough already.

Adding to their pain, many families first learned of these revelations through a full-page newspaper advertisement. You have acknowledged that the display of the remains in the RA Rodda Museum disregarded the cultural beliefs of the individuals and communities, yet after these revelations came to light, the government ignored certain families' cultural beliefs when decisions were made on how to dispose of the remains. These families have every right to demand answers about this dreadful situation. How could it have happened? Who was responsible? Who knew? Why did it continue for so long? Why did the state take so long to act and even longer to tell the families? Why were requests from families for burial or return of the remains ignored?

I want to acknowledge the member for Nelson in the other place for early and tireless advocacy on behalf of many of these families, yet even her efforts were met with initial silence from the government. That silence forced families to go to other MPs, like me, to the media, to statutory bodies, even to federal representatives, simply to have someone listen; to have someone acknowledge their pain and try to obtain the answers they deserved. The coroner's findings in September provided some answers, but raised many more questions, questions families have put to the coroners office and the Attorney-General's office, questions for which they had received no response until today.

When you understand what these families have endured, the initial silence from the government was not merely frustrating, it was deeply hurtful. For some, it reinforced the belief that something was being covered up. Families have told me they felt disrespected, disregarded and ignored after everything they had already suffered, and this, understandably, made many of them angry. This is why I've continued to raise this matter in every parliamentary sitting since it was brought to my attention, why I've written to the Attorney-General and why I've pursued these issues during budget Estimates.

Here we are now, almost a year since families were notified via newspaper notice of this profound failure by the state, the minister is providing families with an update. It should not have taken this long. 

To the Minister for Health, I thank you for taking the time to provide this information today. Your indication that a formal apology is forthcoming is welcome. I hope the government will enable impacted family members to attend the apology at no expense to those families.

I urge the minister and the department when engaging with the families to take the time to understand the unique and deeply personal impact this has had on every family involved. Let them finally be heard, and give them answers to the questions they have. They deserve at least that. Otherwise, an apology without listening, without understanding and without answers will be seen as little more than an empty gesture. Thank you, Honourable Speaker. 


[2.55 p.m.]
Ms FINLAY (Bass - Deputy Leader of the Opposition) - Honourable Speaker, I rise in response to the Ministerial Statement today, and thank the minister for the statement and for acknowledging the gravity of the issue. It's been a deeply distressing matter for families and for the broader community, and it goes to the heart of trust, dignity and respect for those who have passed. 

The coroner's report revealed that 177 human specimens were retained by the Rodda Museum between 1966 and 1991 without the knowledge or consent of the families, and some were publicly displayed. These practices were described by the coroner as offensive to contemporary standards and values, and inconceivable that they continued for so long.

The impact on families has endured renewed grief decades after losing loved ones. Some have had to re-bury remains. Others discovered their loved ones' names in a public notice. The trauma has been compounded by the way the information was communicated - advertisements in newspapers, lists online - often without the adequate amount of support or sensitivity. For many, this has reopened wounds that had barely healed, and it's shaken confidence in our institutions.

Today, while we welcome the statement and the commitment to a formal parliamentary apology - that we've been confirmed will be in consultation with affected families - this is an important step towards healing. In the Ministerial Statement, it was important to note that there is the intention for a formal parliamentary apology, and that it will be done in a way to coordinate access for family members that are affected.

Literally, at this moment, I have had some correspondence come into the office asking about the way to facilitate that support and if it can be done without cost or impediment to families, some of which may have to travel distances, with support for accommodation and transport. It would be appreciated if that was looked into.

It's also, in the Ministerial Statement, important to note that it is clear, for the record, that the current forensic pathologists were not involved in those historic matters; and there continues to be investigations that are appropriate, the Attorney-General himself having referred the matter to the Director of Public Prosecutions and Tasmania Police; and that there be a review that's happening around the Coroners Act.

The apology, when it happens, obviously needs to be meaningful and trauma-informed, and accompanied by action. This is not just about pathology, about law or about history, but it's really about humanity and dignity. We have to ensure, from now, that every Tasmanian can trust that their loved ones will always be treated with respect, both in life and in death. Each member of this place that has spoken today, let us all make sure that this is the turning point for how we handle sensitive matters, and that it is done always with compassion and accountability at the core, so that this never happens again.

[2.59 p.m.]
Ms DOW (Braddon) - Honourable Speaker, I wasn't going to speak today, but I feel compelled to do so, and I feel compelled to do so on behalf of those Tasmanians who have reached out to me about their experience. I know many have to many members of this place and I, too, along with my fellow members of the Labor Party, want to put on the record my sincere condolences to those Tasmanians who were affected and let down by the system.

Those Tasmanians that I've met with, this has deeply impacted them, many of them for their entire life. As Mr Garland said, many of them, in fact, experienced terrible deaths of their loved ones that this has only compounded and made worse, and brought up many traumatic experiences and deeply impacted them years later. 

The way in which they found out, I believe, wasn't appropriate - for it just to be in a newspaper ad, in the daily circulating newspapers. My understanding of the communications that they've had through the coroners office and others, at times has been really difficult. I  would agree with Mr Garland when he said that the government's response has been tardy. I believe it has been as well, and not just this current government but previous governments when this issue first came to light. It is, as Ms Finlay says, an absolute injustice to these Tasmanians and their loved ones and they deserve the respect of this parliament and of the Tasmanian community. I know through those I've worked with, many of those remains went on to be cremated without their permission, which is absolutely abhorrent. I can't understand how this practice occurred in our state and I, like others, want to see no stone left unturned to ensure that we find out exactly why it happened and those who were responsible are held accountable. 

I hope this provides Tasmanians who have been affected and their families with some comfort today, but I think there's a long road to go and this is the first part of that process. An apology will be important, but there are other steps in that process that need to be undertaken by the government to continue to provide support to these Tasmanians. 

As others have said, I think it will be difficult, not only financially but geographically, for many of these families to be able to come to Hobart and hear that apology firsthand, so I want to put on the record today to the government to please consider televising that in other parts of the state or making forums available for people to come together in more rural and remote communities to hear that apology on that day. I think that would mean a great deal to those people. 

I want to conclude my contribution by saying how important it is that there is ongoing support for these families, because many of them have been let down before by the state through traumatic ordeals and circumstances around the deaths of their loved ones. They deserve that ongoing support from us and I believe that's the very least we can do.

Motion agreed to; statement noted.
