(Premier)

[3.06 p.m.]
Mr ROCKLIFF - Thank you, Chair, and my apologies for being a touch late to the lectern. Thank you to all the people who provided scrutiny throughout the course of the week around budget Estimates. I very much appreciate all the work that goes into asking the questions and the research, and indeed the work of our departments as well. I also very much appreciate the ministers and committee members and chairs, so thank you.

I'm very proud of the fact that Tasmania continues to be a state that is strong, confident and full of opportunity. Our government has a clear vision for the state to make Tasmania the best place to live, to work, to raise a family and of course to invest as well. The ambition is not just an aspiration; it's a very real target for us. In this place I often reflect on the achievements of the last 10 years, not our achievements but every Tasmanian's achievements. I do this because Tasmanians ought to reflect on what they have achieved and be very proud of these achievements, because there's plenty Tasmanians have done to help grow, improve and develop our state and improve our services. It is important to reflect on those achievements we've delivered together and take notice of the positive contributions so many Tasmanians have made to our state.

It's through the collective efforts of many Tasmanians over the past 10 years that we've been able to deliver a $40 billion-plus economy, record low unemployment and over 51,000 new jobs created and a leader when it comes to business confidence in the nation. I often refer to these achievements in this House because they are a positive thing for Tasmanians. At a time when the Tasmanian community demands certainty, stability and responsibility, we're able to show that our economic plan for this state is working. There are more people working, more businesses thriving and more opportunities for more Tasmanians. This is what our plan has delivered over the past decade and will continue to do.

We need to be realistic, however, and there's many challenges that all Tasmanians have faced along the way. There will be other challenges that will come up as well and we have shown that despite these challenges, we've been able to deliver a better Tasmania. Under our plan, we will continue to invest in the areas that create even more opportunities for Tasmanians and I'm very proud to do so and continue to rise to the challenges as they present themselves.

We are here today on the back of an interim budget that followed an election that Tasmania didn't want or need. The usual six-month period we had to produce a budget was compressed into two, and we need to be realistic and acknowledge that the interim budget is a reflection of that. Still, the interim budget shows that we are continuing to invest in our communities, our industries, our businesses and growth in Tasmania and provide improved services. We are continuing our record investment in our education system, our healthcare system and infrastructure and investing in Tasmania's future. 

The interim Budget underpins our plan that is delivering a better, fairer and stronger economy, and I'm proud of Tasmanians for achieving that. I want to thank the Treasurer very much for the work and stewardship that Mr Abetz has done on the interim Budget. I know he will thank his Treasury team and staff as well, but I also want to pay tribute to their work in a short amount of time and the work on the next budget has started. They have been doing an outstanding job.

Chair, last week we released our latest update on our 100 days of delivery and it demonstrates our government's record against our first 100 days following the election. What this demonstrates, is our continued commitment to give Tasmanians that certainty and that stability and make delivery a key aspect of our agenda.

What it also is very good at doing, is ensuring that myself, our ministers and our departments are accountable for the delivery and the commitments that we have made and continue to make. In the 100 days since the recommissioning of our government, we have delivered on 465 initiatives to deliver action on the cost of living, health and housing. One example is the delivery of cost of living relief for families through our universal school breakfast program and our expanded healthy school lunch program, which I have often spoken about as helping our young people to engage in education by removing significant barriers, to ensure that they are in school and a learning environment where they feel safe and most comfortable to learn.

We're getting more Tasmanians in a home, the delivery of a more social and affordable housing and delivering initiatives including the increased first home owners grant and stamp duty concession. We're expanding the scope and quality of our healthcare system through service and scope of practice expansions and investing in breast screening and diagnostic services. When it comes to health service delivery, I'm very proud of the work that successive Health ministers have done in cutting red tape, improving access to primary healthcare services and, most notably, as we speak of the scope of practise when it comes to our pharmacies and allowing our pharmacists to do more. If my memory serves me correctly, there's been 10,000 treatments to the women of Tasmania through our pharmacies for their health concerns that would otherwise have been through the local GP. We know getting to see a GP is a challenge for all sorts of reasons, and alleviating that pressure allows people to be supported with their healthcare sooner.

In our next 100 days, we will add an additional 148 items to that list among the many others that will no doubt be delivered. Taking action on the cost of living and providing more housing options for Tasmanians; building a better healthcare system and delivering a stronger economy. We will build an even better health system by assessing expressions of interest for the new TassieDoc program to provide a further four 100 per cent bulk-billing clinics and access the grants program to extend pharmacy operating hours.

We will release the second draft of legislation to establish TasInsure, following public input into a discussion paper and the receipt of expert independent advice to enable Tasmanians to access cheaper, fairer insurance.

We will introduce legislation to freeze changes to the National Construction Code, cutting red tape and helping home builders avoid unnecessary costs and delays and we will establish a roundtable with councils to look at options for fast-tracking rezoning, specifically in nominated housing growth areas to reduce timeframes. And, because our strong economy underpins all that we do, we are continuing this focus with a position paper to be released on Tasmania's 2030 Economic Future Act, a landmark legislation to streamline approvals and unlock investment.

All of these are part of our 148 initiatives because we are keen to keep ourselves accountable, on track and continuing to deliver and getting on with the job.

Turning to some of the matters raised during Estimates, again I thank all those who engaged in scrutiny and relevant to the committees which I attended, the Macquarie Point Stadium, as it has often done, and in the last few years in the 2022, 2023, 2024 and 2025 scrutinies, establishing our very own AFL team. It will give our young people aspiration, transform Macquarie Point from an industrial wasteland, create thousands of jobs and give Tasmanians exactly what they've been fighting for decades. Put simply, we cannot afford to lose this opportunity. Again, I pay tribute to all the 15,000 people who attended the pro rally on the lawns of Parliament House on Sunday. It was uplifting, particularly on a rainy day, to have people attend from all across the state and speaking with one voice about this project.

I also pay tribute to those who have not supported the project and who have scrutinised, asked questions. Each question that was asked and information provided, has helped us to be in a position and we will be in a position, of course subject to the Legislative Council's support, to be the best project possible. No doubt at the next budget Estimates I'm sure we will be scrutinised as well with respect to the delivery of this project.

We also spoke about reinforcing public confidence in the salmon industry's long-term sustainability and our commitment to robust oversight and the government has announced a range of measures. 

Our support for TT-Line was also a matter of scrutiny as well and this is absolutely expected given the forum that we're in. Our commitment was to get the Spirits back on track. We have done so; that's what we're doing and we are ensuring that TT-Line has the resources it needs to get the job done as well. We have provided assurances around the company's solvency. We will always support our government businesses and, frankly, why wouldn't we?

Salmon, of course, again appeared throughout Estimates. As I say, we want robust oversight as well. I thank the members for their questions, most notably coming from the honourable member for Franklin, Mr George and the Greens members as well. What our commitment to the study will provide is to enable all stakeholders to focus on transparency, science and best practice for protecting our environment and the continuous improvement of an industry that we all value for the work that it does, the regional jobs and economic contribution it makes to rural and regional Tasmania. Our objective with the study moving forward is to ensure the continuity of a highly valuable, the largest primary industry in Tasmania and the ongoing contribution to our community. We believe, of course, it's important that we continually improve to strengthen public confidence and provide investment certainty for the future, and these actions reflect our commitment to the sustainable future for salmon farming in Tasmania.

Again, I would like to congratulate the Treasurer for his efforts with respect to the interim Budget, as well as Treasury officials and staff for developing the interim Budget in a compressed timeframe, as well as their efforts to commence the budget development process for 2026-27 state Budget to be handed down in May next year.

Thanks also to the Chairs of the Committees and all members and support staff for their work during Estimates. We will work through the rest of the week on this Budget and, as I said before, the work has already started on the May 2026-27 Budget, in which we will also deliver for Tasmania.

[3.20 p.m.]
Ms FINLAY - Chair, it is always interesting when the Premier presents himself at the lectern. He just apologised for being a little late and he looked a little flustered. Flustered is a high-energy presentation, but I remember the opening moment of Estimates just a short two weeks ago and he looked less than flustered and when asked the first question, there was this massive sigh, a rounding of the shoulders and it was like, 'I've got to get through this week'. It's a bit of an indication of the feeling of this government right now.

The intention and the purpose of Estimates scrutiny is for members of this place to be able to ask questions to seek clarification around the commitments they're making in the budget, to understand the shaping of the budget, what they're looking to deliver and what's in and what's out. Unfortunately, it has become the case, prior to a budget being prepared, when a budget is delivered and when you receive the papers, that this government has an art of not making things clear. This government has an art of trying to hide the full intentions of what they're doing, which doesn't always align with what they've told people they will do, so it often takes intense and repetitive questioning to seek answers and get honesty out of this government.

Some of my personal experiences through Estimates I want to share this afternoon in having to ask multiple questions over multiple rounds of questioning to actually get someone to tell the truth and answer a question. It shouldn't be that hard. It shouldn't be so hard for members of our community, when given commitments in writing, to trust that. It shouldn't be so hard that when members of our community hear our government make a commitment, they can believe it, but it is, and not only through the period of Estimates two weeks ago, through GBEs, through Question Time and through public presentation of this government's position, in everything this government does, they make it hard. 

The Premier just got to his feet and talked about this Budget and this current government creating certainty for the community. That's a laugh. He talked about responsibility. That's also a laugh. He talked about accountability and one of the things that was uncovered in this chapter of Estimates was the fact that there is almost zero accountability required by this Premier, the leader not only of Tasmania but the leader of the government, to seek and create accountability through his ministers. We have seen multiple examples. Perhaps it would be excusable if just once something didn't go quite right, but when there is a repetition of behaviour and it becomes a culture of this government to not feel like it is necessary to do the right thing, and for the Premier not to feel like it is his responsibility to hold his ministers to account, we know that there's a serious problem in this government. 

I want to reflect on two matters that became very evident throughout Estimates, one being something that we prosecuted for further clarification today in question time. It's the matter of the Premier holding his ministers to account through either the caretaker conventions when in an election, their ministerial codes of conduct or holding them to account for their responsibilities under acts and laws in Tasmania. 

In Estimates it was confirmed that Tasracing used taxpayer funds and resources to deliver messages from this government into the public realm to publicly promote positions of this government. We know that's not okay and we know that the Premier, who's not in the Chamber now, also knows it's not okay, because through that time in Estimates when his minister was being asked questions and through questions today, the Premier didn't have an answer. If the Premier actually believes what he said on his feet just a moment ago, that he believes in accountability, then he must immediately launch an investigation into what happened with the Tasracing and TasInsure communications through the election.

In my own electorate of Bass, a commitment was made by the Sports minister to the North Launceston Footy Club that they would have $100,000 to ensure their plans for the future if the Liberal government was re-elected, but now the government has decided that because the footy club spoke to somebody else, they're not going to give them that money anymore. The Budget should be delivered on the promises that were committed to. There should be funds. There should be $100,000 in the Budget for the North Launceston Footy Club to secure their future because they're being pushed away from the home they've had for over 100 years. But when you can't find things in the Budget because the Treasurer and his fellow ministers find ways to not make it easy to see and seek clarity within the Budget, then you have to ask questions. When the answers are, 'Actually, I wanted to give the North Launceston footy club $100,000 but when they stood with Labor I decided they didn't really want it anymore and we've withdrawn it from the Budget', that's not okay and the Premier must deal with that type of behaviour. Ministers are expected to deal fairly, without bias and without regard to irrelevant information, and that's not happening and that's on the Premier. 

In my own scrutiny at Estimates, there were multiple times in some of my shadow portfolio areas where it required diligent questioning to actually get someone to focus on what the ask was and then to give an honest response, none more important than Primary Industries. I think people would say the Primary Industries minister is doing a pretty good job. He's working hard and wants to deliver for farmers and fishers around our state, but the problem with nice people who try to do a good job is that when there are specifics and important information that needs to be truthful, that's when it really matters. That's actually what reveals the true core of a person. 

In our Primary Industries Estimates, there were lots of questions - not surprisingly, given the makeup of the minority parliament - around salmon, florfenicol and lots of different issues in the community, and these questions aren't always asked by everyone because of the particular subject matter, they're actually asked for integrity. I had to ask, through the minister, members of the department multiple times when they first knew that the industry intended to use florfenicol, and blunt faced to me, the person replying said, I' didn't find out till July'. We have correspondence between the government and industry in February indicating their intention to use florfenicol. 

This is not a salmon issue, not a recreational fishing issue, not a rock lobster issue, this is an honesty issue. If this government can't be honest about the fundamentals of the issues that are before them, how can the community trust anything? If this government knows there are things happening in our community that misrepresent critical information and they don't act, how can the community trust them? When members of this place ask questions through Estimates, it is the most fundamental expectation that they will act quickly, without too much fuss or bother, and give an honest answer. 

There were other questions in the primary industry space. NRE is a massive department. They have so many responsibilities and so many areas of work, but there are many people doing a lot of work with few resources. There are a number of teams in NRE right now that used to be resourced to eight or nine people that are working that workload with one or two people. When those questions were asked in Estimates in regard to looking to future budgets, yes, there was a recognition that many departments are under pressure, overwhelmed and there are challenges with the culture within that department because of the workload and the expectations, but they said, 'Oh no, that's okay, we're going to put more funding the budget in the future.'

Way to make your Budget look good, Treasurer, way to make the Tasmanian community feel like you know what you're doing as Treasurer when you say that there is a pathway to surplus when you actually withdraw funds in the future years to make it look good so you think you can get to a surplus. We know that when the real Budget comes out in May you will be required, and we will hold you to account, to make sure that you put credible figures in the Budget that show a true representation of the position right now and a true indication of how you intend to have money in the Budget. In May, you won't be able to say honestly that there is a pathway to surplus unless you do something drastic. The community are concerned that you will either do one of two things. You will either continue to misrepresent the position of the finances of Tasmania or you will take drastic action in an unexpected way that will harm individuals, harm workplaces and impact people's jobs.

The Premier thanked Treasury when he was on his feet before. We all acknowledge and thank Treasury, but if only the Premier and the ministers listened to Treasury because for years through the fiscal sustainability report there have been alarm bells ringing about the need for this government to take corrective action. The government continues to ignore the advice of Treasury, or, in fact, on some matters like TasInsure, don't even ask for the advice of Treasury, and we find ourselves in this situation right now.

We know there are organisations like the North Launceston Footy Club that have been written to by this government and have had things withdrawn, but it's not just sports clubs, it's not just organisations that dare to deal and talk to other people across the Chamber. There are really critical - in my Environment space, which crosses over with the Primary Industries in NRE Tasmania - critical organisations working on behalf of Tasmanians that have not had a fair representation or understanding of their commitments. This impacts employees, it impacts contract certainty for organisations - and that impacts their ability and their liveability in Tasmania, their commitments to mortgages, to families, to their kids. 

So when an organisation as neutral as NRMs in Tasmania are told a commitment that no funds to NRMs will be reduced, redirected, or impacted - in writing - before an election and before a budget is handed down, but then in Estimates scrutiny, when asked particular questions about the allocations to NRMs they were told there wouldn't be a reduction. Then I was told, 'Oh no, it's going back to a pre-bonus level of $250,000 a year.' The deeds for NRMs haven't been $250,000 a year since 2013. Since 2013, the base deeds for NRMs have gone from $250,000, $350,000, $467,000, $544,000. How can this government be honest about the commitments that they're making? What they're doing is seeking to misrepresent positions to organisations and then misrepresent the real financials in the Budget to make it look better than it actually is. 

For people like our three NRMs in Tasmania, it's not good enough that where their funding is coming to an end, they believe they've received commitments, but the government isn't clear in communicating with them about what they can do with their core funding. 

It's like in the area of Primary Industries, commitments that are made to great people in this place, where they make and prepare plans for future seasons around something that is core to increasing the farm gate value for farmers around irrigation. 

There are two matters that I prosecuted in Primary Industries scrutiny with the minister around two different, but equally important, matters with irrigation. One is the Tamar Scheme, where the minister, and the people accompanying him, tried to retell a story that is well understood. They weren't able to secure enough water allocation, water sales with farmers, so we needed to change an instrument in this place to get industrial water to be able to pull water through for farmers. We know that. What we know is, and what we need is, for the government to act, and act fast, to ensure that the farmers that are preparing can have access to that water. We want a commitment. 

The minister has said that he's going to take the lead between NRE and ReCFIT, and he is going to drive an outcome and deliver the water for Tamar farmers. They want to see that sooner rather than later, because otherwise they will start withdrawing the money that they have in that at the moment, with the allocations that they've indicated, and they will start making decisions to look after water on their own properties, which is good for some, but not for everybody. Minister, I know you've made a commitment to act, but you must. 

The other place with irrigation that you must act is in Winnaleah. Initially, early in Estimates, there was a suggestion that a meeting had been set up between yourself and the Winnaleah irrigators. I trust that that's going to happen. I trust that it's going to be a productive meeting and it will advance the needs of those farmers, because for two irrigating seasons now, they have been promised that they would have community management and that there would be a genuine desire by Tasmanian Irrigation to work with those farmers to find a solution for community management of their scheme. They've been waiting for two seasons. Before the next season comes in, by the end of this financial year, again, minister, I know that you have committed to bringing this in, and that you say that it is possible to have community management. It is viable for the Winnaleah farmers to have community management. That must happen as a matter of urgency, because they have been waiting for seasons.

Another area of my portfolio responsibility which is critical to the future of Tasmania - equal to fishers and farmers - is innovation and AI in our digital economy. This government talks a big game about innovation, about its commitment to the digital economy, but talking a big game isn't action on the ground.  One of the first sets of questions that I asked about in Estimates of this government was about the governance, about the support provided across departments and across the government for the use of AI. For people to use AI well supported, well understood, in an environment where there are clear guidelines and frameworks, it can be really useful for us in Tasmania. It can be something that - when people are at the centre of every idea and every thought - it can increase efficiency, it can increase productivity and it could, if done well, increase the experience of people living in Tasmania. If it's just used for quick, gimmick, get out of jail free cards - like we know that the Premier used to prepare his legislation for TasInsure - that undermines the credibility of what can be done for Tasmanians. 

I trust that this government is providing equal access, equal training and equal understanding of people in government on how they can use AI, how it can deliver for Tasmanians, and where it remains human-centred and it keeps the liveability, the lifestyles and the affordability of Tasmanians front and centre with the decisions they make about that.

The other thing that was important to prosecute in Estimates, and that is well on the record from TasICT, is around the digital roadmap for Tasmania. I want to put on record some comments from TasICT, because if the minister and the government are not aware of their concerns, I will now put it on the record so that they are. Our state - we have some incredible people. We have creative and innovative people who do things that matter in Tasmania, that make a difference in Tasmania. We have people here who are operating all around the world, who are leading the world in their innovation and doing amazing things. We need to make sure that there's a clear roadmap for investments and for opportunity in Tasmania. TasICT, and I'm quoting them now, says:

Tasmania's tech industry is calling for leadership from the Tasmanian government in AI adoption and tech innovation, because otherwise it risks a damaging decline in the state's workforce productivity.

In its annual survey, TasICT team members reported optimism about business conditions, but warned more needed to be done to encourage Tasmanians to be confident with technology and consider tech careers.

TasICT CEO, Russell Kelly, said that Tasmania's tech firms were more optimistic than similar surveys taken nationally - 34 per cent of respondents expect revenue growth to be above 10 per cent in 2025 and 45 per cent plan to hire staff steady with last year. 

However, consistent with similar national surveys, retraction in government spending on technology has a huge flow-on effect to tech firms. 

'Tech jobs are productive jobs - they can help businesses get a competitive edge and help government provide services more cheaply. 

'Our sector urges the state government to have a digital roadmap for improving digital services in Tasmania.'

Mr Kelly said that the survey of members taken in September 2025 showed support for the adoption of AI, cybersecurity and skills shortages in tech. They were the major issues facing the sector.

Tasmania's tech sector currently employs 11,200 people (3.2 per cent growth) and adds $1.1 billion to the state's economy, with Deloitte Access Economics predicting 21,000 additional jobs could be added by 2035 in its 'Digital Pulse' report prepared by the Australian Computer Society.

'Crucially, 81 per cent of the TasICT members (almost all of them) consider that the State Government is not doing enough to advance the digital economy - and it's not improving compared with previous years.'

Mr Kelly said that more than 86 per cent of TasICT members were using AI in some form in their businesses, with a further 7 per cent researching adoption.

Mr Kelly said the coming State Government Budget needs to supercharge spending on technology and provide a roadmap to make Tasmania a smart state. 

'Tasmania has great potential to pioneer science, creative industry and sustainable tech, but we are moving slowly at a time when the arrival of AI is changing the world at break-neck speed.' [tbc]

The risk for Tasmania is, if we wait too long, if we are too slow at creating these opportunities, we're going to miss the boat and we're going to be left behind. Tasmania could be, and should be, at the forefront of these areas, and more needs to be done by this government to deliver that.

In the area of energy renewables, it would be expected that we would prosecute issues around Marinus, around North West Transmission, around our renewable investments, around Robbins Island and Whaleback Ridge and others across Tasmania. One of the areas that I wanted to really focus on, though, was around energy poverty - because it's real for Tasmanians. Cost-of-living pressures are real for Tasmanians. Tasmanian families are choosing to turn their power off in winter, to sit under rugs or go to sleep before the sun goes down because they fear that they can't afford, or they can't afford, the power that's required to look after them through winter. Tasmanian families are hurting.

We prosecuted questions around the number of Tasmanians who were having their power disconnected because they couldn't keep up with the increases in their bills. Tasmanians are having their power disconnected and it's taking a long time and a lot of costs to have it reconnected when they're already under financial pressure. I acknowledge there are a lot of good programs out there being delivered by the government. They are being creative about how they can wrap their supports around Tasmanians, but more needs to be done. 

Off the back of the position of Hydro having the two toughest years in terms of dry conditions, there won't be a renewable energy dividend for Tasmanians this year and that was something that came in with great fanfare from this government. When asked to provide the modelling from Hydro about future years and the climatic conditions to see how that would impact profits and therefore impact renewal energy dividends for Tasmanians, there were no answers. Hydro is a modelling entity, it's a climate weather entity that relies on water. They must know, the government and the minister must know what the future years will look like and what the impact will continue to have on Tasmanians. We need to do more to support Tasmanians who are in real energy poverty. They are the families at the centre that need our full attention and need to understand that the government continues to care with greater regard.

We talk about budget mismanagement and areas where there needs to be savings, but so often with this government it's poor decision-making or pauses on decision-making or errors in decision-making, which is costing Tasmanians greatly. Off the back of the Marinus decision with full fanfare, you would expect that the works undertaken at Tarraleah and the projects being proposed at Cethana with pumped hydro would be full gas going forward, but there was a delay. I can understand that there would be a delay with regard to reconsidering a position, but lengthy delays happened in decision-making and the Tarraleah project was put on pause for a period of time, which cost millions of dollars in terms of the consideration and tens of millions of dollars for the outcomes of the capital costs for Tarraleah. They're the sorts of places where Tasmanians are feeling the pressure of additional costs in the Budget. 

The Energy minister was responsible for delivering a power deal for Bell Bay Aluminium, a 10-year power deal they need. They admitted on their own records that they had been working on this for six years, had been in deep negotiations with Bell Bay Aluminium and couldn't get it across the line. We know there will be work done in a committee with the feds around creating an outcome for Bell Bay Aluminium. They've given themselves a 12-month extension and there were questions asked in Estimates about their confidence to deliver an outcome in the next 12 months. When you reverse-engineer the needs for Bell Bay Aluminium and the workers on that site to have certainty for their future and the productions on that site, there needs to be well in advance of that 12 months a commitment for this government to be able to deliver the power deal and the future for Bell Bay Aluminium.

It's like the uncertainty at Liberty Bell Bay which requires a team effort. They are two completely different but critical issues in the electorate of Bass, critical issues for the workers in that local area and, most importantly, critical issues for many hundreds of small businesses across Tasmania that rely on both of those sites and many of our major industrials in Tasmania.

NRET for me covers Primary Industries and Water, Environment and Parks, and there are massive pressures in that department. I raised concerns but didn't get clear answers about the investment in supporting the people. There have been surveys, the outcomes of which have been tabled, of the culture of that department. I hear many times brought to me concerns from individuals who work in the area and from the unions that support the people who work in the area, and in my conversations with fishers and farmers I hear of the concerns of the pressure and the resourcing in NRET. If we want to look after our environment, the quality of our water, our fishers and farmers and our world-renowned parks, we've got to make sure that the people doing the work are looked after. 

One area of concern for me in Parks that was prosecuted in Estimates was the Pioneer Dam. There is a beautiful community of people who love to do the most classic of Tasmanian activities and camp in pretty fundamental capacity at Pioneer Dam. There was a concern with the dam wall and there is an outcome of some engineering reports. Minister Duigan, although not clear, did say he was looking to facilitate the return of those activities to Pioneer Dam. I trust that the minister will maintain his word and that, although complex, he will support the people who love camping at Pioneer Dam, and after the wall is removed and the waters are released above the wall, there is a return to that activity at Pioneer Dam.

Similarly, another great activity that all Tasmanians love is mountain biking. At Kate Reed Reserve, close to Launceston, there is an opportunity to increase infrastructure to provide a trailhead for the mountain bike trails in that area and in doing so, provide roads and open up thousands of housing opportunities across the South Prospect proposal. We asked questions in Estimates of the department on how advanced the RAA was for Kate Reed Reserve and what impediments there were, or what needs to be managed in order to be able to deliver on that. This is a critical piece of infrastructure that is at the centre of lots of challenges and this is a bit of an illustration of the challenges for this government.

In one small area there is a major highway that bottlenecks at the Bunnings turnoff and is completely unsafe in terms of how people exit off the highway into the Bunnings area. Then you've got families under that roundabout that are trying to get their kids out and be healthy and get them into the Kate Reed Reserve and they have to park on the side of the highway on an underpass and get their kids up and over a fence into the reserve. It's extraordinary what is required for families to recreate in that area.

There is a proposal here that can open up solutions for safe access to the mountain bike trails, and with some lateral thinking this government could look to do more work to improve the safety of that highway coming out of Launceston going south, when it turns off to Bunnings in the Youngtown area. It could also unlock thousands of homes for the South Prospect project and lots of community facilities and activities. In the Parks portfolio, a priority of focus has been on the proposal at Kate Reed Reserve. There have been commitments of consideration and a commitment to deliver on those outcomes would be welcomed by the community.

This Budget, again like what's happened in previous years, is an attempt by this government to run a smoke and mirrors campaign, an attempt by this government to say there's a pathway to surplus when we know there's not, an attempt by this government to hide things where they'd made commitments and then don't deliver on them. For something as simple as Flinders Island and the $320,000 that was promised to support them for the lights for their runway, they have correspondence, they were told they were going to get it, in the election it was announced and then it's not in the Budget.

I love the language in the letters this government use when they write to people they've made commitments to. They say, 'Sorry, it's an interim budget', although that's an excuse, 'but we may be able to find savings, or we may be able to find allocations in the next budget'. Who's going to believe that? Who's going to believe what the government says anymore, when you write to correspondents like that? 

Tasmanians deserve better. Tasmanians deserve to be able to trust their government. They expect a government that has integrity. They expect a government that is honest. They expect a government that actually stands up and delivers and takes action on the things that they say are important, not a government that just talks, puts things in writing and then says, 'I didn't really mean it, it doesn't really matter', and has a premier who, when his ministers do things that are abhorrently disrespectful and against their own codes of conduct and caretaker convention, doesn't stand up and do anything about it.

Between this interim Budget and the budget next year, this government and the Premier are on notice. Maintain the standards of your ministers. Don't raise expectations in the community in a way that you know that you never intend to deliver. Premier, when your ministers are doing things that are not okay, you need to take action.

When I asked why it is that salmon companies are dumping florfenicol instead of destocking their diseased fish and fallowing their pens - which is the way farmers deal with disease - I had no answer to that. Mr BARNETT - Chair, I'm very pleased to stand speak to the budget Estimates committee hearings. I thank the Chairs and all those who participated in budget Estimates committee hearings. Certainly, what has been confirmed is that Tasmania has come a long way since 2014. Tasmanians are now backing themselves. What we do know that's come out of budget Estimates is that we have a strong economy and that continues to grow and, in fact, last month the latest information showed that Tasmania's economy recorded the highest per capita growth of any state in November 2025 and our economy is now worth $42.8 billion.

That is so important because you cannot reinvest in health, education, police, public safety without a growing and strong economy, and that's something I've certainly learned over the decades, including under the John Howard coalition government at the federal level, but it's a principle that I know is true and what we also know is that business confidence is up with the NAB survey again last month, being the top of the tree in Australia for Tasmania and we also know that business conditions, come in at second across all Australia in terms of the regulation rumble from the Business Council of Australia - coming second in Australia. That's a good sign for business. It shows there's confidence and the conditions are good, but we can always do more. 

What we've seen is the 51,000 extra jobs, the lowest or amongst the lowest unemployment rates in our history in Tasmania and what we saw in terms of confidence on the weekend were the 15,000 reasons why we're confident in Tasmania and they have expressed that confidence in the progress to seek support for the Macquarie Point multipurpose stadium as an intergenerational asset. See, this is an investment not just for the now, but for tomorrow and for the generations to come. Tasmania is not shying away from the big ideas and this government is not. What it's about, I believe, it will be a catalyst for renewal. It will be a catalyst for major events, for jobs, development, growth and opportunity, for aspiration, for our young kids and their future and, as the Premier has said, I've said many times, we want Tasmania to be the best place in the world to work, to live, to raise a family, to invest and to do business. That's part of our plan and we want to do that in Tasmania.

I was very pleased to be able to back in our small businesses as the Minister for Small Business. It is very encouraging, with 42,000 small businesses now, employing more than 100,000 Tasmanians, the backbone of our economy, particularly in those rural and regional communities, all across Tasmania. We're pleased and proud of them and, as a government, we're getting behind them with the 24/7 online business continuity planning tool and through the Business Tasmania website and the online service that's made available. Very pleased and proud of those contributions of our wonderful public servants in that space.

In terms of export success and global reputation, we certainly have what the rest of the world wants and in terms of our exports, it was made very clear that they are now record levels or near record levels of $6.16 billion in the last financial year 2023-24 and that's the second highest result ever recorded in our state and, in fact, it was a 5.2 per cent increase, which is a significant increase bearing in mind across the nation there was a slight decrease.

I want to pay a tribute to two national champions and that's the International Health Exporter of the Year, to Marinova, and they're based out of Cambridge, I have been there some time ago. Excellent organisation and I can see some nodding around the chamber. It's certainly a world leader in high purity fucoidan, which is a naturally occurring compound derived from seaweed and its products are used globally in pharmaceuticals, nutraceuticals and advanced health technologies, and the award recognises not only commercial success but scientific excellence, innovation and a commitment to sustainable marine biotechnology. Marinova's growth shows that Tasmania can lead the world in specialist high-value industries. Congratulations. Well done at the national awards just last week in Canberra. That's a great credit to you.

Likewise, Small Business, that's such a joy to be Minister for Small Business and Trade and to know that the Small Business Exporter of the Year was Three Warriors. This again was a great credit to Three Warriors. It's been founded in Hobart. Three Warriors produces certified organic skin care and tanning products using Tasmanian ingredients. The company is expanding rapidly into international markets and changing perceptions about what Tasmanian businesses can achieve.

Their wins send a powerful message. Small business in Tasmania can scale globally and win. Yes, they've won in Tasmania, they've won nationally. It is a story of success of that small business; likewise, being the Small Business Exporter of the Year, it's a great credit to them.

In terms of our justice system, again, as an Attorney-General; Minister for Justice, Corrections and Rehabilitation; Tasmanians absolutely deserve a system that's fair, that's modern, that's responsive and accessible. There've been significant improvements that have been delivered under our government and I've often said and will say again right here and now that the TASCAT establishment, the Tasmanian Administrative Appeals Tribunal, has delivered one of the best justice reforms in the last decade or more. It gives you easier, faster and cheaper access to justice. The new Launceston TASCAT registry has opened. I was there just a few months ago with the relevant legal and justice stakeholders in Launceston. It was great to be with them and they're all delighted with the new registry in Launceston - multimillion dollar commitment and we're very pleased to allow access to TASCAT in northern Tasmania in that regard.

Talking about regional areas, the north-west coast has been the beneficiary of the new Burnie Courts redevelopment progress and that's very encouraging. You can see that the complex is going replace outdated facilities, it's going to improve service delivery in the north-west coast and for north-west coasters, this is delivering better access to justice. Both the Supreme Court and Magistrates Court will be there. There will be a justice precinct, so we're getting on with the job.

The legislative reform agenda has been very expansive and extensive, purposeful, with more than 22 major bills progressing in just one year alone and it's reflecting on the agenda of our justice team in our government. We're strengthening accountability, improving protections for victims, supporting vulnerable Tasmanians and certainly modernising a whole range of contemporary expectations. Family and sexual violence reforms, for example, released the discussion paper just recently. Very comprehensive, and we're inviting public feedback on that and it certainly complements the $100 million Family and Sexual Violence Action Plan and the targeted actions under that. I credit Will Hodgman, the former premier, for his leadership in 2015 for leading the nation in terms of some of those reforms.

In terms of legal aid, I am pleased and proud that this interim Budget has delivered an additional $4 million to meet rising demand and to reduce backlogs so the advocacy continues to secure appropriate Commonwealth funding for family law matters, which is absolutely a matter for the Commonwealth government.

In terms of rehabilitation and community safety, these are top priorities for our government, so our efforts continue in terms of expanding plans for electronic monitoring, home detention options and to increase community safety and provide smart sentencing pathways. Alcohol treatment orders through the court-mandated diversion programs are also very important and making a difference. We have the reforms from the commission of inquiry. They are being delivered. We've now got 54 recommendations that require legislative action and significant drafting and policy work is well under way. I thank my department and those across the government for that support. 

The new Commission for Children and Young People bill has been tabled here and it's currently upstairs. Delivering on that commission of inquiry report and recommendation is a key structural reform, and that's being delivered under our government. We are strengthening our victim-survivor rights and supports and will continue to do so. 

In the short moments I've got to conclude, I want to say we're continuing our efforts to cut the red tape, streamlining the processes to allow business to get on with doing what they do best, and that's with the national construction code freeze. We've made that decision, we've put a freeze on that, we've led the nation. The implementation of the National Construction Code 2025 changes will provide certainty for builders, certainty for tradies, certainty for small businesses, and make it easier and more streamlined. 

I say thank you to the Housing Industry Association, Ben Price; to the Master Builders Tasmania, Jenna Carney; and the Property Council of Tasmania for his strong support for this government initiative and support of these initiatives more generally.

I am aware of the Victorian Labor government's ban on Type 2 fireworks and the impact of the importation of fireworks into Tasmania and that particular ban through the Port of Melbourne. I want to make it clear that it is a great shame. We're standing up for Tasmania and the Tasmanian way of life to be able to enjoy cracker night, and it is part of the Tasmanian way of life. This blockage through the Port of Melbourne as a result of the Victorian Labor government is very disappointing. Both the former minister, Felix Ellis, and I have written to the relevant minister in Victoria. I'm asking the Victorian government to reconsider. We will continue to stand up for the Tasmanian way of life under our government and we will do everything we can to save cracker night in Tasmania. 

In conclusion, this is a good budget. It delivers for Tasmania and we are backing it in. I thank the House.

This is a deeply concerning and worrying budget for Tasmania for very good reason. From having no net debt in 2014 when they came into office, that's in the general government sector, more than $200 million-worth of net cash and investments in the bank to now heading towards more than $10 billion worth of net debt if you take this Budget at face value, or $13 billion if you take the PEFO report that came out from Treasury earlier this year. 

The Budget is unsustainable. The very definition is it is unsustainable because we are spending far more than what we are bringing in in revenue. The sort of rhetoric that we hear from over on the other side is simply not going to cut it. Simply talking about growing our economy and that's how we will fix it is wrong. We've heard from Treasury that that can't happen. The number I really want to focus on today is on page 103 of the budget forward Estimates as discussed during the week regarding the cash deficit position. There's an operating surplus - that's the headline figure the government will use and talk about getting to in the 2029 financial year. The issue is that, even once they get to an operating surplus - if they can actually achieve that and I have very serious doubts that they will - the cash deficit position in that year is still $423.6 million in cash deficit, which means that the debt keeps rising. For the 2024-25 budget, the figure was $1.5 billion in cash deficit. For this financial year that we are talking about, it's $1.3 billion. The following year will be $1.3 billion before it falls to a little over $600 million in 2027-28 and then 2028-29. The reason that the debt is growing, the reason that credit ratings agencies like S&P and Moody's are making the predictions they're making and downgrading our credit position is that the debt keeps rising. 

We asked about this position during budget Estimates ahead of Moody's and S&P making their determination and effectively giving a score to this government on their fiscal management. Moody's went first. They said:

The downgrade reflects a sharp and sustained increase in Tasmania's debt interest burdens, which has weakened the state's intrinsic financial strength. We expect these fiscal pressures to persist over the next few years. Net direct and indirect debt (NDID) has doubled from about 66 per cent of operating revenue in June 2019 to more than 130 per cent at June 2025 and absent countermeasures could surpass 180 per cent by fiscal 2029.

S&P also lowered the credit rating following this budget, which the former treasurer called a good budget. They looked at it and, I have to say, didn't agree. In their response they said:

Tasmania's budgetary metrics are very weak. We project cash operating deficits averaging around 3 per cent between fiscal 2025 and 2027, with a return to operating surplus possible in fiscal 2028. Tasmania's return to surplus relies heavily on tight cost control to contain operating expense growth to just 1.1 per cent per year between fiscal 2026 and 2028.

Deputy Chair, the problem with that is that it has very seldom, rarely happened in Tasmanian budgets. In fact, on two of the out years, the government is projecting that they will spend less than the year before. They are going from budgets where only a few years ago we had expenditure growth of 14 per cent under this Liberal government, under the former treasurer, Mr Ferguson, to now getting towards having negative expenditure growth and/or very, very small expenditure growth. This is going to be very difficult to do within the policy settings that they have, which is why people are so worried about next year's budget. 

People in the community sector are deeply, deeply concerned about the level of services that they can provide with their existing funding, but also what they are going to be left with at next year's budget. These are organisations that were promised five-year funding agreements back in 2014 and still don't have them. They are still living year-to-year, month-to-month on promises made or on writing grant deeds year to year to try and retain their staff, attract new staff and deliver the services that Tasmanians need. In this sort of context with an expenditure target that's been set very, very low, with 2800 public sector workers that are set to go; they are deeply concerned, and I don't blame them, there is going to be serious pain in the next budget.

This is predicated on reducing expenditure and on massive cash deficits, which are going to continue to increase the debt, and it didn't have to be this way. This is a policy choice. Every single one of these decisions has been made by the Liberal government. What we are left with is a government that's been in power for 11 years and has no one else to blame but themselves, which is why this is going to be so difficult for them to try and deal with. A serious problem that has been allowed to fester since the 2021 Fiscal Sustainability Report that I like to reference every time I speak on the budget, because that was the report that gave the warning and said the size of the corrective action gets harder the longer you wait to take decisions. In that report, it talked about in the short to medium term, things were manageable, but unless something is corrected, things are going to get much, much harder.
