When you hear Premier Jeremy Rockliff or when you hear Minister Jane Howlett say they are totally committed to the other two codes, you cannot believe them, because that's what they told greyhounds only three months ago. You can't believe a single word they say. You can believe the Greens on this, because they've been consistent. Yes, they've focused very heavily on greyhounds, but now they're turning their sights to thoroughbred and harness racing. The question is, when they tell the Premier that they want those two codes gone, what will he say this time? Last time they said they wanted harness and thoroughbred racing gone, he said yes. He rolled over and did over all those people that have backed him, his government, for years - his minister included.

With that level of uncertainty that's pervaded through the Budget, through Estimates, with a government that can't be trusted, with a government that is prepared to do over community organisations like the North Launceston Football Club, like the Launceston, Devonport and Hobart greyhound racing clubs, it's hard to have confidence in this government that it is going to do the right thing by Tasmanians.

[5.03 p.m.]
Mr BAYLEY (Clark) - Chair, I rise to give my reply to the Estimates process. Upfront, I want to acknowledge the process and thank everyone involved in it - parliamentary staff who moved heaven on earth to make it happen - not least with GBEs following - the services staff as well. I want to acknowledge our team, who work tirelessly to assist us with the questions. Also, I want to acknowledge the chairs of the committees. Honourable Chair, you did a great job, as did Mr Fairs. I thought you were fair and even-handed. 

This Budget is deeply concerning, and corrective action is needed. During the election, we Greens showed that you can deliver that kind of corrective action. We showed that there was a billion dollars worth of savings that could be made in the Budget. We showed that there was $900 million worth of revenue that could be earned by making big corporations pay their fair share, and when we included additional expenditure, we showed that the Budget could be $1.4 billion dollars better off overall. 

It is possible, and it has been really made clear by people, including Dr Saul Eslake, that the state of the Budget is purely down to decisions - policy decisions of this government. It's not down to COI, it's not down to COVID, it's down to policy decisions.

Saul Eslake said: 

Drawing on publicly available information, the Review finds that the deterioration in the financial position of Tasmania’s 'general government sector' - that is, state government departments and agencies funded primarily by taxation revenue or grants from the Federal Government - over the past decade is entirely attributable to 'policy decisions' by government (to increase 'operating' or recurrent expenses, and spending on infrastructure projects, and to a lesser extent to reduce taxes).

We've seen this government engage in pork barrelling, refusing to raise revenue and to set infrastructure budgets that are exorbitant - synonymously characterised, obviously, by the stadium.

The Pre-Election Financial Outlook (PEFO) showed in its key findings that the Budget has a structural problem, and that despite the fact that the government talking about rivers of gold coming, the Treasury, unfettered by the hand of the Treasurer in the PEFO during caretaker period, said that:

Economic growth correlates weakly with general government sector revenue growth in Tasmania. Accordingly, this structural problem will not be resolved through future economic growth.

Explicit policy choices are required. We need those policy choices - we need them desperately, because with our credit rating now being downgraded, the state of the Budget is going to get even worse. Debt is going to get more expensive, and it will come at a cost to our services.

The first thing this government should do is drop the Macquarie Point stadium, because that is unnecessary debt for a third AFL stadium that will get ever more expensive. Instead, it's focused on cuts. It stood up the efficiency and productivity unit that's looking across departments for places to find savings - 2800 public servants over the coming years. In the Budget consultation papers, it's writing to community service organisations asking them for suggestions as to what they can cut. These are people who are providing critical services to our community, and they are being asked to find the cuts to their own services. It's insulting. If you turn on the television or read the newspaper in any one week, you hear story after story of people doing it hard. Whether it's the Chigwell community garden that has to close down, the Hobart City Mission that can't meet the needs of its community, or things like jacking up TAFE fees for really important courses, you can see that there are cuts and hikes going on everywhere. 

Of course, the stadium sits central to a lot of this - $1.8 billion over the next 10 years, a contaminated site, so it's high-risk. A high risk of delay; delays equal cost overruns. There are high-impact effects - and I will talk about some of this later - the Cenotaph, the heritage precinct of Hobart, and the Tasmanian Symphony Orchestra. There are significant issues.

It was good to have the opportunity to directly put to the Treasurer, in an Estimates-type process, questions about some of these things. Questions about debt and servicing debt, $10.4 billion by the end of the forward Estimates - that's almost double, and that doesn't include the debt being carried by government businesses and non-financial government corporations.

This Budget is a matter of choices. The government is actively choosing to prioritise things like the stadium, and things like not raising revenue by making corporations pay their fair share. They're making us, the community, pay as a result, and it's baking in an absolute mirage to this so-called interim Budget. The notion of a surplus, albeit a modest surplus of $5 million, at the end of the forward Estimates is an absolute mirage. We know that the forward Estimates for many critical services are blanked out, and they are things that will need to be funded. Things like 26Ten, which I raised during the committee process with minister Jo Palmer, and the school lunch program. These are things that the government is working on strategies to deliver, yet hasn't put in funding beyond 2026, because that's when the current funding ends. We know that they're going to need to do these things. We hope they're going to give funding to these things. If they're not, it's an even bigger indictment on this government. 

The point I want to make is that the notion of the surplus is an absolute mirage. This Budget has been reverse-engineered to find a surplus in 2028-29 and work it backwards from there. I mentioned the public service reductions - we know that the budget coming is going to be way worse than this one itself, and 2800 public service jobs are in the crosshairs of this government.

When it comes to the stadium, it was great to talk to the Treasurer about implications of the stadium. It's had four cost blowouts. It seems there is no upper limit to the price tag the government is willing to spend on this stadium. The commitment of '$375 million and not one red cent more' by the Premier that was clearly announced day one of election 2024 was clearly all about neutralising the issue during the 2024 election. The Premier was completely undermined by his Treasurer, who completely contradicted him. The Premier has often said that borrowings are not capital, and when he said that capital was $375 million and not one red cent more, the Treasurer, Mr Abetz, said, 'Borrowings are clearly going to be part of the capital input'.

Unfortunately for the Premier, the charade of 'not one red cent more than $375 million' has been blown out of the water by his own Treasurer.

The Treasurer did not get advice from Treasury about scenario mapping about how much this will cost if it blows out by a certain amount. He has no modelling or evidence around the rivers of gold. He talks about payroll tax; he talks about land tax. However, in listening to the Treasurer and the Premier you would think this stadium is going to deliver some incredible bonanza. Don't worry about the experts who say that's not going to happen. Don't worry about the fact that the Planning Commission and Martin Wallace the other day said that that's not going to happen, that other experts presented to the upper House said that all the evidence looking at stadia around the world shows that that river of gold, that bonanza will not come. Put that aside. If you ask the Treasurer about what he bases his claims on, has he got modelling from Treasury, does he have evidence to substantiate that? It is clearly 'no'.

Servicing the debt: the Macquarie Point Development Corporation is going to borrow $490 million. The government itself is going to borrow $375 million simply to service the Macquarie Point debt of $490 million - this is pre-credit rating downgrade - which is going to cost up to $32 million a year. It's an incredible amount of money and, as Martin Wallace said the other day, it is compounding and it will cost more and more every year because we are going to be borrowing money to pay the interest on borrowed money. This is ludicrous and, as Martin Wallace said, an untenable situation.

Of course, the GBE debt is unsustainable as well. I will talk a little bit about Homes Tasmania later, but it is really clear that this government is effectively using the GBE sector - public, non-financial corporations - as vehicles to carry debt so it doesn't sit on the general government sector budget.

In the Treasury portfolio, it was good to ask questions about the mandatory precommitment card. The Finance portfolio has responsibility for that and we again pushed the government on the release of reports, the Deloitte's report, and other evidence around their position and, of course, to push them on leading the nation. Don't follow the nation when it comes to this because this is something that we can do. We know the damage that poker machines do in our communities. Millions and millions of dollars - $17 million stripped out of our communities every single year. This is the evidence. The mandatory precommitment card was the evidence and was the recommendation of the Liquor and Gaming Commission.

The community support levy which is drawn from poker machine revenues is supposed to be handed out to community support organisations to help people with problem gambling. It's a levy of $7.5 million. There's a balance of $2.4 million being held at the moment. Why is it that the government is withholding money that it is collecting from gambling profits to assist with problem gambling? It should be divvied out. It's ridiculous to think that that's not the case.

TasInsure was an interesting conversation to participate in. Despite the fact that Tasmania tops the list when it comes to the National Climate Risk Assessment, this government seems to think that TasInsure is a good idea because it means that Tasmanians won't have to cover for the exorbitant cost of insuring and paying out on claims from bushfires, cyclones and other natural disasters and floods in northern Australia and on the mainland, but, of course, the government has absolutely no modelling to back this up.

I remember sitting in this place once over the last couple of years and the Premier looking across at Mr Winter when he was opposition leader and levelling the accusation at him, 'Mr Winter, you're all shopfront and no shelves,' meaning you have no substance and you're all façade. If there was ever a policy position that is all shopfront and no shelves, it's this when it comes to TasInsure.

The Tasmanian Audit Office came for half an hour and to the Treasurer's credit - I want to pay credit where credit's due - he effectively stepped back from that conversation and allowed questions to be asked directly of the Audit Office and for them to be answered. That was very welcome. The Auditor-General was really clear on TT-Line when he said, 'I haven't been presented with anything that would change my opinion at this point of time'. Meaning, that, in his mind, TT-Line is still insolvent. GBE debt, it was a concern in the PEFO and TT-Line is clearly a concern front of mind when it comes to the Audit Office, but there are concerns being raised about others. Of course, we asked questions about the Wilkinsons Point land sale and the fact that the Audit Office couldn't get access to Cabinet documents that would have helped their analysis.

When it came to the stadium and Macquarie Point urban renewal, with the Minister for Macquarie Point Urban Renewal, it was, again, an exercise in uncovering the ludicrous house of cards upon which the Macquarie Point Stadium is to be built. We have, on one hand, the Tasmanian Planning Commission's expert evidence saying don't build it, the stadium should not proceed and, on the other hand, we have a really flimsy - basically a PR pamphlet from the government that reruns all the same arguments that have been run and lost and puts up a few comments from punters on the street or vested interests. 

Of course, when it comes to urban renewal, the Tasmanian Planning Commission is utterly clear that putting the stadium in that spot is not going to leave enough room to activate the site. I listened to the Tasmanian Planning Commission before the Legislative Council committee the other day. It was fantastic to see Shelley Penn AM, the architect who is on that panel, unpack exactly why it is the case that there is too little room and not enough access. It is going to be unsafe for people to be walking around there. It's not going to be a commercial venture.

It is Mac Point Urban Renewal in name only when it comes to this portfolio. But when it comes to the $490.7 million that the Mac Point Development Corporation is going to borrow, the state government is clearly going to guarantee all those borrowings. When the Treasurer accepted the $375 million that the government will be borrowing is added to the $490.7 million, the government will be borrowing at least $865.7 million to build the Macquarie Point Stadium and, as mentioned, simply servicing the Mac Point debt - the $490 million-worth of debt - is going to cost $32 million from the general government sector. This is the same sector where we pay nurses and teachers, and give out money to community service organisations.

When it comes comes to private sector investment, the minister said they have not taken that off the table. The correct terminology might be 'deferred it,' but clearly there's been a lack of interest there. When it comes to paying down capital borrowings, the minister points to the commercial returns that will be generated from site redevelopment. However, if you do listen to the experts, like the Tasmanian Planning Commission, the returns are going to be pretty marginal there because urban renewal is going to be very difficult to deliver.

As for the NRE land that's to be transferred to the Macquarie Point Development Corporation for housing, which is a requirement of the $240 million being given by the federal government, is yet to be transferred. Moreover, in the Tasmanian Planning Commission's evidence, they basically said that there couldn't be a worse place for housing development. It is isolated from the city, right next to an industrial site - the working port - and full of hazards for residents. It's the worst possible place to put a housing development.

We asked some questions about the Aboriginal culturally informed zone. While the previous master plan for Mac Point had truth and reconciliation front and centre, the Aboriginal culturally informed zone is a real titbit that's left on the outside of the stadium that Prof Greg Lehman, has described as, and I quote, '... gestures amount to what I would characterise as a typical example of what is commonly called "Black-cladding"'. [OK]

Under questioning we found out that the Aboriginal culturally informed zone is not funded as part of the $1.13 billion of the stadium expenditure. It will be developed in the first instance, I imagine, as simple pavement. Then, if this thing is ever built, additional money will have to be spent over time to deliver that zone, if at all.

When it comes to the Sport portfolio with minister Duigan, it was really clear that the government has not yet considered what's going to happen to the debt that's associated with the stadium when it comes to transferring it from Macquarie Point Development Corporation to Stadiums Tasmania.

The question was, when you transfer the stadium from one entity to another, does the debt go with it? That's the normal thing that would happen with debt. However, as minister Duigan said, this is not a scenario that has been contemplated. We found out during questioning that sports betting, that advertising for gambling inside the stadium, that there is no current policy position to preclude that kind of betting, and that 90 per cent of the advertising inside the stadium actually comes with the tenants. If you get the AFL and their relationship with sports betting companies, you get their baggage as well. You get their gambling advertising and there's no capacity to preclude gambling advertising in the stadium and it seems there's no intent to do so. 

I was shocked to hear the response from the minister, Mr Duigan, under questioning from Mr Winter in relation to the North Launceston Football Club and that funding. I find that outcome really disrespectful and indecent as a response. It's great to see our member for Bass, Ms Rosol, put on the record today that there should be an apology, there should be a demonstration of respect and decency, and that money should be honoured.

Chair, when it comes to the environment, it's obviously critical to our brand and our economy. We pride ourselves on being clean and green. Of course, the State of the Environment Report was an absolute wakeup call for Tasmania and Tasmanians, but it seems not for this government. Of 29 indicators, 55 per cent - more than half of them - were deteriorating, 21 per cent were stable, 7 per cent are improving, and 17 per cent are unknown. It's a shocking indictment on a decade of Liberal policy and funding priorities. The report made several recommendations, including:

develop a long-term vision and strategy for Tasmania's environment
explore opportunities to collaborate with the Aboriginal community
establish more marine protected areas
remove protections for wild fallow deer from regulatory frameworks and invest in and expand the terrestrial reserve estate.

When you look at the Budget, there are no dollars in it to implement the State of the Environment Report recommendations. There's nothing explicit there. We know from the government's response to the State of the Environment Report that few have been picked up and many have, or some have been, completely rejected. Many others have been acknowledged in part and they will be delivered in part. It's simply not good enough. 

If we are going to maintain that clean, green brand, we need to be funding elements and we need to be funding the protection of our environment and indeed the restoration of our environment. The EPA was in the room when it came to the environment portfolio and Dr Woodruff extensively questioned them about florfenicol and antibiotic use in salmon farms and found a shocking figure: 700 kilograms of florfenicol had been released into the D'Entrecasteaux Channel in less than two weeks. Absolutely shocking. Of course, there are many concerns when it comes to the EPA needing new teeth, needing new laws to be able to implement and to regulate. 

One of the most shocking revelations under questioning of the EPA was its intervention, its appeal in a legal case to try to block Mine Watch Meander Valley, which has been joined to a legal case by TASCAT. EPA is using public money to appeal to the Federal Court to try to block that community group from that appeal. It's a shocking indictment. The motivation for this, according to the EPA, is all about law and in regard to appropriate processes. For some reason the EPA sees it appropriate to intervene to try to block a community group from participating and exercising its legal rights to challenge a mine in its local area. It confirmed that there's no upper limit on what's being spent.

Threatened species protection was a feature of the State of the Environment Report. We're seeing precious little of it when it comes to the swift parrot and when it comes to the loss of potential breeding and foraging habitat, the government's own recovery plan for the swift parrot  shows that loss of potential breeding habitat in Tasmania via clearance for conversion to agriculture, native forest logging and intensive native forests silviculture, continues to reduce the amount of available swift parrot nesting and foraging habitat and therefore remains a significant threat to the continued persistence of the species.

We had no answer from the minister when pushed on increasing habitat protection for the swift parrot. We had no answer from the minister when it came to logging at Lonnavale and the declaration of a swift parrot important breeding habitat over that area. The only thing the government can point to when it comes to the protection of habitat for species is private land conservation and, even in that regard, it's failed to meet its targets. 

It was welcome to have a conversation with the EPA about the Lake Rosebery oil spill because that was a response that appeared to be very well managed. We asked questions about legacy site auditing, about how many of these legacy industrial sites are out there in the landscape and what they are doing to understand it and address it. It does seem that there are steps being taken, which is really welcome. 

It was refreshing to have a conversation with minister Vincent in the chair when it came to housing. I find his answers real and really honest, despite the fact that he has some significant challenges, not least in the Housing portfolio. The Homes Tasmania review identified a whole range of different issues and implementation needs to be delivered, but it seems that next year's budget is where most of those outcomes are going to be found.

Homes Tasmania debt is now shocking. It's $500 million now and trending towards a billion dollars at the end of the forward Estimates. It's going to cost us $25 million now to service this debt. It'll cost us $50 million at the end of the forward Estimates. Meanwhile, the priority wait list is 5336 people, which is over 8180 people. There were questions asked, I acknowledge not by myself, this is a question I've thought about a lot, about how many actual people sit behind those 5336 applications and it is at least 8000 people based on the numbers that were provided.

It's really clear that short stay accommodation needs to be reined in. Treasury has estimates as to what could be generated through the short stay levy. It's welcome to see a commitment for that to be implemented next year, but it means that we've done ourselves out of $22 million in the two years that we've now been delayed.

What was very welcome in the conversation with Homes Tasmania was the acknowledgement that the private rental market is broken. The CEO of Homes Tasmania made it really clear and I quote, when we're talking about private rental issues - 'those are important things for all governments to think about, but private rental in Australia is broken'. I concur with that. We need rent control, we need no cause evictions, we need minimum standards and at the federal level it's clear that we need to deal with capital gains tax and negative gearing.

When it came to the Energy portfolio and minister Duigan, it is shocking. It was good to put to him and push him on the fact that his federal parliament colleagues have abandoned the target of net zero emissions. It's an indictment on the federal Liberals.

When it comes to Marinus and the North West Transmission Developments, it's a key issue. The scale of the development and the scale of the investment is absolutely astounding. $191 million in the budget this year for TasNetworks and that's on top of $103 million in the last year's budget. What was shocking for many of us in here, was the willingness of the government to make a decision to invest in Marinus Link during the caretaker period despite the red flags in the whole-of-state business case.

It has raised red flags with the structure of the NEM with the increase in transmission costs and the government. Those increased transmission costs are well understood, and acknowledged by government, but they say that they're going to be mitigated by wholesale power prices being reduced and by Hydro Tasmania's super profits. However, when it came to the push and shove, and when we read some quotes into Hansard in Estimates, the Deloitte's report actually drafted to inform the whole-of-state business case, shows that there would be a 59 per cent increase in wholesale energy prices with a Marinus Link scenario versus a no Marinus Link scenario. Again, another house of cards. The whole argument for Marinus and the North West Transmission is built on these arguments where experts give advice, departmental officials give another advice and government go with that.

Meanwhile, Hydro profits are reducing, there's no dividend this year. Despite the fact that there's $470 million of super profits forecast into the future, absolutely no modelling was produced. No river flow modelling either. It's all based on river flow inflows into the dams and despite a pre-Basslink river flow assessment way back in 2001, it appears that Hydro has not done the work on assessing the inflows into its dams. We did have the opportunity to raise the issue about biofuels and the fact that all of these different biofuel proposals that are popping up across the landscape come at significant risk. The budget itself identifies some of these risks, and as far as we're concerned, we will identify one of the risks and that is native forest logging.

I only have a minute left. Through the Veterans' portfolio we had the opportunity to push the minister again on the Cenotaph. It's going to be 100 years old in a couple of weeks' time and it's going to be significantly impacted by the stadium. I also pushed the minister on recognising Aboriginal veterans' and veterans from the Black War here in Tasmania.

When it comes to minister Ogilvie, in the Arts and Heritage space, again, the heritage value of the Cenotaph was raised and the commitment of the RSL to it. I raised the issue of TMAG's leaking roof, and the fact that when I went to a presentation there a couple of weeks ago, there was a bucket on the floor in the middle of the central gallery. Also, the TMAG holds Aboriginal remains. They've managed to repatriate one third of the remains, but the balance is yet to be repatriated, and something needs to happen in that space.

We know the May 2027 Budget is going to cut deeper. We know it's going to cost Tasmanians dearly.

Ms HOWLETT - Chair, I firstly thank the department officials and their teams for the work in preparation for the Estimates hearing. I greatly appreciate their assistance, and I'd also like to thank the Chair of Committees as well. I also thank members in the House and the other place in their role in scrutinising the interim Budget, to ensure it continues to deliver for Tasmania, which it does.

We're focused on delivery and building a strong Tasmania now and into the future. We detailed investments in health, education and community safety and we will continue to strongly support our job-rich visitor economy sector, our racing industry and strongly support the prevention of sexual violence and family violence, and importantly, investment in job creating intergenerational infrastructure. To ensure that government can support and care for Tasmanians, we need to keep building and growing a strong economy. Our plan is to make Tasmania the best place to live, work, raise a family and do business.

I was pleased to detail to the committee that the Budget contained significant investment in our visitor economy. Under the Tasmanian Liberal government, funding for destination marketing has continued to increase. Last financial year we delivered $10 million of marketing funding and in this interim Budget we delivered the same amount for 25-26, and this is committed for 26-27. I will continue to fight for further investment in this industry, including through destination marketing, as the return on investment numbers speak for themselves.

We're continuing to deliver the tourism and hospitality support package to support the industry. As part of the package, we have delivered on our election commitment to recommence Eat Local, Stay Local Voucher program. More than 26,000 voucher claims have been paid since then.

The interim Budget continued our support for the Aviation Attraction Fund and I'm excited that the first ever direct flight between Hobart and Newcastle operated by Jetstar has landed now at 5.25 p.m. so that is incredibly exciting and this brand new route was unlocked through the Aviation Fund and it opens up Tasmania to a catchment of around 1.4 million people in the Greater Hunter region. 

We estimate visitors travelling on this route will contribute $41.5 million in spend into the Tasmanian economy over this season. It also outlined the funding priorities not part of this interim budget will be considered as part of the 2026-27 budget development process.

I outlined our ongoing support for the hardworking hospitality business operators across our state. In 2024, we committed $8.4 million over three years to Hospitality Tasmania through the Hospitality 2030 strategy to deliver initiatives to build and grow this job rich sector. This includes through the great customer experience, hospital health and programs focusing on business sustainability and workforce development, like the Women in Leadership program, for example. 

I was also asked about our continued support for the Tasmanian Hospitality and Tourism Academy to address skill shortages and labour shortages. I was pleased to detail that our event strategy 2023-27 continues to deliver a strong return on investment to the state and the budget provides strong support.

Events drive visitation to Tasmania, especially through the quieter months, building local communities and culture and supporting our tourism and hospitality businesses. I outline that in the 2024-25 financial year, the government supported a total of 155 events. This support brought over 129 200 visitors to Tasmania and generated over 742 700 bed nights.

The budget also provides funding for important major partnerships such as Hawthorn Football Club agreement renewal. Our commitment to deliver additional games in 2028 and 2029 means that UTAS Stadium will see more AFL content than ever before.

I advise the committee that Women and the Prevention of Family Violence are combined under one budget portfolio and output. The two are fundamentally linked, and we know that women disproportionately experience family violence and that improving gender equality outcomes fosters a safer environment for both women and girls.

I outlined to the committee that this year our government increased its support for our International Women's Day grants programs. Eliminating family and sexual violence is a key priority for our government. In the short and medium term, we know that family and sexual violence reporting rates have increased as more victim survivors gain trust, informal support and response systems as well as increased understanding about the nature of family and sexual violence.

Our government's third Family in Sexual Violence Action Plan 2022-27 survivors at the Centre is supported by funding of $140 million over 5 years to deliver vital actions which include additional Arch Centre funding since the action plan commenced. I was asked about an Arch Centre program.

Our centres in the north and south of the state are delivering a survivor-centred, holistic and integrated response to family and sexual violence and we are delivering a third arch centre in the north west which is expected to be operational in early 2026.

In my portfolio of racing, I was pleased to affirm our support for the broader racing industry, its participants and our regional communities. Work has started on a new racing industry funding deed to ensure the financial sustainability of Tasmanian racing industry continues. Importantly, this is not a review into whether there will be funding, it's a review of what the funding model will look like beyond 2029 for thoroughbred and harness racing codes.

I was able to detail the delivery of a strong plan for the biggest reforms in racing industry in decades. This includes the state's first racing Integrity Commissioner, Mr Sean Carroll. Mr Carroll was able to take questions and detail how he has hit the ground running with the immediate establishment of a complaints management framework, and how he has started working on assessment of the code of practice for racehorse welfare.

Chair, in conclusion, I was pleased to be able to outline how the Budget delivers for Tasmania, how the Budget backs in businesses, supports jobs, supports family, and builds stronger and safer communities, and importantly, with the future of our great state in mind. 

[5.41 p.m.]
Ms HADDAD - Deputy Chair, I look forward to making a contribution on two portfolio areas - first of all, my shadow portfolios of Justice and Attorney-General - and to reflect on some of what we discussed at the table with Mr Barnett.

First of all, I asked the minister about a very important project called the Astria Project. It might sound a little bit boring because it's an IT project, but it's actually a very vital project that's designed to connect the IT systems of everyone involved with the justice system, so from police to courts to the prison and everyone in between. It was initiated through legislation that passed through this place, I believe, almost nine years ago, eight or so years ago, and it's been going on for a very long time. Much to the frustration of practitioners, both prosecution and defence lawyers and people working in the prison and court systems, there is still far too much that happens in a paper-based way across those parts of the justice system. People are very much holding on for the Astria Project to be delivered. Not only is it important in terms of the work that goes on in those sectors, but it's a critical part of delivering access to justice. We know there are massive delays across our court systems in Tasmania, and they are referred to year on year in the annual reports of the Magistrates Court and Supreme Court. Many of those access-to-justice delays are caused through issues of sharing information. It should be so simple, and yet it isn't. 

The Astria Project was tended out to a company called Fujitsu, and the reason that I've continued to ask questions year after year about that project is that Fujitsu have a pretty poor record when it comes to delivering on significant IT projects. For example, they were commissioned to do a similar project to this one for the Queensland Department of Health that totally failed, and it cost their government another $1.2 billion to fix it. They were involved with a similar project again for the Health Department in Western Australia - and I had to double-check these figures - but that blew out to $175 million due to poor governance from Fujitsu. Outside Australia, they were also involved with some work for the United Kingdom Government on the Horizon IT system which led to wrongful convictions of hundreds of postmasters across the UK. Some of you might have read about that in the media. So, I do have worries about the deliverability of this project and the effect that it's going to have on access to justice in Tasmania.

The original budget for that project, when first allocated, was around about $16.6 million, and there was money added to it year-on-year through various budgets.

The minister gave some figures at the table. The total contribution so far to the project has been just over $32 million, as well as vendor costs to Fujitsu of $17.146 million. It's pretty clear that already we seem to be heading down a similar path to what happened in the United Kingdom, Western Australia and Queensland. That's project is really vital for the government to deliver on, because it has a critical effect on access to justice. It's all very well for the government to have a tough-on-crime agenda and a tough-on-crime mantra, which we hear all of the time from some of the more conservative members of that caucus, but that doesn't work if you don't -

Mr Ellis - Party rooms.

Ms HADDAD - Don't you say 'caucus'?

Mr Ellis - No, party room.

Ms HADDAD - My apologies. Mr Ellis, I'm looking at you.

With respect, to have a tough-on-crime agenda you actually have to fund all of the parts of the justice system for that to be effective, and actually have what they believe is going to be an effect on the crime rates. I have a different philosophical approach to that. Nonetheless the funding of the entire justice system is what is the point here. We need to fund all aspects - from police through to someone being convicted, spending time in prison and receiving rehabilitation services so that they, hopefully, don't continue to offend. Without funding that whole system, we are really failing Tasmanians - both victims of crime and those who want to rehabilitate. 

That certainly was relevant, as well, to the conversation that we had around funding of the community legal assistance sector. It was acknowledged by both sides of the table that yes, we want more money from the federal government. I've certainly made that clear to my federal colleagues, as I know the Attorney-General has. The fact is the national partnership agreement that funds the community legal assistance sector through the state governments is a two-sided agreement. There is also an obligation and an expectation on state governments to do their bit; to make sure they are doing their part to fund these vital services. Again, this is an access to justice issue. If we don't have defence and prosecution lawyers funded properly and a robust community legal assistance sector, in particular, to represent people who can't otherwise afford legal fees, then we are talking about a serious lack of access to justice.

One of the things that I found really disappointing is that, yet again this year, the duty lawyer service run by the Tasmanian Legal Aid Commission or Tasmania Legal Aid, is yet again being funded through the Solicitor's Guarantee Fund. That fund is not intended to fund core services of any kind. It's a volatile fund that sometimes has money in it and sometimes doesn't have money in it. Yes, it should be used for worthy projects in the justice system, but it shouldn't be used to fund core services. I would say a duty lawyer service operating out of the Magistrates Court, which is catching people before they fall through the cracks, making sure that people have representation who otherwise would be appearing in court unrepresented, is a really vital service. It needs to be funded securely through core funding and not through this year-on-year, unstable source of funds, such as the Solicitor's Guarantee Fund. 

Philosophically you might not think that those people deserve representation, but the fact is, it costs your government money when people aren't represented. It takes time out of the court. It means people have their matters adjourned and they come back again and again. It wastes the time of the court and it wastes the time of everyone involved, including victims of crime who might be there expecting a case to be heard and it gets adjourned yet again, through a lack of representation. If Legal Aid is not funded to be able to provide that vital representation to Tasmanians, well then, you're going to continue to see delays in your court system.

I also asked the government about the High Court case of DP vs Bird, which people might have read about. It's a High Court case which was about a victim/survivor of child sex abuse. He was a victim/survivor who was abused in the Catholic Church. To most lawyers' surprise, the High Court rejected the extension of vicarious liability to religious institutions for historical child sex abuse committed by clergy. Priests employed by a church who sexually abuse children are not considered liable because they're not considered to be employees of the church.

Well, that flies in the face of the commission of inquiry, of the Royal Commission into Institutional Circumstances of Child Sex Abuse, and of all of the work that is happening around mandatory reporting, around Working with Vulnerable People Cards, around systems that we are trying to make sure are in place to keep young people safe. This High Court decision flies in the face of that work, and it flies in the face of logic. To think that because a priest is in, what the court said, a relationship akin to employment - they're not actually an employee - they are therefore not liable for child sexual abuse.

It's grotesque to think that people could be getting away with sexually abusing children because they are in a powerful, privileged position of being a priest in a church. That's a decision that the High Court made, but states have a responsibility now to rectify our legislation, because state governments can legislate to extend that liability to people who are in a relationship that the High Court described as 'akin to employment'. States can fix this problem, this grotesque loophole that priests and other religious people working in religious organisations might have if they're not directly employed.

The commission of inquiry gave 191 recommendations, including what happens with volunteers in organisations and the liability that volunteers have to keep children safe, and the reporting requirements that are imposed on everyone, if they see someone working for or volunteering for an organisation doing the wrong thing. Yet here we have a really stark example of religious institutions, some of the worst offenders, being allowed to get off scot-free.

I did hope that I would hear from the Attorney-General that he had plans for legislation to deal with this early next year. He did say that it's been on the agenda of the national meeting of the Standing Council of Attorneys-General but, unfortunately, he's waiting for a national approach. This doesn't need a national approach. Lots of things do, and I agree things should be nationally consistent, but that's not a reason for us not to act first. I believe we absolutely have a responsibility to do that, particularly in the light of the incredible amount of work that went on in the commission of inquiry over recent years.

Also on Justice, I asked the Attorney-General for a timeline on a bill to ban conversion practices in this state. There was a draft bill released by the Premier some time ago, which got pretty negative feedback from the LGBTIQA+ community. Unfortunately, it might have had the counterintuitive effect of making it more possible for conversion practices to be inflicted on people in this state. For anyone who doesn't know what they are, they are a dangerous, cruel pseudoscience inflicted by sometimes religious-based organisations and sometimes not. Sometimes they're kind of veiled in scripture and theology. Basically, they are 'pray away the gay' type programs and they are deeply damaging. They inflict lifelong harm to people who are subjected to them. The legislation that was released for consultation didn't actually do enough to make sure that they would be banned here. In fact, they could have, like I said, the unintended effect of making it easier for people to conduct them here. 

Meanwhile, other states are acting on this. Meanwhile, other states are moving to ban conversion practices, and if we don't act quickly, we will be the last jurisdiction where it's not banned. This will, in some ways, make us a safe haven, which is an awful word to use, because these practices are anything but safe. I wanted a time frame from the minister on when he's going to ban conversion practices in Tasmania and recognise the harm that they cause.

I was really shocked to hear from the minister that he doesn't think they happen here - and they do. They do. He said he's looking at the information that he's been given on the draft bill, but he 'hasn't received any advice that harmful conversion practices are currently occurring in Tasmania.' That's a quote from Estimates. They do happen here, and I told him that. I asked him will he meet with advocates. We've had advocates come and sit in this parliament and offer to meet with Tasmanian parliamentarians. I urge the Attorney-General to do that, to listen to Tasmanians who have been victims of conversion practices, historic and recent. They are still happening now and if we don't act quickly to ban them, Tasmanians are at great risk.

Perhaps one of the biggest bombshells that we had revealed at the Attorney-General's Estimates was his plan to abandon and disband the Tasmanian Industrial Commission. That's an organisation that has been around for an extremely long time and is the arbiter of workplace disputes here for public sector employees. The Industrial Commission are seen as a cornerstone of justice for Tasmanian workers. They have specialised industrial experience and expertise that is pinpointed at being able to deal with public sector workplace matters and we are at great risk actually as a state of losing that really significant expertise if the minister pushes ahead with his plan to disband the Tasmanian Industrial Commission and move it into TASCAT. That's not a reflection on TASCAT. They have a very important role and we have supported their work, but they are also an organisation struggling with under-resourcing and we are already hearing, even in their early days of operation, that they don't have the resourcing needed to make the administrative decisions that Tasmanians expect of them and need of them.

This is a little different. This is a little different to the other jurisdictions that have been moved into the TASCAT. The Industrial Commission has a specific and very important role to protect the rights of Tasmanian workers. At a federal level, the same responsibilities have not been moved into the Administrative Appeals Tribunal. They sit with the Fair Work Commission, as they should. It is deeply worrying that the minister has the plan to disband the Industrial Commission and there wasn't a guarantee given at the table that those commissioner roles will be moved across. So, there is a risk that we're going to lose that specialised expertise in the TIC. There is a risk to Tasmanian workers, that we will lose that expertise and that very important role that the TIC plays in being the arbiter of workplace disputes in this state.

He said that the roles and functions would be moved. That's not the same as moving those commissioner positions across and there is a massive risk with that move. I want to reiterate the fact that at a federal level, that work is conducted by the Fair Work Commission as it should be. It hasn't been moved into a super tribunal and there is a strong argument for the TIC remaining as it is in the Tasmanian system as well. The fact is, that while the minister showed us that there was a downgrading in funding in the TIC that indicated its move across to TASCAT, most public sector unions found out about it at the Estimates table when they were listening to that output. Most unions found out about it that way, which is an appalling way to operate.

We heard from the minister that he intends to consult with public sector unions, but we already heard that a whole heap of work has already gone into this work. The minister said, and I quote, 'There is a lot of work that has gone into this already. There will need to be more work done. There will be a lot of consultation with the relevant state service unions, for example, they are stakeholders.' They are more than stakeholders. There's already a lot of work that's gone into this and they haven't even contacted the unions. They got news of this through seeing it at the Estimates table. This government has such a poor record when it comes to consulting on any matter. All they do is sit in their ivory towers and make decisions then broadcast them out and say that's consultation. Well, it isn't.

We certainly heard that very loudly from the ANMF from their branch secretary Emily Shepherd, who explained the important role of the TIC and her shock at the fact that she heard that news. She described it as an announcement in that budget Estimates that the government intends to absorb the TIC into the TASCAT. She said,

It's often only through the TIC that we are able to enforce the needs of appropriate consultation and procedural fairness, otherwise this government would do whatever it wanted, irrespective of the registered awards and agreements offering our members industrial entitlements, conditions and protection.

This was a bombshell dropped at the Estimates table that the minister, Mr Barnett, intends to get rid of the Tasmanian Industrial Commission. It's a massive risk to Tasmanian workers, it's a massive risk to their livelihoods and to the safety of their working conditions and the minister's done no consultation on it. He said a lot of work has already happened and he has a plan in the future to maybe consult with unions as stakeholders. That's not good enough.

I will also speak a little bit about the Health portfolio. I'm not the shadow minister for Health anymore, but I attended the Health Estimates with my colleague Anita Dow, on behalf of our colleague Sarah Lovell, who's the shadow Health minister.

In case I run out of time, I want to start on a positive in this portfolio, which is that I noted that the minister and I were co-hosting a panel. That happened just yesterday in the Tasmanian parliament. The context of it is that Hobart had the honour of hosting a really important national conference last week. It's the conference of AusPATH, which is the Australian organisation for professionals providing gender-affirming care to trans and gender diverse people. There is a lot of hate thrown at trans people in this state, I find it every time I post anything about the rights of trans people online; I face a wall of abuse on my Facebook page that takes hours to moderate. But I don't mind that, because it's nothing compared to the abuse that trans people experience in their day-to-day lives when they're just trying to get on and live their lives.

It was great that Tasmania could host that conference and they had dozens of Tasmanian health professionals come and attend that conference, who wouldn't otherwise have been able to go and learn so much from other practitioners working in that field. I really want to commend Equality Tasmania for putting together the forum that was co-hosted by myself and the minister for Health, Bridget Archer. They brought together four incredible speakers, Eloise Brook who's the CEO of AusPATH; Yalei Wilson, who's a clinical psychologist; Dr Miro Rainsford who's a counsellor and behavioural sciences researcher; and Amilie Courtney and her parents.

Amilie is a young trans girl who lives in Launceston, who has spoken with such strength and such integrity and such warmth about her experience of seeking and finding gender-affirming care. She's spoken in the federal parliament, she's spoken to Local Government Association and yesterday she spoke here, in the Tasmanian parliament. It was an absolute honour to meet her and to hear her story and her journey, but also, so importantly to hear from her parents, her amazing supportive parents and to recognise that not every trans or gender diverse person has that kind of loving support that Amilie has. In particular, I want to commend her - all four speakers were incredible, we learned so much at that forum about the importance of evidence-based gender-affirming care and that it absolutely saves lives. It absolutely saves lives and makes a huge difference to people who just need healthcare.

I mentioned that forum because it is relevant to some of those questions that we asked in Estimates about LGBTIQA+ health and I want to really commend the minister for her department's involvement in that conference, and I believe the minister spoke at the conference herself. That's such an important show of support and strength from a government that cares about the rights of trans people.

Importantly, we also had Rodney Croome, who's well known to people in this Chamber, come and speak about the launch of a new parliamentary friendship group. It's probably had the most sponsors of any parliamentary friendship group, which is a really lovely thing because it shows just what warmth and support there is across this parliament, both Chambers, all parties and independents, in supporting the rights of LGBTIQA+ Tasmanians. Those parliamentary friendship groups to me, are much more important than a symbolic support vehicle, they are much more important than that. Parliamentary friendship groups offer an opportunity for us to work across the parliament, across party divides in the interests of Tasmanian people, and that's certainly what Rodney focused on in his speech launching the parliamentary friendship group. It's co-hosted by the minister, Mrs Archer, myself as the Labor member for Clark and shadow minister for Equality; Kristie Johnston, the independent member for Clark; Cassy O'Connor, the Greens member for Hobart; Dr Rosalie Woodruff, the Leader of the Tasmanian Greens; and Meg Webb, the independent member for Nelson. There were many more than us, almost half of the lower House in terms of numbers were there yesterday at that forum. The former minister, Mr Jaensch, was there. Many members of the upper House were there as well and everybody who was there learnt so much. The community members I've spoken to since were incredibly heartened by how welcomed they felt in the Tasmanian parliament and that they saw that their rights were front of mind for people of Liberal, Labor, Greens and Independents, upper House, lower House, across the board, and that's a really beautiful thing.

I have gone longer than I intended to, which I always seem to do and there's a whole lot that I didn't speak about that I did raise in the Estimates, which is really important and I will try to return to it in another form of the House, but I want to finish with two short things. 

First of all, a sincere thanks to all of the staff involved with Estimates. It's a long few weeks, budget week followed by budget reply week, followed by two weeks of Estimates, and now here we are for the report back week. It's really important work that we do, that we scrutinise the Budget, but a whole lot of work goes in far above and beyond the 35 of us who have the privilege of serving our communities in this place and I want to in particular thank all of the parliamentary staff, the clerks, the attendants, the Hansard staff, and the catering staff who were here at all hours making sure that we were fed and watered throughout Estimates and, of course, all of the people who work for the various members of parliament as well. I know how much work and preparation goes into those budget hearings and then they're over in a blink of an eye. I know that there's always a lot more that we, as crossbenchers and opposition members, wish to raise, so thank you to everybody involved with that very significant work.

Finally, I just wanted to make a comment that is relevant to both of the portfolios that I sat in for Estimates and that is funding of not-for-profit organisations. The government has for 11 years now been promising longer-term funding agreements, outcomes-based purchasing indexation and more surety of funding for community organisations. Yet year on year we are seeing the pressure on those organisations get higher and higher and their resourcing go lower and lower because it isn't keeping up with inflation. Community organisations are being asked to do more with less because not only is their core funding at risk, and not only is their core funding not keeping up with the cost of providing services, the demand on their services is also increasing. We've seen that particularly through COVID and since COVID, that people who have never needed to rely on community services before are doing so for the first time in their lives.

We heard throughout the election campaign and in the lead up to this Budget and the last Budget, the pain that is being felt by the community services organisations out there providing grassroots support to people who need it most and how hard it is for them to be able to keep up with that increasing demand. They're doing more with less. They are eventually going to have to cut services or cut staff, or both. It was a real shock that in the lead-up to next year's Budget and in the context of this year's Budget, organisations were asked to find their own ways of cutting funding, and I want to read some of it into the Hansard. Community organisations who are coming to government every day and saying we can't do what you're asking of us, it's getting too hard, were asked these questions:

	Which programs or projects could be refocused to deliver core outcomes with more streamlined resources and a refined focus?
	
	Are there initiatives that could be reconsidered, helping balance the budget now without losing sight of long-term goals?
	
	3.	Are there programs or services that could be transitioned out as the needs of Tasmanians evolve and priorities shift?

The needs of Tasmanians are evolving because they're getting worse. The cost of living is higher, the cost of housing, the cost of food, the cost of utilities is higher. People are struggling. Families with two full-time incomes are struggling to make the rent, and yet this government is outsourcing their responsibility to do the right thing and adequately fund those organisations by asking them to find their own savings. Asking them how they can transition services out, how they cannot lose sight of long-term funding goals while finding savings today.

I was appalled when I saw that that's the way that this government is approaching community organisations because they might as well have just said to them, 'Hey, community sector, how can we get blood out of a stone?' Because that's what they're actually asking of these organisations that, don't forget, are providing services on behalf of government. They're not luxury services, they're not 'nice to haves', they are core social services that are being funded by the government to Tasmanians in need. 

If it wasn't clear to anybody yet that this government doesn't actually think about those people, doesn't actually really care very much about those people who are in great need of those services that they fund, well then this upcoming budget priority statement template is the evidence of that. If we needed any more evidence that this government doesn't really care about struggling Tasmanians, this is it. This is what this shows us. On that note, I'll conclude my comments.

[6.11 p.m.]
Mr Di FALCO - Chair, I rise to give the House a recap of the recent budget Estimates process and to outline the issues that stood out across the portfolios I examined. Estimates is not only an administrative exercise, it is our opportunity to drill down into detail, hold agencies to account and bring to this House an honest picture of where things stand.

Over the past week I asked questions across health, primary industries, energy, infrastructure, tourism, racing and state-owned companies. The sessions were long and I thank the standing members for their commitment. They gave us a clear view of the challenges and opportunities facing Tasmania.

One of the strongest themes that emerged was the pressure on our regional industries. I questioned the Minister for Resources on Sustainable Timber Tasmania land that has been tied up in uncertainty. Communities want clarity about future use and they want security for jobs. Ministerial leadership matters when whole towns rely on forestry, agriculture and land access.

I also raised issues around the forestry announcement made by federal government and the risk to jobs in regional Tasmania if Canberra overlooks our industry. Lyons alone carries a significant share of forestry activity, and families in those communities deserve certainty.

I prioritised speaking with Minister for Police on the delays facing firearms services. These delays are preventing farmers, land owners from protecting their crops and delaying talented sport shooters from competing. Safety is the priority, but unnecessary red tape and processing times are unacceptable.

I also spoke directly to the police about the need for infringement notices for minor offences. This brings undue stress on community as well as backlogs to court proceedings. I've been a strong advocate for expanding deer hunting access, however, I focused my questions on the Minister for Primary Industries on inland fishery funding. This department has been underfunded for a decade and is in dire need of additional funding. I will be strongly advocating for support in the May budget. 

In housing, the message remains stark. Demand is rising faster than the system can keep up. I focused my questions on the experiences of women with young children who cannot find stable accommodation, as well as older Tasmanians who are at risk of slipping into homelessness because of rising rents and unsuitable housing. The budget includes initiatives but Estimates reveal the scale of the backlog and delays in new supply. 

I asked how the government plans to clear bottlenecks in planning and land release, whether the agency is working close enough with non-government organisation and local councils.

Energy was another area of significant scrutiny. I questioned the Minister for Energy on their long-term strategies regarding TasNetworks. I raised concerns that many land owners along transmission corridors feel they are not being listened to. Transmission upgrades are necessary for future reliability, but they must be done in a way that respects property rights and communicates honestly with affected people.

Community and hospitality are also important areas. I questioned the minister on her responsibility to support Tasmania's visitor economy. I asked whether they were prioritising insurance and payroll tax as they are a significant cost to small businesses. 

Tourism operators rely on predictable travel options for booking regional dispersal and seasonal planning. With Tourism Tasmania I sought clarity on the strategy to attract high-value visitors, strengthen regional tourism and support small operators. Tourism remains one of the biggest economic drivers in our state and it must remain a priority, both marketing and infrastructure. In infrastructure and transport, I pressed the minister on future infrastructure projects that will bring economic development. If the stadium does not go ahead, the focus will be on roads and bridges. People do not come to our state for roads and bridges. We must invest in projects that enable economic activity.

During Estimates with the Health minister, I highlighted the ongoing pressure in emergency departments and the strain on staff. I raised concerns about long patient wait times, bed block, and the lack of available home care. I asked whether regional health services were being supported adequately, as the gap between regional and metro is widening. Health spending is one of the largest components of the Budget and Tasmanians expect improved outcomes for the amount being invested.

Racing also formed part of my focus. I asked the minister about the sustainability of the industry and the role it plays in regional communities. Hundreds of small businesses rely on racing to remain viable, from trainers, breeders to hospitality and transport. I will speak more about the greyhound ban during the debate. However, I made it clear I do not support this ban. My questions centred on transparency, accountability, and ensuring that the significant government contribution is delivering real benefits for participants and the broader economy.

The most consistent theme across all sessions was the need for long-term economic planning. Over and over again it became clear that Tasmania must grow its economic base if we expect to fund better hospitals, better roads, more housing, and stronger services. Estimates have reminded us that we cannot simply shift money from one department to another and assume the problem is solved. We must grow the overall size of the economy, or we will continue to struggle with rising demand and limited revenue.

While I raised concerns, I also acknowledged the positive outcomes - areas where agencies demonstrated real commitment to improvement. Many public servants are working very hard in difficult circumstances, and I thank them for their professionalism throughout the process. My criticism is never directed at staff; it is directed at systems and decisions that require improvement. As a member of this House, my responsibility is to make sure that taxpayers receive value for money and that the choices made in the budgets reflect the real priorities of Tasmanians. Estimates helped illuminate what is working and what is not. It clarifies the problems we must confront in housing, health, regional industry, energy, and infrastructure. It reinforces that tough decisions are ahead, and a growing economy is not optional - it is essential.

I thank the ministers and all agencies for their engagement. Budget estimates are demanding but vital for transparency. I stand here today more informed and more motivated to continue advocating for practical solutions that support regional Tasmania, strengthen our industries, and deliver better outcomes for families across the state. Thank you.

[6.20 p.m.]
Mr ELLIS - Deputy Chair, I swear I got the jump last time, but Mr Di Falco is quick. I am pleased to appear before Estimates Committee A in relation to my portfolios, Police, Fire and Emergency Management; Business, Industry and Resources and Skills and Jobs. I'm grateful to have been given the responsibility for each of them, and in each there's much work that has already been achieved. I look forward to continuing to deliver for Tasmanians in each of these portfolios. 

Firstly, I want to acknowledge all the hard work that goes into making Estimates possible, including from parliamentary support staff, departmental officers and ministerial office staff. These processes are important for government accountability and transparency, as Mr Di Falco has quite rightly just noted now, and that's reflected in the quality, quantity and volume of work that goes into supporting them.

Police, fire and emergency management is a critical portfolio that touches on almost every aspect of our community. I'd like to again put on the record my thanks for our amazing emergency responders. They go to work every day and volunteer in our communities without knowing what they're going to face, all to ensure that every one of us is safe. 

Our emergency services volunteers are almost 5000 across the state; another point of pride that I gladly spoke about at Estimates. Our volunteers are at the heart of community safety during floods, storms and bushfires. 

We're continuing to invest in new infrastructure, equipment and protective gear through an additional $2.5 million to the volunteer infrastructure investment fund. When disaster strikes, Tasmanians know the SES and TFS will be there, and this government is ensuring that those volunteers have what they need to keep the rest of us safe. 

Many of our first responders will be on duty and on call over the Christmas period and summer holidays. I want to acknowledge that they will be working to keep our families in our communities safe so that we can all have time off with our loved ones. We're very grateful.

At Estimates I spoke about the $3.7 million in funding that the government is providing for the police protective equipment and safety enhancement package. This initiative will provide funding for the multi-use integrated protection vest replacement project, which are a mandatory part of the Tasmanian Police uniform and provide officers with protection from firearms and dangerous weapons such as knives while maintaining a visible presence. It will also fund the permanent roll-out of handheld metal detector wands which will improve police officer safety when searching for metallic objects and dangerous implements such as knives in accordance with Reid's Law which was passed in May. Additionally, the package will fund a Taser trial for frontline policing and bring Tasmania in line with other jurisdictions in Australia.

We're delivering a police force at record strength, with 334 more officers on the frontline since 2014. That increased capability is exactly why Tasmania Police is detecting more crime and intervening earlier when harm threatens our communities. Last week we did hear some dramatic claims from the opposition about firearms crime. The reality is that when we have more police officers and stronger powers to uncover and disrupt criminal activity that previously went unseen, that's what our community expects, and that's exactly what our government's delivering.

On illegal and reckless driving, Tasmanians want to take strong action. I was pleased to speak at Estimates about our strong new laws that will crack down on hooning by implementing tougher penalties and creating the new offense of road rage. What last week did prove was that Tasmanians don't care about the grubby politics; what they care about is our government delivering for community safety, and that's exactly what we're doing, with record police numbers, increased powers and the tools that police need to keep our communities safe. That's our government's record and we stand by it.

Tasmania is one of the safest places to live and raise a family and that's why our government wants to ensure that we keep it that way, which is why we're continuing to back in our police and our emergency services. I was pleased to be appointed to the business, industry and resources portfolio this year, having the opportunity to again pick up resources and working with industry and employers right across Tasmania. At Estimates, I was pleased to outline the continued strength of Tasmania's economy and the work our government is doing to back in business confidence, secure investment and support regional jobs. The office of the Coordinator-General remains a major driver of new economic activity, facilitating $431 million in new investment last financial year and more than $5.3 billion since 2015, with a pipeline worth over $21 billion, in that, delivering real projects, real confidence and real jobs for Tasmanians. 

I also spoke about the strength of our advanced manufacturing sector which continues to win national and global work and demonstrate the on-island capability that we have here in Tasmania.

Through the Advanced Manufacturing Action Plan and Defence Tasmania, we're supporting local companies to secure new opportunities across defence and maritime supply chains, areas of genuine future growth for Tasmania. We discussed the government's commitment to standing with regional communities when major employers face challenges, whether it's the Bell Bay response team providing certainty through a period of transition or the support provided to King Island Dairy and Group 6 Metals, our government steps up when our jobs and communities are at stake and does the hard work needed to secure outcomes.

On resources, I was pleased to highlight that Tasmania recorded the second highest level of mining royalties on record in 2024-25, with around $71 million in royalties and a further $2.2 million in fees and rentals paid. These revenues directly fund essential services for all Tasmanians, demonstrating the importance of maintaining a strong and responsible mining sector.

I also outlined the new bolstering Rehabilitation Trust initiative, which provides an additional $100,000 per year for two years to expand Mineral Resources Tasmania's work in rehabilitating abandoned mine sites, responding to rising costs and community expectations about legacy mining areas. This commitment complements the existing $150,000 per year program and ensures contemporary mining continues to meet high environmental and rehabilitation standards. 

I also advised the committee of the progress we're making in supporting our renewable and sustainable forestry sector, which is worth over $1.2 billion to our economy annually, a progress and process that's put at risk by federal Labor's late night deal with the Greens and we'll have more to say on that soon.

Our timber workers deserve support, not to have their jobs and livelihoods put at risk through policy changes that they haven't been consulted on and have been roundly welcomed by extremist groups, hostile to forestry.

I was pleased to speak about the skills and jobs portfolio where we're supporting thousands of Tasmanians to get the training that they need to build their careers. We're getting on with it as a government, building a better training system that puts the learner at the centre and delivers real outcomes for Tasmania. Tasmania's economy is strong and growing. We've seen record job creation and investment, which means opportunities for Tasmanians. We're working together with community and training organisations to support more Tasmanians into training and employment pathways.

Our regional jobs hubs are making a real difference by connecting people with local employers and training. They're tailored to community needs and helping thousands of Tasmanians find meaningful work. Through the highly successful High Vis Army initiative, we're working together with the building and construction sector to deliver the next generation of tradies. There's a $6 million commitment to extend the High Vis Army to 2027 and to include sparkies and plumbers in this effort.

Our government's committed to building a skilled workforce to grow our tourism and hospitality sector that will support our visitor economy and TasTAFE School for the Visitor Economy will transform how we train future generations of tourism and hospitality workers. The Tasmanian Industrial Commission or industrial tribunals are a specific and very important part of the resolution of serious workplace issues, either for individuals, for classes of employees or indeed, for whole industries and sectors. All public servants are under the jurisdiction of the Tasmanian Industrial Commission. You have thousands and thousands of Tasmanians who would potentially need to get access to the Tasmanian Industrial Commission.

I raised a question the week before the Estimates regarding the performance of the current Industrial Commission where there were significant delays in making decisions. That was a critique of the performance. It was not a critique or a criticism of the structure. It's very important that people understand the history of industrial commissions - they are lay tribunals. The role of the commissioners in the first place is to conciliate. There's a long history of this in this country in state and federal jurisdictions where the first job is in the lay tribunal and industrial tribunal, how can you resolve the matter without putting the worker through cost and without putting the employer, or the state in this case [Bookmark: Phr2_O=14.6_D=15.2_C=0.83] , through significant litigation and cost. It's a lay tribunal with the emphasis on conciliation. 

In my former role in the trade union movement, I represented workers and industries [Bookmark: Phr3_O=30.0_D=25.8_C=0.88]  over many occasions. I would say 90 per cent of the resolutions back when I was representing working people, were done by conciliation and agreement. It's where the commissioner plays a role to get the parties together, mindful of industrial law, but focused more on resolution as opposed to [Bookmark: Phr4_O=56.6_D=2.4_C=0.87]  a very legalistic, expensive litigation process.

 [Bookmark: Phr5_O=60.0_D=28.8_C=0.84] I'm not necessarily against the functions of an industrial commission to be incorporated more broadly into TASCAT, but what I am against is the lack of a clear pathway for working people that are under the jurisdiction of the Tasmanian Industrial Commission to seek conciliation and arbitration. My fear is that rolled into a broader tribunal it will delay justice [Bookmark: Phr6_O=89.1_D=29.6_C=0.79]  and, as we know, justice delayed is justice denied. The point of my question is that I'm concerned about the delay in the decision making of the Tasmanian Industrial Commission. 

It seems to me that, over the last decade, there's been an over reliance on a very black and white law legal approach to workplace relations. That approach never works. They talk about how the net interstate migration has been negative. It puts our rating in context of the institutions that we rely on - that Australia, the Commonwealth and state governments have relied on for many years.
