Dr BROAD - Chair, it's a very unusual year, this year. We've had a late Budget, then we've had budget Estimates, then we've had Government Business Enterprise hearings, all sort of rolled into one. Now we're reporting back on the lot. This is fourth or fifth week, and the big question that's in my mind is, is this as good as it gets? I think that's probably the question that the Liberal members should be asking themselves: is this as good as it gets, after 11 years? I think if the Liberal members reflected honestly, they're definitely on the downhill slope, because everything is getting worse. If you look across a whole range of budget issues, the budget is in a far worse state now than 2014 when they came into government. Health is worse, housing is worse, education is worse: Just about everywhere you look, things are worse. Performance of the ministers, I would argue, is also worse. How did we actually get to this point? One of the things that I will be talking about today is the way the standards have been slipping, definitely been slipping, in the years I have been here - eight years now? - I've seen the standards definitely slip, and we have seen it in this budget.

This budget is actually already out-of-date, and it's already out-of-date for a couple of reasons. The first reason is because during this whole budget process, the state has had two credit downgrades. This is just astonishing, really, and as Saul Eslake said, 'It is a vote of no confidence in this government', the Liberal government's budget management. Two credit downgrades, first Moody's and then Standard & Poor's decision to downgrade Tasmania's credit rating, shows that this Liberal government has lost control of the Budget.

What do these credit downgrades mean? These downgrades mean that Tasmania will have higher borrowing costs for years to come, and that will mean that there will be less for every dollar that's spent on interest. It won't create a single job in Tasmania, it won't provide a single hospital bed in Tasmania, it won't build a single home in Tasmania, and it won't keep a single teacher in a classroom in Tasmania. It will simply be money that's heading somewhere else. I just have to remind the Liberal members that when they came into office some 11 years ago, there was no net debt. None. No net debt. Now, Tasmania has just officially become the state with the worst finances in the country. The worst finances in the country, I just have to repeat that. When the Liberals came into government 11 years ago, there was no net debt, and now Tasmania has the worst finances in the country. It has the worst levels of debt, of state sector debt, and also, now, the worst credit ratings. We have a Treasurer and a former treasurer who say that this was actually a great budget. It is just beggars belief.

The debt keeps rising. The debt that we have today, this very moment, is not the problem. The problem is the trajectory of the debt. I have been warning about this for years and so have others, including Saul Eslake, including Treasury, including a lot of other people, that the debt is well and truly out of control. That is the problem. It's not the debt that we owe today, it's where the debt is headed.

Let's just talk about these credit rating agencies. These are global organisations and what they do is they rate various countries and states all around the world. They have their methods and so on, so we can compare against other countries and other places. Moody's said that Tasmania's credit rating, when they downgraded it from a A2 to A3, making it the lowest of all Australian states, they said the decision was due to 'a sharp rise in the state's debt burden and growing interest costs', which it says will be difficult to contain over the next two years. 

As a result, these pressures will increasingly constrain the state's ability to respond to future shocks, reflecting weaker governance strength than we assessed in the past.

You have a global credit ratings agency saying that this state, the Liberal government, reflects weaker governance strength than we have assessed in the past. Weaker governance - that's what a global ratings agency said and rated your government. The credit ratings are used by lenders as an indication of the ability to pay debt and, as a result, we will actually be paying higher interest rates when we go to the market seeking people to hold our debt for us. 

I think the most ridiculous thing about Moody's credit downgrade was the way that Treasurer Eric Abetz described it. The Treasurer said he was 'not alarmed' by the downgrade, and instead he 'welcomed Moody's revising its outlook from negative to stable'. So, they had a higher credit rating with a negative outlook, and now the state has a lower credit rating with a stable outlook, and he's saying, 'Oh, that's great, we've gone from negative to stable.' Well, you've gone down a level. It's so silly. 

For those on the other side who do not understand the reality of this credit downgrade, I'll give you an example. It would be like a manager in the English Premier League being very happy about being safely lowered down into the championship league. That is the equivalent of Eric Abetz's statements. It's just like an English Premier League coach/manager who's been downgraded into the championship league saying, 'Oh, well, at least we're safe in the championship league for now.' That is how ridiculous the Treasurer of Tasmania's statements were around the credit downgrade. Absolutely unbelievable, his comments. Talk about head in the sand.

Then we had Standard & Poor's. After that first credit downgrade, late last week we had another one. Standard & Poors ,when they gave their credit downgrade, they said:

We expect Tasmania's operating accounts to remain in deficit until fiscal year 2027 due to strong growth in operating expenditure in recent years. This will result in much weaker fiscal metrics than for many 'AA+' rated peers globally. 

They know this stuff. They watch it around the globe, and they've said that Tasmania has weaker fiscal metrics and that's why they're downgrading us as a state. In addition, they're saying the state's capital rising capital spending will weigh on overall cash deficits, driving its debt burden structurally higher. What that actually means is, because this state, and this government, are borrowing to pay interest, that will put further pressure on the budget. The classic example would be putting groceries on the credit card. Then, Standard & Poor's went on to say:

We consequently lowered our long-term issuer credit rating on Tasmania to 'AA' from 'AA+'.

Also in this, they give a warning. The credit ratings agencies gave the state a warning when they said that Tasmania was on a negative outlook. That happened a year ago and a year ago before then, where the credit ratings agency said, 'Look, we've now got a negative outlook about you.' The state did nothing, the state kept spending, the Liberals kept spending, the debt's getting worse, so now we've had a downgrade.

There's also a warning in Standard & Poor's credit decision here. They're saying, in the downside scenario, 'we could lower our rating on Tasmania if its operating deficits persist or if its deficits after capital accounts underperform our forecast, driving debt materially higher'. That is a massive warning for this state, because what we know is, this government has lost control of the budget and so they are not going to outperform any forecasts. These are dangerous times for the state and the state budget.

I also have to reflect on other issues in this Budget, like housing. Now, as I've said, is this as good as it gets? Is this as good as it gets?

If you go back and look at priority back in when the Liberals came into government some 11 years ago, the wait for category one, or the most urgent, was 21 weeks, which is less than six months. Only last month, the month before, it was almost two years. It was just outrageous. 

How can this happen? It's because the government are simply not building enough houses. They're pretending they are, but they're not. I will give you one classic example, there are many. I have in my hand a housing land supply order for Devonport. This became effective in July 2019, and I think Roger Jaensch was the minister who signed this - I can't tell the signature, it's a bit hieroglyphic, but I'm pretty sure Mr Jaensch was minister at the time. This is for 39A North Fenton Street in Devonport, which was the old bowls club site. The bowls club moved out to Spreyton, the land became available, the government acquired it and now it's called Higgs Court.

This Housing Supply Order was supposed to fast track, by doing all the rezoning and the like, as part of the order. This order's intended zone in relation to the application is to be declared in a residential zone referred to in the applicable planning zone, so it's supposed to get things going a little bit quicker. That was 2019, so six years ago. I went out there only just last week - 2019 there are a number of subdivisions, none of them have sold or maybe one has sold - there is one house that has been constructed and it's only very recent. There has been one house constructed on this lot, which was supposed to be 13 or 14 lots, I think from memory, and there's one single house.

What do you have in that place? Ironically, what you have is homeless people having set up camp in this area. Homeless people, and there's one house built. Oh, the irony. Those homeless people should have a roof over their heads and they don't because in six years only one house has been built, and that very recently - they've only just moved in. One house in six years and homeless people camping around the outside, waiting for a house. How long are they waiting? Well, probably getting close to two years.

This is one of the most frustrating things that I get in my political life is those people coming into my office who are just about to be homeless, whose lease has ended - I just had people recently, there's been a cancer diagnosis, there's children with autism, and they're about to lose their rental because the owners are doing renovations. They've been on the housing waitlist for years. Years. And they are scared. They're petrified that in only the end of January, they will be homeless. You have a husband who has a terminal cancer diagnosis, and you have autistic children, and they could be homeless. That just says everything about this government. They're not doing the work. As I said, is this as good as it gets?

If the Premier had any standards, he would investigate. In Question Time today he said he didn't know anything about this. Well, he needs to get to the bottom of it, because it is outrageous.

Can you imagine what the Liberals would be doing if a government business enterprise was openly campaigning for the Labor Party by sending text messages promoting a Labor policy to all the people that they had a number for? Promoting a Labor policy, there would be outrage. Where's your outrage, Liberal members? Do you seriously think that Tasracing campaigning for a Liberal government during an election is okay? [7.24 p.m.]
Ms JOHNSTON - Chair, after a week of Estimates, one thing is very clear. The government has taken an overstretched public sector and laid upon it even more complexity, reform and financial pressure, while offering very little honesty about the depth of the challenge we face. The stadium, sat like a shadow over almost every portfolio, whether it was questions about planning, governance, debt servicing or the hundreds of conditions that now need to be met, the message was the same. The government has taken on a multi-billion dollar project at the exact moment the state is least equipped to manage it, and it can do so thanks to Labor, who have unconditionally backed the stadium in without even a whimper of opposition. Labor, who sold out to the Liberals and have now seemed to form an unholy country's first Liberal-Labor coalition, now find themselves in direct conflict with angry rank and file members. These members do not want their MPs to vote for the project.

It has been revealed to me today the prominent Devonport Labor branch voted last week in support of a motion opposing the stadium and they've been muzzled by the head office. It begs the question: how many other branches are also opposed to the stadium and have they also been muzzled? How can Labor's Legislative Council members vote for the stadium? The Devonport branch told me directly:

As a branch, we are not supporting the PLPs and the executive's position on the Macquarie Point Stadium purely on the economics and the commission's report.

Of course, they are referring to the Tasmanian Planning Commission's (TPC) report. Labor's branch members get it. They know how dire our financial position is as a state. They heard all the warning signs given by the TPC and they saw the evidence of that in Estimates.

The Estimates hearings painted a very bleak picture indeed. Departments are already dealing with major reforms, the Youth Justice Blueprint, the commission of inquiry recommendations, the Watt review of the Public Service and the ongoing crises in family violence, health, disability, literacy and education. Every agency is stretched. Workers are reporting burnout and rising workers compensation claims, and in some departments between one third and more than half of all workers' compensation claims are now for psychological injury. That is an unmistakable sign of departments under serious stress and strain, people trying to meet ever-growing demands with shrinking teams, unclear priorities and constant structural upheaval. Morale is fragile, yet they are expected to absorb a project of the scale of the stadium without additional capacity and without clear governance.

Time and time again we heard about worrying signs of issues not being dealt with, problems put off, denial that there was even an issue to face in the face of clear evidence that there was, and a lack of resources or will to do anything about the issues.

I remarked to one of my parliamentary colleagues when we were both trying to self-medicate with a coffee that it felt like we were witnessing multiple fires and the government was just standing by with a barely a thimble of water telling us there was nothing to see here, and all of this is happening in the same week that Tasmania's credit rating was downgraded by both Moody's and Standard & Poors. The Treasurer tried to put a brave face on it, but the rating agency is explicitly pointed to weak budget management, rising debt and unsustainable spending patterns, including election-driven promises and a stadium, as major risks. These warning signs are not political commentary. They are hard external assessments of the state's financial health.

Across the Premier's and Treasury's portfolios we saw the same troubling patterns: no transparency around the mandatory player card report; no commitment to evidence-based gambling harm measures; no assurance that essential programs like 26Ten will continue; unrealistic assumptions about Public Service wages, and a so-called natural attrition strategy that, everyone knows, is neither strategic nor realistic; and the Premier's Wellbeing Framework, an idea that had potential, was revealed to be no more than a communications tool than a driver of budget allocations. That is a profound missed opportunity. If wellbeing is to mean anything, it must shape spending decisions, not sit on a shelf as a glossy publication.

In youth justice, the contradictions were even more stark. Whilst minister Palmer reiterated her commitment to the Youth Justice Blueprint and the commission of inquiry, both of which call for reducing young people's contact with police and the justice system, not expanding it, minister Ellis declared everything is on the table and maintained his call for adult time, adult crime laws. These two positions cannot coexist. You cannot talk about rehabilitation while designing policy around punishment.

When I challenged Minister Ellis to provide any evidence that adult crime, adult time laws actually make our community safer and prevent youth crime, he had the audacity to table a media release from the Premier of Queensland; a finer piece of political puff you could not find. Not evidence. Indeed, there is a plethora of evidence to the contrary that shows that these types of laws actually do more harm, but then again, this government has a serious allergy to evidence and expert advice.

Whilst I'm still discussing youth justice, it would be remiss of me not to mention the revelations about the funding of JCP youth. We heard evidence of deeply inappropriate practices, disturbing conduct and astonishingly, there was no government, no departmental advice to fund the program. No analysis, no scrutiny, no accountability, only young people at risk. Meanwhile, programs that actually work, like the Tasmanian Aboriginal Legal Services Bail Support Program, Dress for Success and a range of prevention focused community led services struggle each year for stable funding despite delivering proven results and clear savings to the state. This is the government's Achilles heel. It does not invest enough, or for long enough, in the prevention measures that stop harm before it occurs. Prevention doesn't produce a ribbon cutting moment. It doesn't generate a headline during an election campaign, but prevention is precisely what reduces demand on hospitals, police, prisons, child safety, housing and emergency services. Every dollar saved in acute responses tomorrow starts with prevention today, yet prevention is treated as optional while far more expensive crisis response continues to swallow the budget.

The Auditor-General made it clear that they did not have the resources required to conduct the performance audits Tasmania desperately needs and that transparency barriers, including limited access to cabinet documents, are hampering their work. Investing in proper audit and oversight is not a cost, it's protection against waste, mismanagement, and exactly the sort of government failures we saw around JCP youth.

When it came to revenue, the Treasurer left the door slightly ajar, reluctantly saying he's open to suggestions. That is not leadership. He has the entire Treasury at his disposal. If we are facing cuts to public and community services, then government assistance to industry must also be on the table. The Treasurer agreed with that in principle, but in the next breath ruled out increasing casino taxes, a tax that wouldn't affect vulnerable Tasmanians and only impact on one very large and influential gambling business. Mind you, that would probably jeopardise the next generous political donation to the Liberal Party, so there'll be more chance of pigs flying, I suspect.

However, if the Treasurer was even remotely sincere in putting the private sector under the microscope, I look forward in the next budget for evidence that subsidies and grants to private entities are being wound back unless they deliver a clear, measurable public benefit, because difficult years lie ahead. Cuts are coming. The state's finances are in a dire shape. 

The only way Tasmanians will accept difficult decisions is if the government is honest about the problem, transparent about its evidence and committed to shielding those who will be harmed by cuts. They also need to see the government itself is willing to make sacrifices, not only the public sector, not only frontline workers and not only vulnerable Tasmanians. That's why I'll continue to call for legislative end to pork barrelling. The Auditor-General and the Integrity Commission have recommended it, experts have recommended it, and even ratings agencies point to election driven spending as a budget risk. Ending pork barrelling is one of the clearest ways to restore public trust, improve governance and repair our finances.

Transparency was a repeated theme throughout Estimates, and nowhere is a lack of it more obvious than the government's refusal to release Estimates briefs prepared for ministers. My office sought them through Right to Information over the last week or so, and we've been told departments have prepared too much material and it would take too long to assess our RTI requests. That is simply absurd. In other jurisdictions, these briefs are routinely released, sometimes proactively. If this material was prepared to inform the public record during Estimates, then it should be ready for release now. Anything less is a deliberate barrier to scrutiny. We all saw those folders sitting on the tables. They ought to be released.

Estimates week confirmed what many Tasmanians already feel. A government making high risk commitments without the government capabilities, resources or transparency needed to deliver them, while long-standing crises across literacy, health, education, housing, disability and community safety continue to worsen and absorb an ever growing share of the budget.

The public service is not a problem to be trimmed. It is the backbone of delivering services for future generations. Yet we heard again and again that this is being hollowed out by attrition, stretched by reforms, overwhelmed by demand and now threatened by budget cuts. Cutting people who deliver essential services does not make government more efficient, it makes it less so. It forces remaining staff to juggle multiple roles, creates bottlenecks, slows down service delivery and increases stress, feeding directly into the rising psychological injury claims we are now seeing. An overstretched workforce is not an efficient workforce.

Tasmania can't afford business as usual, and Tasmanians deserve more than optimistic talking points. They deserve honesty, they deserve evidence, they deserve decisions that genuinely serve the public interest, especially the interest of young Tasmanians who will inherit the consequences of what we do now. If we want a healthier, safer, more prosperous state for the next generation, we must invest in prevention, demand transparency and make decisions that strengthen, not weaken, the systems that every Tasmanians rely on. That is a standard the government must meet now, but instead the government has given us a 'vibe'. The pretence of a stadium-led budget recovery, with no evidence, no modelling and a blatant disregard for expert advice.

I spoke in my contribution on the stadium order debate a couple of weeks ago, that I feared that there'd be so many horrible told yourself moments to come if the order passed this parliament. Well, those moments sadly, are already here. Credit rating downgrades have already come to fruition. Significant cost loads are already occurring, a 900 per cent increase in the transportation cost of sand for the concrete, just to name one. An admission that we, the Tasmanian people, are paying for MPDCs borrowings and the borrowings of our equity injections, and programs and capital works unfunded or delayed thanks to the need to pay for a stadium that divides our state. These are really tragic, told-you-so moments that have already come to fruition. It is beyond sad. As I go about my community and Clark, I hear the worry, and I see the impact.

May 2026 is only six months away, but we have been warned. We must act now and do the right thing. Tasmanians are relying upon us.


DIVISIONS 8 and 10 -
Minister for Primary Industries and Water, Minister for Veterans' Affairs

[7.38 p.m.]
Mr PEARCE - Thank you, Chair. It's good to be with you again, isn't it?

CHAIR - Always a pleasure.

Mr PEARCE - I rise today to reflect on the Estimates proceedings, where I was pleased to provide a comprehensive update on the Tasmanian government's priorities right across agriculture, bio security, water management, fisheries and Veterans' Affairs. These are sectors that underpin our economy and sustain our regional communities.

During Estimates, I outlined that the Tasmanian agri-food sector continues to be a cornerstone of Tasmania's economy, now worth $2.46 billion at the farm gate. I reported to the committee that wine grape growers have achieved record yields of up to 35-37 per cent and opening new export markets right across the world. Dairy production remains robust, and fruit growers delivered strong exports, particularly cherries into Asia, blossoming - pardon the pun. Field crops such as cereals and poppies show promising price outlooks.

I also noted that during Estimates, that in October, [inaudible], and I realised something very wonderful when we released the paper on developing a new Tasmanian agricultural strategy, that builds on previous work that seeks feedback from industry on priorities such as innovation, smarter regulation, climate resilience and market expansion. 

Since 19-20, the government has invested over $34 million in agricultural research throughout Tasmania, through the Institute of Agriculture and industry partners contributing more than $66 million. I explained to the committee that Tasmanian Farm Innovation Hubs and Farm Business Resilience programs are helping farmers to adopt new technologies, new R&D and prepare for drought resilience and improve profitability. I emphasise also to the committee that the Tasmanian bee and pollination industries are absolutely essential and underpinning our agricultural industry.

To this end, we have reduced red tape by streamlining hive licencing. One licence now will cover multiple sites across paddocks rather than one licence per paddock, making life easier for our farmers and reducing that red tape. We have also released the Varroa Mite Action Plan of 2024-2034, developed with industry to help reduce risk and prepare for potential incursions. I explained to the committee that the Tasmanian poppy industry relies on its reputation as safe, secure and reliable growing region in Tasmania.

I reported the Poppy Advisory Control Board amended licence conditions in January 25. Growers are now required to approve the property rather than paddock by paddock, again making this easier, reducing red tape, making farmers get on with their business. These reforms provide flexibility while maintaining Tasmania's reputation as a secure supplier of pharmaceutical poppy material.

I advised the committee that while wild fallow deer are a part of Tasmania's heritage, deer increasingly present management challenges with deer increasingly impacting our peri-urban areas, agricultural and forestry areas, biodiversity and causing some safety risks. The budget commits $2.25 million to continue to deliver Tasmanian wild fallow deer management plans which will guide our approach to reduce the numbers.

Importantly, public consultation for the 2025 deer plan is now open, focusing on streamlining zone one and making zone three alpha a complete eradication zone. I reported also that our biosecurity systems is one of our greatest strengths here in Tassie. We continue to exclude evasive pests such as Varroa mite and Queensland fruit fly whilst preparing for high pathogenic Avian Influenza.

I highlighted to the committee that fresh water is a fundamental part of the Tasmanian economy, the environment and the Tasmanian way of life -

Mr ELLIS - Hear, hear.

Mr PEARCE - Mr Ellis. Tasmanian way of life is important and fresh water becomes an integral part of that. Our $10 billion by 2050 target will meet water in the right places for the right reasons to grow the right crops, again adding to the Tasmanian way of life, Mr Ellis. The Rural Water Strategy is our blueprint for managing water resources and our sustainability. Since 2021 over $60 million has been invested in science, policy, infrastructure initiatives, with an additional $440,000 in this year's budget.

The 2025 progress report shows tangible results, including extending the groundwater monitoring network, surveying over 500 boars in the Smithton Syncline alone, engaging with King Island and Flinders Island communities on water security and biosecurity sampling at 75 sites to track waterway health. I explained that the 16 major initiatives that are currently underway are deepening our understanding of water resources and refining our policy settings.

I was also pleased to advise the committee that the Tasmanian Irrigation system and scheme delivered over 93,000 megalitres of high surety water last year alone with major transfer systems and schemes underway, including the Northern Midlands Scheme, Sassafras Wesley Vale Augmentation and many others. These projects underpin long-term water security for our farm businesses and provide opportunities for increased productivity.

In the fisheries area, I reported to the committee that maintaining waterways for both commercial and recreational fishing remains our priority. The budget provides $1.3 million over the forward Estimates for the Institute of Marine and Antarctic Studies to continue world-class fishery research into core fisheries. The Tasmanian Fisheries White Paper sets out our pathway to modernised fisheries management and ensure that legislation can adapt sustainably in order to manage stocks.

I highlighted also that the Inland Fisheries Service continues to lead pest fish management for species such as redfin perch, terrible things, and tench. Targeted surveys for 2024-25 found no established populations thanks to electrofishing, where we zap those little pests. Netting and community engagement also assisted in removing those pests from our waterways. These efforts protected Tasmania, its unique freshwater ecosystems and supported recovery of valuable fishing resources.

Trout populations in Lake Sorell have rebounded strongly and deliver excellent angling opportunities, while Lake Crescent continues to be a renowned trophy-sized trout fishing attraction.

In the veterans' space, I was honoured to have updated to the Estimates Committee that the Tasmanian Government remains committed to the unwavering advocacy for our veteran community. Our state has an extraordinary legacy of service with 15 of Australia's 101 Victoria Cross recipients hailing from our great state. It's more per capita than any state or territory in the country. We have something to be proud of.

The Tasmanian government delivered practical, targeted support for our veterans through the Tasmanian Veterans Employment Strategy, supporting veterans to explore roles within the State Service align. The Veteran Wellbeing Voucher Program encourages healthy and connected lifestyles, now expanded to include widows and partners and those close to our veterans. Active engagement with veterans' reference groups ensures these policies are well aimed, well informed and are informed by the experience.

The Final Report of the Royal Commission into veterans' suicide, released on the 9 September 2024, made 122 recommendations. They include the establishment of the Veteran Wellbeing Agency. The Tasmanian government carefully reviews these recommendations and ensures alignment with national reforms.

Across agriculture, biosecurity, water management, fisheries and veterans' affairs, I was pleased to have presented to Estimates the practical, targeted and forward-looking initiatives being delivered by our government. These investments strengthen our economy, they safeguard our environment and they honour those who have served our nation and I'm proud to represent all of those important pillars in my portfolio responsibility. We remain committed with the government to ensure Tasmanian industries, our communities and our veterans are well supported, they're recognised and they're empowered for the future.

[7.45.14 p.m.]
Ms BADGER - Chair, I should start with an apology because when we were discussing in the House last time, extending the time for debate on the stadium, and we were talking about the length of time that we have to respond to big items such as the budget and I came in here and said we had 20 minutes for the Budget reply and 20 minutes for the Estimates reply, I was wrong. We have 30 minutes. We have an even longer time.

Reflecting how much of a disappointment that debate was due to the time constraints, when we have a budget situation, a financial situation in this state that is in such a poor situation that both Moody's and S&P have downgraded the state's credit rating, and what a time that is with the budget falling as well, but it's not only the financial mismanagement that we're going to see long-term impacts having on the Budget, but on the social fabric of this state as well. When we have organisations and NGOs that are missing out on funding, perhaps not in this interim Budget, but what's going to come in May, what has to come into the future? It's also going to impact our environment above all else because the funding into managing invasive species, into extending protected areas, into properly managing our protected areas, and upscaling what we can do, simply isn't there.

Member for Franklin, Mr O'Byrne, spoke in his address about how the Estimates process is changing and losing a bit of its scrutiny appeal and its ability to extract information. As far as not being able to extract information out of a minister and bad budgeting goes, the Parks minister absolutely took the cake. That was peak poor performance. Parks are going to be getting an extra $800,000 revenue by doubling the cost of parks passes for senior citizens, while they have $61 million in major projects and they could not provide any detail whatsoever on it because they were concepts, just concepts. These have been concepts: the Tyndall Range since 2021 and the Freycinet Gateway since 2019, that was a concept in 2017 when Matthew Groom, the then-minister, started playing around with the idea of what that might look like. That is not a concept now when we have contractors reaching out to our offices, to the community, and talking about the millions of dollars of concrete that they're going to be putting into this project, and yet we have Parks saying that they don't have that information available to us because it's just a concept. It's simply not right.

Speaking of budget mismanagement, up at the Tyndalls, there are three mining leases along the proposed walking track. They're not current, they're just exploration leases, but we have the exploration grant drilling initiative here in Tasmania, where if you're a mining company looking to explore possible minerals in the state, you can apply, and the state government will give you that grant to go out and do it. Since 2021, where you have three mining leases along a proposed walking track that the government's willing to put in $40 million, at least two of those mining leases have received a grant for mining exploration. Why are you paying them more taxpayer money to go out there to explore for minerals? What are you going to do if they actually found anything worth mining? Are you going to dump the Tyndalls walk? It makes absolutely no sense how that budget's been managed.

On Mount Field, we asked about the $7.8 million that is going to be invested into making Mount Field a year-round destination. Again, 'It's just a concept, we can't provide anything more than it would possibly be spent on some huts and walking tracks', which is just standard Parks work. In the story as old as time, two days after Estimates when we couldn't get told anything, of course, we received an RTI back that very explicitly showed us exactly the options and the details that were going on. That RTI had been fully explored with five different scoping options for how the money could be invested at Mount Field. It recommended option number five and that was dated March 2025. Yet, when we're asking about it in Estimates, we can't get that information. I'm not sure if the minister is just simply completely not across his portfolio or if he's misleading. Unfortunately, I think he's completely not across his portfolio because when I was asking about the TWWHA, the Tasmania Wilderness World Heritage Area, one of the highest-ranked Wilderness World Heritage areas on this planet, for which the Parks minister in this state is a custodian of, I asked him about the extensive cultural heritage assessment that has to happen on that property. There are 10 stages in that. It's been ongoing for years. We simply asked for an update, and he asked me more questions about what that process actually was than what I was able to ask him. Absolutely appalling.

On the reserve activity assessment (RAA) reforms, we asked the minister when the government was walking away from the options paper that they touted was going to go out after the full reforms that had been requested. There was a statement in that where the minister said they would be looking at streamlining coordination between the RAA and the Land Use Planning and Approvals Act (LUPAA) process. We asked what that meant. What does that mean for the future of the RAA process? We couldn't even get answers on that.

The special treatment of the proponent over at Lake Malbena and the Halls Hut continues stronger than ever. He wasn't even required to provide the six-monthly update through the government's EOI process - the 'unlocking our parks' process. Every single other proponent that has a project under that EOI process has to provide six-monthly updates. That could be local government updates, updates through the Environment Conservation and Biodiversity Conservation (EPBC) process, or simply the business case that they're refining to ensure that their product is modern and fit for purpose? Why is it that Daniel Hackett, the proponent for the Lake Malbena proposal, does not have to give six-monthly updates when everybody else does?

We also spoke about getting the TWWHA back onto the state of conservation reporting cycle for UNESCO. That's a really important reporting cycle because it means that that we have international oversight coming from bodies like the IUCN and ICOMOS. When we're seeing increasing dry lightning strikes and fire, really risking the both the natural values of the World Heritage area and the cultural values as well having that oversight and scrutiny and backing and support is incredibly important and yet there's no real talk and we've raised it in this place before and we've raised it with the premier and again, I got more questions from the minister about that than he got from me.

On fire, as the climate becomes more unstable and the weather is more predictable and more extreme events are growing in frequency and severity, we need greater investment into emergency services. It is crucial to ensure that they have the equipment, that we have the paid professionals on the ground, greater volunteer resources, they have the resources for planning and the response tools, options and contingency plans. It was heartening to hear from the TFS about the great work that they've been doing and off the back of this summer's fires where 100,000 hectares of wild places burnt, the mission that they took over to LA to learn from the fires over there and that's particularly important here in southern Tasmania, where we have the Derwent Valley and Hobart that are particularly dense urban areas, by Tasmanian standards, with enormous fire risks, that are almost unlike anything else on the planet. Having the TFS go over to LA to learn those lessons is fantastic and something that we should see continuing into the future.

It's heartening to hear as a part of their next steps of what we can do in Tasmania to stop the house-to-house ignition that really infiltrated and exacerbated what happened in LA. Looking at furthering the work, that Prof David Bowman at UTAS is already doing and the work that can be happening with RACT on mitigation, and some of the grants that have been looked at for home hardening projects, which is something that's already happening in the US. It will be very interesting to watch and see how that unfurls on a Tasmanian scale and how that might actually look.

SES volunteers are facing rising rescue demand and we've been hearing a lot, as I'm sure other members have, about volunteers who are paying out of their own pockets sometimes for rescue equipment. The uniform that they have on, has to be specialty, particularly if they're going into wilderness areas, dense areas of vegetation. It's concerning that, when we know that we're going to see an increase in rescues happening that there appears to be no plan to increase or provide sustained funding for the SES. Particularly, in the new TFES draft bill where it seems that the money will be drawn from the fire department. We look forward to learning more about that, hopefully before the May budget, so we can have proper scrutiny on what the future's going to look like because we know what's coming. We have to make sure that we have the resources and the supported personnel to deal with it and keep Tasmanians safe.

In the police hearing we had some serious concerns over the online crime reporting, the kind of amalgamation of the normal crime stoppers reporting into a Tasmanian police reporting that was due to be online in November last year. That's currently only delayed due to some technical issues that are ongoing, but it is promising to hear that that is still coming down the pipeline and to come after that initial crime reporting, will be the ability for victim/survivors to report sexual assaults online and we know that this is a really important part of the commission of inquiry and Weiss review recommendations. It is promising to hear that that is still happening.

In the prevention of domestic family and sexual violence space, the minister, Ms Howlett's work on the liquor licensing rebate reduction policy that the government has and ensuring that, as a part of that, family, domestic and sexual violence is very much prevalent - it's in the terms of reference for the review that's happening and it has to continue to be part of that conversation because we know the effects that it has and we've all seen the statistics of family, domestic and sexual violence in Tasmania at the moment. We cannot be doing enough in this space and I commend the minister for getting out the Family Violence Act review discussion paper, albeit pretty much bang on the day of the Estimates hearing and we only had an hour for what is an incredibly important topic that touches the lives of so many Tasmanians. We need to ensure that there is more time for this in the future and the critical documents, such as an act review, that there's more time for members to digest it and have a look at it. In saying that, I do appreciate that there is a long timeframe for feedback into that review. It is very much appreciated that it's not going to be a quick process and it's going to receive the respect that it deserves.

In skills and training, I, like everybody there, was devastated to hear about the 18 staff -17 teachers and an education manager - who are going to be made redundant two days before Christmas. Now, it wasn't that long ago under the 2021 act review or reform that it was clearly stated that no teachers would be worse off under the changes, that there wouldn't be any redundancies. Yet, we've seen automotive training in the south gutted, now we have arts, fashion, design and lab tech going, so what's next? Campus sales? That's what was touted in the 30-month act review. We asked about that review, it cited that the KPMG assessments, which were commissioned by TasTAFE, found that TasTAFE's 'footprint was too large' and that there were 'efficiencies to be found in the divestment of campuses.'

We asked for that KPMG assessment review, report, whatever it was, to be tabled and Mr Gardner from TAFE replied that the KPMG report, it is unfortunate that the 30-month review picked up on one line. We unfortunately had to break at that point, but it is unfortunate if it picked up on just one line that is otherwise a good piece of work or a terrible piece of work, but then after the break I wasn't in the room, member for Clark Ms Burnet was taking my place there. The response that we got was that the KPMG work was not available as it was not submitted as a report. It is an internal piece of advice that informed further internal work. That, of course, is despite the fact that it was referred to as a report twice previously in the hearing.

We also questioned about the Southern Automotive Centre and the new automotive centre out at Cambridge done in conjunction with the Tasmanian Automotive Chamber of Commerce and what the process was for getting that infrastructure up and running because, of course, there were other buildings, other locations that were touted previously, but this is the one that eventually a decision was made. So, what was that decision, how was it made and what process was gone through, for TAFE to be providing money for this new facility? We didn't really get an answer on what that process was, we were just told that there was nothing wrong with the process. I didn't ask if there was anything wrong with the process, I asked what the process was and, of course, now that we don't have an answer, we are more inclined to think, why was that conclusion immediately jumped to? Now, it does leave the question wide open about why you would assume that we thought the process was inept. Was it swayed to a particular developer to get this up and running?

On Primary Industries, we know that invasive species' populations are going up and the funding is not. On deer management, as a part of the response to what the government was doing, there was talk of the Australian Heritage Grant that the government's just received. Now, that's the second time that the government's received the Australian Heritage Grant. The first time, the grant was for $440,000 for deer management in the World Heritage area. The last time the Australian Heritage grant came through though, it was only $400,000 and it's not only for deer, it's for cats, it's for foxglove. Now, we are having less money that we have to do more for to spread seriously thinly. It's simply not good enough when we know the scale of the problem. The minister - the department also tabled the 15 conservation areas, reserves, FPPF land that is currently going through a level two RAA process to be opened up for recreational hunters. These are areas that are particularly high volume for tourists. There's Hardings Falls, of course, the Eastern Tiers is a spot that a lot of people go to, to look for swift parrots. I note a Level 2 RAA process does not include community input - that is despite there being tourism operators that go into some of these reserves to show Tasmania's natural environment.

We asked in the Police Estimates hearing about whether or not police were consulted on opening up these new conservation areas and reserves, and the answer was no. In fact, minister Felix Ellis said it was probably too early, at this stage, to be consulting on those lands. Well, we already have the 15 parcels of land; we know which ones they are. They're going under review through the RAA, so it should have already been consulted on well and truly, particularly when it's only a Level 2 RAA, so there won't be public consultation under that threshold. We echo our calls for Parks to reconsider that, or make an exception under the Level 2 RAA so that there is public consultation. That way, people who do use those areas for bushwalking or other recreation, or any tourism operators that have tours out there, can have their say, raise their concerns, and find out how they coexist in that space into the future.

In the Budget there is no extra funding for professional shooting of deer, concentrated shooting programs, or for the removal of the partial protections which still remain. We know that's what works, and we know that's what has to be done. How many reports and reviews are going to come out and continually reflect that that's what has to be done? Meanwhile in New South Wales, the legislation to repeal the 'Brumby Bill' that protected the heritage values of the horses in Kosciuszko has gone through. Tasmania needs to hurry up, catch up and protect our special wild areas.

There was no funding for cat management. We know that is a huge issue, particularly for local governments that are not resourced to deal with this growing issue. In the 2024-2029 cat management plan that doesn't exist, we still don't have an estimated time of when that draft is actually going to be complete, when it's going to be out for public comment. This is an issue, as with all invasive species - the sooner you get onto it, the sooner it can be fixed. We've knowingly had to do this work and just kicked the can down the road. Thank goodness the minister hasn't done that on deer, and has opened up public feedback for the 2027 plan slightly earlier.

With rabbits, it was fantastic to hear that the government has secured the calicivirus after the shortage issues and the reliance on the single lab that we have not only in Tasmania, but right across the nation. However, we have to ensure that the release happens under the right conditions at the right time of year, so that we don't see a repeat of what happened this time that we had the virus and into the future, so that it's actually effective and we can deal with those populations.

On rivers, it is incredibly disappointing and one of those things where you just bounce around from department to department, minister to minister, trying to get a result. It seems that no-one has bothered to do the environmental flows for Tasmania's rivers under the projected Marinus Link. Before Basslink came online in 2001, there was a fantastic series of studies and an incredibly dense amount of work done by Helen Lockyer on what the impacts of changed hydro flows could be to our river system. It was those reports that gave us a baseline for a lot of our river systems. It hadn't been properly investigated the whole way down. It's that report that showed us the health of the meromictic lakes at Lake Fidler, on the Gordon River, so that some attempt could be made to save them - although unfortunately, it was too late.

We have people on the Derwent River increasingly concerned - a river that has most of its water allocation already attributed to various landowners - wondering what it's going to mean for them under Marinus Link. You have berry farms and the like - this is not an area that's connected to Tasmanian Irrigation - wondering when they can take water from that riverway into the future, what are the flows actually going to mean?

The minister was talking about the Rural Water Use Strategy, which in past budgets has had around $200,000 over each of the forward Estimates each year, for various things, but in this interim Budget, it has $440,000 and that's it. What happens after that? Clearly, we are completely off track for what's recommended in the State of the Environment Report for actually looking after our waterways, for the monitoring that needs to occur. For all the reports we need to have staff on the ground, we need to have scientists out there actually looking at what's happening and projecting. We've made a decision, the government has pushed through a decision on Marinus, without proper consultation with members of this place or the community, and they haven't done any of the basic environmental assessments for it.

On that note, we're also getting bounced around on AI data centres. We've got one of the biggest AI factories in this nation, if not the world, coming to Launceston. What is planned by stage 2 of that proposal? Yet nobody - not the Planning minister, not the Primary Industries minister, not through anyone, really, certainly not the Innovation and Science minister - had any idea about the future planning of what that's actually going to mean for our state. 

You have the Office of the Coordinator-General spruiking our state as a key place for data centres for AI factories, yet no-one's looked at the planning of exactly where they should go. Where is the best place for them? Therefore, what's the best size of them? How much power are they going to consume? How much water are they going to use? Yes, the current Firmus proposal is spruiking a non-flammable, liquid cooling system. That's an incredibly novel technology that's only just come on board. If it doesn't work, where's the contingency plan for the water take? There certainly isn't any by the river at their proposed site.

If we're going to have these major consumers - and it is a major power consumer with absolutely minimal employment opportunities for local Tasmanians - coming into this state, sucking the energy dry, using our water systems, where's the actual planning for it prior to seeking major investment?

I echo the comments from other members in this place and to thank all the parliamentary staff, both in the parties, in the departments, the secretaries for the committees and in Hansard as well. It's an enormous amount of work that goes into Estimates at the best of times, let alone when it's in the middle of sitting weeks after a snap election and we're having not only budget Estimates but GBE scrutiny at the same time. They did a fantastic job and, really, we can't really express enough gratitude to them for the work that they've done.

As May approaches and we have the proper budget upon us, we're seeing an increase in community concerns grow, particularly those in the NGO sector - people from neighbourhood houses, for example, not knowing what's going to happen next. There's not a lot of confidence in what Tasmania's social economy is going to look like into the future. How can people be confident when their current services, be it health, education or housing, are already under pressure now? That will only grow under a budget that just delivers compounding debt above all else.

[8.13 p.m.]
Mrs GREENE (Bass) - Chair, from the outset, I would also like to place on record my sincere thanks to the hard-working public servants who have spent countless hours preparing brief after brief for Estimates. Their professionalism, dedication and attention to detail ensure that ministers are properly informed and that the scrutiny of government can take place transparently and effectively. I know that work is demanding, often time-pressured and rarely acknowledged publicly, but it's also vital to our democracy. Thank you for your care and expertise and commitment that you bring in serving the Tasmanian community. 

Estimates exposed a number of serious matters that I will touch on today. It exposed a pattern - a culture of detachment, avoidance, incompetence and political decision-making that leaves everyday Tasmanians carrying the cost. Nowhere was that clearer than in the Children and Youth portfolio. Let's start with the Strong Families Safe Kids advice and referral line (ARL), which is the front door to child safety in this state. Since its commencement in 2018, the advice and referral line has experienced steady growth in the number of contacts it receives each year. In many ways, this reflects an increasing awareness and trust in the service from community and stakeholders. However, more recent system and legislative reforms, including the implementation of the Child Safe Principles, the establishment of the Office of the Independent Regulator, and the commission of inquiry into institutional responses to child sexual abuse, have contributed to significant shifts in both the volume and the complexity of demand reaching the ARL. 

It's not only call numbers that are rising. The level of risk, the seriousness of those reports, and the time that is required to ensure children's safety have been escalated. Yet the government has not matched that escalating workload with resources needed to keep children safe.

What we heard in Estimates was not a story of reform. It's a case of band-aid fixes and under-investment in child safety services. Call volumes have been rising year on year. Initially back in 2018, there were about 12,900 calls to the ARL. Now it's closer to 22,000 calls. That is a huge shift, but there hasn't been any additional staff put into that service.

We know that burnout for staff is becoming the new normal. There are really high rates of workers' compensation for these staff, mainly psychological injury, and children are waiting longer for help. The government's solution, you might ask? Well, there were no plans to increase staffing; we didn't hear anything about that. There's no structural change on the agenda.

What is positive in this space was the Workforce Incentive Program, but that, again, is just another band-aid measure and, unfortunately, it seems the government couldn't even deliver that. They promised an extra $100 a week to entice workers to stay, and that was really welcomed by staff last year, but that temporary payment ran out in June. It was meant to be extended until December whilst the Allied Health Professionals Agreement was being negotiated, but that deal has yet to be struck. Workers in July, August, September, October and November, and even the start of December, I'm led to believe, still have not received their incentive payment. It's going to be back paid. They shouldn't have had to have waited five months for that money that was promised to them months ago. There was no reasonable explanation from the minister about the delay to those incentive payments. I also note that those payments were only offered to allied health professional staff within child safety services. There was no incentive payment for other key roles like disability liaison officers, support workers or unit coordinators, and those staff all do really important roles within child safety services.

We also learnt in Estimates that the government has done yet another review for the advice and referral line and, of course, outsourced the wait list at a cost of over $800,000 for just 12 weeks' work with Tradewind, so think about that. When children needed help, the government didn't build capacity, didn't build its own expertise, didn't back its own people. They sub-subcontracted responsibility and then they called it 'expanding the workforce'. Outsourcing the child safety wait list is not good enough. It was an emergency patch on a system that has already failed. 

Tradewind were contracted to progress up to 1700 conversations initially over an eight-week period, but that contract was extended for another four weeks and completed, in the end, 1120 conversations. A substantial number of matters involved concerns relating to sexual abuse and child exploitation material consisting of both recent and historical concerns. These matters should not have been outsourced to a labour hire company, and concerningly, over that 12-week period, six of those labour hire staff chucked the towel in. One of them gave up after week three. There were more in weeks five, six and seven.

I've been hearing from workers that labour hire staff didn't have access to all the information that they needed, like police reports, so practice managers and practice leaders were sending them information in Excel spreadsheets via email. That is simply not good enough. What is most disturbing is that the minister said during Estimates that if she was in the same position as the former minister, with a huge backlog, that she would have done the same thing. I would really hope that the minister will consider what she said during Estimates. Perhaps it's something that she can reflect on over Christmas, because our kids deserve better.

The ARL review identified structural confusion and duplication, staff reported feeling anxious and there is a serious problem when it comes to clarity of roles. The review also found that there were wait times of on average one hour and 22 minutes to get through to the advice and referral line. One hour and 22 minutes - that is not good enough. Then, when they finally get through to speak to someone, the conversation, on average, is about 14 minutes. Staff were then reporting they were spending considerable time on admin or taking phone calls that weren't even related to children on their wait list, so they were having to, once again, duplicate and hand over case file notes to other workers who were unavailable when somebody called.

Instead of releasing the review so that the sector and workers and advocates could learn from it and improve the system, the government locked it away. The document has now been presented at my request. So, my question is, if everything's going so well, why hide the evidence? And if everything's improving, why did they need to outsource the waitlist? These are not just statistics, they are children. A child who's been reported to ARL is waiting for safety. A family that needs support and cannot get a call back is not a line item, they are trying to survive. These are complex cases, families with complex issues, it's really not good enough. Of course, caseworkers who are overwhelmed with work, are trying to do the impossible and every day support is delayed, the risk doesn't stay still, it escalates. We cannot reform child safety via LinkedIn posts from the department, we cannot protect children with branding and graphics and new job titles and think that that's going to make a difference and we cannot announce change after change and just hope that it's going to work because children in this state need more than hope. We need to properly resource the system.

Through Estimates, we also heard reference after reference to vacancies, burnout and turnover, not just in child safety services, but everywhere the government relies on the public service to protect people because, the truth is, a system is only as strong as the people who keep it running. If you hollow out the workforce, you hollow out the system. If you don't have enough child safety staff, children wait, and if you don't have enough forensic staff, justice waits. If we don't have enough radio dispatch staff, emergencies wait, and if we don't have enough teachers and TAs and professional support staff, then learning waits and kids fall through the cracks. When the public service struggles, Tasmanians struggle.

We saw the same failures across police, fire and emergency management. Forensic science services have been under immense workload pressure for years. They are essential, they're how we solve crimes and how we deliver justice. If we cannot process evidence quickly, investigations stall, victims wait longer, our courts clog up and offenders remain free in the community. Yet, in Estimates, when asked how long the delays are and how they are being fixed, the minister couldn't provide a plan and it's worse than that, we learned that there are 789 unstarted DNA cases. They were sexual assault samples, and they have been sitting untouched since January of this year. That means that survivors, families, and the accused are all waiting because the system is overwhelmed. When governments fail to properly resource forensic science services, the consequences aren't just theoretical, they are real, devastating and already playing out across Australia.

Queensland is the cautionary tale. They underfunded their service, there were backlogs and pressure to do more with less and that led to catastrophic delays, systemic failures and, ultimately, a commission of inquiry that found thousands of DNA samples had been incorrectly reported at levels too low to detect, meaning vital evidence was dismissed, investigations derailed, and families denied justice. Survivors of violent crime were forced to wait months and sometimes years for answers. Offenders who should have been caught earlier remained free in the community. Court cases collapsed and public trust was shattered. Forensic science isn't a luxury, it's the backbone of a functioning justice system and when the government cuts corners, under-resources labs or ignores warnings from experts, the price is paid by victims, families, frontline workers and the safety of our communities.

At the same time, and this is really extraordinary, we heard in Estimates of the disaster that is the Launceston Reception Prison. A building infested with rats - yes, rats. Broken plumbing, failing intercoms, and it only has three showers and that's often for about 40 remandees. The minister himself admitted on the record that it is not fit for purpose and hasn't been for quite some time. So, we have a prison the government knows is unfit, holding people who are waiting for court appearances, which are delayed because Forensic Science Services is backlogged. If that isn't a system in crisis, what is?

This government talks about law and order, but the reality is we have rats in remand and rape kits sitting in freezers. No spin can cover that up. That is failure, pure and simple. Our community deserves so much better.

Other parts of our justice and community safety system are in trouble, too. Calls to radio dispatch services drop out in 15 per cent of cases before anyone is even able to pick up the phone. Their rosters are also a nightmare. These are Tasmanians who are calling in distress, they're calling 000 or the non-urgent line and they are desperate for help, but disconnected. That's not just a service challenge, that could be the difference for them between life and death. It might be the only time they feel brave enough to reach out - they might be calling about a family violence matter. It is simply not good enough. Our courts are backlogged and member for Clark and shadow attorney-general has already outlined the resourcing constraints of Legal Aid. I encourage you to go and have a look at that contribution if you weren't able to listen.

Community services funding is now on the chopping block, too. This comes at the exact same moment that complexity of cases are rising, demand for services is exploding, family violence services are overwhelmed, and multicultural organisations are dealing with increased safety concerns, especially post the anti-immigration rallies, which should be condemned by every member of this place. Neighbourhood Houses and charities are seeing record need in our community.

The government's budget papers confirm a reduction in forward Estimates for the community sector, and we know that organisations received letters from the government saying, 'What services can we cut, what can we trim?' That's not what they need to be hearing from government right now, I can tell you. If we weaken community services, you do not save money, you simply shift the cost into hospitals, into policing, homelessness services and the child safety system. A responsible government would reinforce the community sector, not starve it.

We saw this same behaviour across every portfolio, broken promises, delayed commitments, blame shifting, no accountability and some ministers simply refusing to own their own decisions. We saw that in Question Time today, with the North Launceston Football Club with $100,000 promised that's now been pulled because the club met with the opposition during the election period. You can dress it up how you like, that's what happened - the club know it, the community know it, and we will hold this government accountable for that awful decision.

We know that Surf Life Saving rescue services have been whisked away from the clubs who have been providing it for years and I'd love to hear an explanation for that from government. We also know that school infrastructure in my electorate of Bass has been delayed. That's Mowbray Primary School and Port Dalrymple being pushed further and further away from students who were looking forward to those upgrades to their schools.

This was my first ever Estimates and I must say, I was pretty disappointed with the deflection from ministers not providing answers and some ministers seeming to be offended by us asking the very question. That is not leadership. I note for Committee B, Mr Duigan is yet to provide answers to his questions on notice, so hopefully that can be addressed soon, that is simply not good enough.

All of this might sound quite political, but the consequences are anything but. Children waiting for safety, families waiting for support, victims waiting for justice, community organisations waiting for certainty, emergency responders waiting for staffing and resourcing that they deserve, schools waiting for infrastructure and volunteers waiting for promises to be honoured. When governments don't do their job, people get hurt. What we heard in Estimates was not simply a lack of answers, it was a lack of concern. I guess that's what really struck me out of Estimates. A government that cannot face reality is a government that cannot solve problems.

Tasmania deserves better. We deserve a child safety system that measures outcomes and invests in its workforce; a forensic service that delivers timely justice for those who are relying on it; a prison system that doesn't hold people and facilities that aren't fit for purpose, that are filled with rats and broken plumbing - that's just not good enough. Tasmanians deserve someone to pick up the phone when they call the RDS, not to have 15 per cent of phone calls drop out; our community services sector needs to be strengthened, not cut, and we need ministers who will listen to their stakeholders and their communities and not punish them for talking to other people when they were just trying to get the best outcome for their club. We want a government that honours its word, not its spin.

Governments are judged on how well they treat the vulnerable, the sick, the elderly and the young, and this government is setting the bar very low for our community at the moment. Every Tasmanian deserves a government that measures success, not by announcements but by results, and that's what I will be holding you to account for. Thank you.

Progress reported. Committee to sit again.
