Answer to Question - Tasracing
Answer to Question - Macquarie Point - Contaminated Soil

Mr ABETZ (Franklin - Leader of the House) - Honourable Speaker, in support of that motion, can I respond to some questions that were raised by member for Franklin Mr Winter, to the Premier in relation to Tasracing? The answer is:

I note the questions about this matter were put by the member and answered at the Tasracing scrutiny committee hearings on 26 November. As previously advised, this was an operational matter and the decision made by the company. 

I'm advised the Chair took immediate action when alerted to the post and has spoken to the CEO about his actions. As a state-owned company, Tasracing is not covered by the State Service Act. This was a matter for that Tasracing board and the Chair has already addressed it during the scrutiny committee hearing. If the member has other allegations, he can make them to the appropriate authorities.

Can I also provide further information in response to the question attempted to be asked by the Leader of the Greens and, in fact, asked by member for Clark Ms Burnet, in relation to what they falsely alleged was misleading evidence by the MPDC. What has occurred is that the Greens have confused volume with weight. One cubic metre of fill or soil does not equal 1 tonne, in fact it is equivalent to about 1.7 tonnes. As both MPDC advised in Estimates and I in a question on notice taken during the Estimates, MPDC advised that it estimated that about 130,000 cubic metres of fill would need to be moved from the site to facilitate construction of the stadium. If you multiply that by 1.7 to convert this volume to weight, we get approximately 220,000 tonnes. Cue breathless releases claiming that the EPA was providing a different figure to that of the MPDC was misleading. As I said in my answer this morning, to accuse public servants of misleading when they cannot defend themselves, and if the Greens would have asked 'how can you explain the difference?' we could have got the information without these terrible allegations being made about our officials.

Dr Woodruff - Suck on your own hypocrisy and look at what you did to Nicholas Gruen and the Planning Commission panel.

The SPEAKER - Order. Dr Woodruff, you can make your own contribution.

Dr Woodruff - Suck on your own hypocrisy.

The SPEAKER - Dr Woodruff.

Mr ABETZ - This has to stop, because when the Greens - I think it's only the Greens, in fact, make these sort of allegations against our officials - it is unfair, it's unreasonable and on this occasion completely and utterly untrue. It was a Greens mistake that has led them to this situation. I invite the Greens, should they speak on the Adjournment this evening, to apologise to the MPDC.

Can I also indicate that the Copping C Cell can take 45,000 cubic metres of level 3 material annually, and, in fact, it can take even 45,000 tonnes of level 3 material annually.

Dr Woodruff - How's it going to deal with 220,000 tonnes? Four times the amount.

The SPEAKER - Order, Dr Woodruff, you can make your own contribution on Adjournment soon.

Mr ABETZ - Not all of the material to be removed is level 3 material. Once again, contaminated material, but there are levels of contamination and it's only level 3 that has to go to Copping. The other levels can be used in other areas which has occurred. 

In this desperate attempt to denigrate anything to do with Macquarie Point, the multipurpose stadium, the Greens go on this jihad against anybody who supports the stadium and that includes good, hard-working public servants of this state who do not deserve the sort of denigration and personal attacks, personal vilification that they have suffered in recent times courtesy of the Greens. 

To be clear, the volume and quality of fill likely to be removed is within the industry's capacity and Copping C Cell to manage. I again invite all members to ensure that before they make allegations against our public servants, they get the facts together so that they don't make those sorts of statements. I call on the Greens to apologise to the MPDC.


Paul Reynolds - Institutional Response

[8.37 p.m.]
Dr WOODRUFF (Franklin - Leader of the Greens) - Honourable Speaker, I rise to talk about a very deeply serious matter tonight that I would expect should concern all members. It relates to outstanding questions relating to actions taken or not taken by senior members of Tasmania Police and the state government in relation to former police officer Paul Reynolds. 

It's well known that Reynolds abused and exploited teenage boys over a period of at least 30 years. The facts of that were established by the Weiss Review, which reported to government last year. However, despite this review being undertaken, a number of serious and important questions remain about Reynolds and the institutional responses to his actions, responses made during his life, and after his death. These questions continue unanswered because the Weiss Review's terms of reference were too narrow, and its powers too weak to enable a full and proper examination of relevant matters. 

This problem has again been rearticulated in recent days in the latest episode of the podcast Badge of Betrayal, produced and hosted by Jay Walkerden. The series takes a close look at the Reynolds matter and in the new episode, episode 3, Mr Walkerden speaks to a currently serving senior officer in Tasmania Police. The police officer is unambiguous. He describes the Weiss Review as, 'A failed inquiry.' To be clear, the police officer doesn't blame Regina Weiss, who was the appointed reviewer of the Paul Reynolds investigation, for the failure. Not at all. What he says is that, 'The terms of reference, when you look at it, never allowed it to be successful.'

I don't think we can overestimate how much bravery it would take or how much concern this officer must hold in order for them to speak out, but we are so glad that they've done this, and that Mr Walkerden has given them this platform because since the day the government announced the Weiss Review, the Greens have maintained it was set up in a way that made it impossible to answer the questions that need to be answered for the full truth to come out. The government did not initiate this review because they thought it was necessary or the right thing to do. They were pushed into it after the Greens publicly called for actions and after other members of parliament supported our call. When the government conceded and announced a review would occur, the terms of reference were written personally by the Police Commissioner. We expressed our strong concerns about both the narrow scope and weak powers of the review. In particular, we criticised the fact it did not cover an investigation about the decision to award Paul Reynolds with a full-honours police funeral. I quote from my statement from that day, 6 October 2023:

It is good news to see an independent investigation has been announced, but we are troubled to see the inadequate terms of reference that have been set by the Commissioner of Police. It also appears the investigation has not been given the power to compel witnesses, order the production of evidence, or refer individuals for prosecution. This investigation should be empowered to look at this deeply serious issue in full without being hobbled from the outset.

A week later I wrote to Police minister, Felix Ellis, to reiterate these concerns and suggested a much more comprehensive terms of reference. Minister Ellis denied this request and I still don't know why he wouldn't want to do everything to make sure the review was comprehensive.

The Weiss Review was released on the 4 July 2024. As predicted, it left important questions unanswered. At the time I made a statement that called for further action, saying:

This report has shone a light on extensive abuse. However, unfortunately, it did not have the scope to address all relevant matters or sufficient powers to undertake the full investigation required. We called for the government to refer the unanswered questions about Paul Reynolds with all available evidence to the Integrity Commission so it could do a complete investigation with the powers it needed to compel evidence, but this was not done.

Again, I can't find a sensible reason to explain why Police minister Ellis has never taken these steps.

As a result of this inaction, it is still the case today that the questions about officer, Paul Reynolds, known to have exploited and abused boys over years, have not been satisfactorily investigated, and critical questions remain unexamined. The Greens are very concerned about this matter

I foreshadow that I will further contribute to this matter tomorrow evening.


Ice Sports in Tasmania

[8.42 p.m.]
Ms JOHNSTON (Clark) - Honourable Speaker, I rise tonight to talk in relation to the Ice Sports report that I tabled earlier today. This is a report with regards to the closure of the rink in 2022 and Ice Sports Tasmania (IST) has continually been provided with anecdotal evidence of this, including reports of decline in athletes' psychological functioning, disruptions to their sense of belonging and identity, reduced self-worth, increased stress and difficulty coping, increase in maladaptive coping strategies and, in some cases, has resulted in significant episodes of poor mental health resulting in the need for professional support.

I should say before I continue that I do recommend to anyone who is struggling at the moment to reach out to a number of services if they find themselves with poor mental health to Beyond Blue, Headspace, Anglicare or Relationships Australia in particular. I urge people listening today to reach out if they do need help.

For this reason, IST, in conjunction with psychologist, Dr Katie Dykes, conducted a quantitative study on the mental health impacts of the rink closure. Psychometric measures were used to assess the mental health impacts and current symptoms experienced by athletes, children and adults and parents and carers of athletes. Results confirm the closure of Tasmania's only ice sports facility in 2022 has had a profound and measurable impact on the mental health of athletes and families across the state. In fact, data shows an alarming rate of clinically significant depression, anxiety, stress and trauma-related symptoms in both children and adults, with many experiencing severe or extreme levels of psychological distress related to having no rink accessible in our home state. Individuals have lost access to a critical protective factor for their mental health and wellbeing.

The findings demonstrate the absence of a permanent ice sports facility is directly related to a serious and escalating mental health issue for Tasmanians. I urge members to look at the report that I tabled earlier today. It details some very distressing facts. It showed in this report that when it comes to young athletes under the age of 18, the impact the rink closure shows that 80 per cent are experiencing psychological distress directly linked to the rink's closure; 60 per cent are experiencing severe post-traumatic stress symptoms. When it comes to mental health symptoms, 60 per cent of under eighteens are experiencing mental health symptoms or distress considered to be within a severe or extremely severe range. 

When it comes to adult athletes participating in ice sports, again, the impact of the rink closure shows that 43 per cent are experiencing severe post-traumatic stress symptoms. When it comes to mental health symptoms, 86 per cent are experiencing mental health symptoms or distress. Of course, this has a flow on effect to parents and carers with 80 per cent reporting to experiencing notable or severe post-traumatic stress symptoms as a result of the rink closure and its impact on their children.

What does this mean for the ice sports community, a very close knit community? The closure has created significant mental health impacts across all age groups. Young people are showing particularly high levels of stress and trauma. Adults and parents are also experiencing severe anxiety and post-traumatic stress. This really does reinforce the urgent need for stable, safe and permanent access to ice sports facilities alongside mental health support for the whole sporting community.

Ice Sports Tasmania understands that this is a challenging situation for many and, again, if this does invoke some stress or distress for individuals, I encourage them to reach out to support services. 

In the little time that I have left, I remind members that it wasn't that long ago, October 2023, that this House considered a motion that I put forward to support the ice sporting community. They are a sport in Tasmania without a home, without a place and without facilities. That's been the case since 2022 and this House unanimously, with support across the political divide, supported a call for this government to fund an ice sports rink facility in Tasmania within the 2024-25 Budget.

That hasn't happened, and this community has been left without a facility. Whilst we debate, in the other place, the addition of a third stadium to Tasmania, I think it's a really timely reminder that we have a grassroots sporting community that don't have a facility at all for their particular sport. Participants are forced to go to the mainland to participate, or not engage at all. They're losing their community and the mental health impacts of this are really clear. I urge members of the House to engage with Ice Sports Tasmania, to hear from them themselves the impact of this. Thank you.

Federal Labor - Impact of Policy on Tasmanian Native Forest Industry

[8.51 p.m.]
Mr FERGUSON (Bass) - Honourable Speaker, I want to talk about the most urgent matter facing our state at the moment. It amazes me that it hasn't been brought up at question time this morning. It wasn't the subject of the matter of public importance raised by the Labor Party this morning, and you'd nearly think it was not an issue at all. That is, of course, what Labor federally have done to our forest industry here in Tasmania.

In 2004, Mark Latham, the Leader of the federal Labor Party, declared war on the forest industry with a policy that he took to that federal election to shut down native forests operations in our state. It was vigorously fought against by the John Howard-led government of the coalition and the then-Paul Lennon Labor government, and we won that matter. It seems like you nearly need to set your alarm clock; every 10 or 15 years they come knocking again.

In 2012-13, Julia Gillard, in a minority federal parliament, did a deal with Bob Brown, Lara Giddings and Nick McKim, and they had a similar policy to lock up hundreds of thousands of hectares of native forest in Tasmania, with the same objective to shut down native forest operations jobs in regional communities. We didn't win that battle, we lost that battle. We lost it nobly. We fought it late into the morning in this House, but ultimately, on the basis of a Labor-Greens government in this House, we lost that battle.

The alarm clock's going off again, and I am very alarmed at what has happened, 12 years later. The federal Albanese Labor government, in the dead of night, have done a deal with the federal Greens party to strip away the legal protections of Tasmania's Regional Forest Agreement (RFA). It surprises me that anyone would find it amusing, because it is a very serious matter. It's so serious, proven by how pleased the Greens are, the high-fiving Greens, at what they've achieved in Canberra. That battle happened very quickly, very quietly, almost secretly. It happened, and then it was over. The deal has been done, and tonight, as I stand here, it's the law. 

The federal Labor government have stripped away the legal status of Tasmania's RFA, signed by John Howard, Prime Minister, and Tony Rundle, Premier, a long time ago - I think in 1998 - providing resource security for Tasmania's forest industry, which has been the basis for job security, which has been the basis for investment security, which has been the basis for the revival that we've been able to see in our forest industry since the election of the Hodgman government in 2014, which tore up that appalling deal.

Labor ministers are now scrambling, having done this terrible deal - a dirty deal - to hit the airwaves and try and convince Tasmanians and forest communities that there's really nothing much to worry about, 'You have 18 months to get ready for this.' Get ready for what? 'Eighteen months is plenty of time to get compliant, compliant with what? Isn't it already the case that Tasmania runs forestry better than anywhere else on Earth? After all, that's what Labor have said until recently. It is a betrayal of the state of Tasmania. I believe that the state opposition in this House is happy to allow that betrayal to go quietly by. They should be raising the alarm as we do on this side of the House. It worries me that some of them really do want this.

Of course, Labor's federal member for Bass in the April run-up to the May election did a radio interview with the ABC. The question from the journalist was, 'You're putting that pressure back on the state government to make a decision there, would you like to see the Tasmanian state government end native forest logging?' The answer from Ms Teesdale, and the ABC reports in square brackets 'long pause', then, 'Yes.' Well, Ms Teesdale later that same day walked those comments back and said that the Albanese Labor government is not changing its policy on native forest logging. Of course, this was to say, 'Hey, don't worry about what I said before, we're good on forestry.' Well, Labor isn't good on forestry and they've sold our people out.

This side of the House are not satisfied with those reassurances and will fight it. And I congratulate Sussan Ley and the federal Coalition for also pledging to fight it because this battle needs to be won for those 5700 forestry families in our state.

Time expired.


Tasmania's Young People

[8.57 p.m.]
Prof RAZAY (Bass) - Honourable Speaker, today, I would like to pay tribute to our inspiring young Tasmanians. At a time when young people are under so many pressures, including increasing cost of living, housing shortages and high education costs, it is understandable that many young people are struggling to see a positive future. I want to speak about something that sits at the heart of every thriving Tasmanian community, strong family, and hopeful future, the importance of inspiring young people.

Young people are not just the leaders of tomorrow, they are leaders of today. They are shaping culture, challenging old ways of thinking, and bringing fresh ideas to problems we have been wrestling with for decades. To unlock that potential, however, they need more than opportunity. They need inspiration, the spark that tells them they are capable, valued and able to make a difference, even when the going gets tough. Inspiration matters because it changes the way young people see themselves. When a young person feels inspired, they begin to imagine new possibility. They start to believe in their own capacity for growth. They take risk, they try, they fail, they try again and that's how confidence and resilience is built. Without that spark, their world becomes smaller, but with it, everything opens up.

When we inspire young people, the benefits come back to all of us. Inspired young people contribute ideas that help solve community challenges and they inspire other young people. They bring energy and creativity into the workplace. They show compassion, fairness and willingness to confront injustice. They remind us of what's possible when hope and opportunity meet. 

Inspiration alone is not enough. We also need to build environments where young people can thrive. That means safe spaces, accessible support, meaningful choices and the freedom to explore who they are. It means trusting them, including them and valuing their lived experience. It means recognising that every young person has a story, and each story deserves respect. An article in today's Examiner celebrates 40 inspiring young people. Some are young entrepreneurs, some are young athletes and coaches, some are disabled athletes, some are artists and curators, some apprentices, some are performers and musicians, some are academics and some are advocates for important causes, some are innovators and some are even young parliamentarians, including this parliament's Labor MP, Jess Greene.

At a time when 47 per cent of young people drop out out of school after year 10, we must do more to motivate young people to undertake further vocational training in areas they are interested in or feel passionate about. Not all young people want to go to further tertiary education, preferring instead to enter the workforce to gain experience. We must address skill shortages and encourage young people to consider careers in areas such as engineering. People from low socio-economic backgrounds are more likely to drop out of education, so we need to do more to address inequality among our young people.

If we want a stronger future, we must actively lift up young people today. Let us mentor them through mentor programs and encourage, challenge and celebrate them. Let us help them see the greatness they may not yet see in themselves because when young people are inspired, they don't just imagine a better world, they create one.

Members - Hear, hear.


Dream Again Mentoring Program

[9.01 p.m.]
Ms BURNET (Clark) - Honourable Speaker, last Thursday I was privileged to attend the graduation of students from the Dream Again Mentoring program, a partnership of the Be Hers Foundation and TasTAFE, which supports women from refugee and migrant backgrounds to develop skills in sewing and retail cosmetics. You were there, Speaker, so too was the Leader of the Opposition and other members for Clark, Ms Haddad, Mr Vermey, and Hobart's Deputy Lord Mayor, Dr Zelinda Sherlock.

Be Hers have raised millions of dollars for women who in other countries are fleeing from slavery, exploitation and domestic violence. Their Dream Again Mentoring program, which has run for a few years, has shifted the focus to migrant women here in Tasmania and it has also lifted those women's skills, their hopes and their aspirations. Women like Aimen, a young mother of two who was there with her husband, and my friend's, Sonya, mother who escaped, is a Hazaras woman who escaped Afghanistan many years ago.

As we know, unemployment and underemployment are significant issues for refugee and migrant women who are who are more likely to find it difficult to find work than their male counterparts. There's a 2023 report by the Victorian state government that says that just 47.3 per cent of culturally and linguistically diverse women were able to secure work, compared to 69.5 per cent of men. There are complex and multifaceted reasons for this, including racial prejudice, being denied career advancement and mentoring opportunities, limited recognition of overseas qualifications and experience, prior trauma, lack of cultural safety and exposure to culturally motivated sexism in the workplace, and a greater likelihood of working casual, unsafe and exploitative roles.

The Be Hers Foundation has been working to create opportunities for refugee and migrant women in Tasmania and to empower them to enter the job market. It was clear that both students who had done the sewing or the cosmetics course had relished their experiences and had grown in confidence. You could just see it in their demeanour and in their faces during that course.

TAFE sewing teacher, Katrina, showed how keen the women had been. They took to the industrial sewing machines like ducks to water, didn't want to take breaks nor leave at the end of the class. It was one of those situations where you could see for the cosmetics teacher and Katrina and mentors alike, that they got as much out of the program as the participants.

There was joy at this graduation, but the reality is that some of these women will not have the option of studying and learning when the minister, Mr Ellis, cuts these TAFE courses. With the Tasmanian Liberal government announcing cuts to TasTAFE, including 18 staff roles and cuts to the areas of design, screen and media, music, fashion, visual arts and lab technology, the future of this program is unclear. These women are the human faces of cuts to courses that seem expendable. This is the human cost, which is a travesty. The most vulnerable members of our community will be robbed of taking those important first steps to independence and skills.

I implore the minister, Mr Ellis, on behalf of women like Aimen and Sonya's mum who is a refugee who had fled persecution and has had to deal with mental anguish with family separated by war, who were at the graduation ceremony and Sonya's mum told the audience that she has found purpose and it has been a lifesaver for her to graduate from this course, to interact with other women and to have such good training and skills. Please do not cut these TAFE courses that make such a difference in the lives of women like Sonya's mum, so that Be Hers can continue to do their work alongside migrant women, teachers and mentors alike.


Dementia in Tasmania

[9.06 p.m.]
Mrs ARCHER (Bass - Minister for Health, Mental Health and Wellbeing) - Honourable Speaker, to acknowledge the inaugural Parliamentary Friends of Dementia roundtable today that I was honoured to co-host with fellow members for Bass, Prof Razay and Ms Rosol. Working together, this forum reflects our shared commitment to improving the lives of Tasmanians living with dementia.

Dementia is a significant and growing health issue in Australia and has a substantial impact on the health and quality of life of people living with the condition, as well as their family, friends and carers. In 2024, the Australian Institute of Health and Welfare estimated that there were 425,000 Australians living with dementia, which equates to 16 people with dementia per 1000 Australians. This means that most of us present here tonight are likely to know someone impacted by dementia.

With a growing ageing population, the number of Australians living with dementia may exceed 1 million by 2065. Here in Tasmania, it is estimated that more than 10,600 people are living with dementia. This place is a significant strain on our healthcare system and on the communities that support those living with dementia. In September, the Australian Institute of Health and Welfare announced that dementia is now the leading cause of death for Australians. Dementia accounted for almost 17,400 deaths in 2023, equivalent to almost one in 10 of all deaths, further revealing the significant impact dementia has on the Australian and our Tasmanian community, which emphasises why gatherings like today's parliamentary friends event is so important.

The group builds on the theme of this year's Dementia Action Week, which was 'nobody can do it alone.' Thanks to my co-conveners and other members of parliament, along with representatives from Dementia Australia, for working with us to make the roundtable happen and to ensure that no one is alone. The roundtable provided the opportunity to bring together people living with dementia, family carers and Dementia Alliance members to discuss their experiences.

We explored practical examples of how all members of Tasmania's parliament can make a difference for their constituents across the state and commit to creating a dementia-friendly Tasmania. Dementia Australia will now draft an action plan to champion dementia messages throughout the parliamentary community. I encourage everyone to join us in creating dementia-friendly communities where people living with dementia can maintain social connections and live with dignity and respect.

In addition, one of our government's recent election policies is to provide a new training program to enhance dementia care across Tasmania. Working with Dementia Australia, the government will provide the training model D-Esc to staff across our four major hospitals and community nursing teams. D-Esc is a workshop that provides virtual reality training on de-escalating behavioural emergencies related to dementia. This innovative training will reduce the risks of harm for both the person living with dementia and our valued health staff, as well as improved personalised care and quality of life for patients and support for their loved ones.

In closing, I would like to take this opportunity to sincerely thank our local healthcare providers, support organisations, volunteers and carers, who work tirelessly to assist those living with dementia. Working together, please consider joining us in supporting the Parliamentary Friends of Dementia Action Plan to create dementia-friendly communities, including promoting education and research to reduce the risk of dementia. 


Caravan Parks - Long-Term Occupancy

[9.10 p.m.]
Mrs GREENE (Bass) - Honourable Speaker, for decades, caravan parks have quietly housed thousands of Tasmanians. They've been the safety net when rentals were tight and an option for people on fixed incomes or looking to downsize, and particularly elderly Tasmanians. Right now, those residents and the park owners who house them are in a legal grey zone. There is no clarity, no certainty, and no modern framework governing long-term occupancy. Decisions that affect people's homes and futures are being made under outdated laws that simply don't reflect reality. These residents live with uncertainty and some in my community have been living in fear. They live with the knowledge that the Tasmanian government has left them exposed without the legal protection, security, or planning framework that they deserve. I want to be really clear that this is not a sudden crisis. This is a crisis that the government has been warned about repeatedly and one they have consistently failed to act on.

Eighteen months ago, before I was a member of this place, I brought a motion to West Tamar Council calling on the Tasmanian government to urgently modernise the laws governing long-term residents in caravan parks. I was asking for them to be recognised properly under the Residential Tenancy Act, to provide clear protections and fair processes for these long-term park residents, and also to support park owners with a framework that actually reflects how parks operate in 2025, not 1985. Every single councillor around our table supported that motion, every single one, because we could all see what was happening. We could see that residents were living in limbo, and we could see parks that were trying to operate inside this legal grey zone, which was also very challenging for West Tamar Council at that moment in time. 

We knew that the Tasmanian government needed to step up. Eventually - finally - the Liberals announced a review and when they released the details, I noticed something pretty striking. The wording, the principles, and the commitments were almost identical to the motion that was passed by West Tamar Council - in parts, copied word for word. I didn't mind, I wasn't looking for credit, I was looking for progress. Today, progress is sadly what is missing because here we are more than 18 months later, and Tasmanians are still waiting. 

The government completed its review and held its consultations. It told residents and operators that legislative change was coming. What have we seen since? We've seen evictions, we've seen stress, and we've seen legal actions - it is still continuing to this very day. No bill before the parliament. What could have happened in this time was interim protections, but this government failed to do that too. There was no clarity for park owners and no security for residents.

The Attorney-General rose in parliament last sitting and said we should finally see a draft in early December. I hope that will happen this week and then it will go back out for community consultation again. That's more delay while some of Tasmania's most vulnerable people continue to live under the threat of displacement. 

The hardest part is that residents have heard all of this before from this government. Some of those opposite were more than happy to visit these caravan parks during the state election in 2024. They posed for photos. They put their arms around residents. They gave them promises that help was coming. What good is a promise if it's never delivered? These are not people with endless options. Many have lived in the same park for 10 or 15 or even 20 years. They've invested their own money into maintaining their homes, and they are part of the fabric of their local communities. Without legislative reform, they can still be told with very little notice that they must pack up everything they own and go. We know the reality of some of these homes: they can't be moved. So, they need to leave their property there. Their investment is gone. 

Park owners are also stuck. Most are small business operators trying to do the right thing, but they're left navigating outdated laws and carrying responsibility without clear guidance. Some of these people are very vulnerable, so it's no easy task. They deserve some certainty too. 

Other states have already done this work. Victoria, New South Wales, and Queensland all have modern residential parks legislation. They've recognised the reality of long-term occupancy and built systems that protect residents and support operators. Tasmania will be the last state in the country to protect people's homes and that is not good enough. 

If the Liberals had acted when they said they would, we wouldn't be here today. Residents would have protections and owners would have the clarity that they need and this parliament would have the legislation in front of it. I hope that happens this week. People have waited long enough.


Rental Affordability

[9.15 p.m.]
Mr BAYLEY(Clark) - Honourable Speaker, I rise to talk about housing and a couple of recent reports that have been released. You only have to ask a renter to be told that finding an affordable place to live is getting harder and harder. The National Shelter SGS Economics and Planning Rental Affordability Index released in November only confirms it. They found that the affordability of renting in Greater Hobart has declined in the past year. Even average income earners are on the cusp of rental stress, with the median rent of $520 a week accounting for 28 per cent of renters' income. Even for a couple both earning minimum wage, rents are, I quote, 'Modestly unaffordable'. For low-income householders it's even worse. People on Job Seeker, pensions and single parents working part time are facing extremely or severely unaffordable rents.

This is a problem of the Liberal government's making. They simply haven't built enough houses in the last decade, and they have let Tasmania's homes be converted to Airbnbs at record rates. It's time they gave renters real rights and stopped unreasonable rent increases.

Rents are unaffordable around the rest of Tasmania as well. Things aren't much better in Launceston, Devonport and Burnie than in Hobart. If you want to find affordable rentals, it's pretty much a matter of moving to Queenstown or St Helens. It's simply not good enough. Housing is a human right, and we know that housing and secure housing leads to better school attendance, better productivity at work, better mental health and health outcomes.

Shelter Tasmania's acting CEO, Dr Lauren McGrow [OK], said that homelessness services are reporting that workers and families who have never struggled before are finding it difficult to find affordable housing. Unaffordable rents are causing people to skip meals, the cost of living and housing crisis is hitting Tasmanians hard, and the Liberals just simply aren't doing enough to help struggling Tasmanians.

They often talk about how things are getting better here than on the mainland, but it's simply not true anymore. According to SGS Economics and Planning senior associate, Kishan Ratnam [OK], and I quote:

Hobart renters are paying only 10 per cent less than their Melbourne counterparts, even though their incomes are 22 per cent lower.

While all areas are unaffordable in Hobart, in Melbourne and Sydney at least there are some areas which still remain affordable. The Liberals review of the Residential Tenancy Act is welcome, but struggling Tasmanians can't wait for action. We need some action now. 

On another report, SQM Research has said that Tasmanians are already struggling to find an affordable place to live, but many things are set to get worse again. That's according to this research which has predicted what life will be like for Hobart renters in a decade. In 10 years, they're predicting rents will rise by 6 per cent to10 per cent. For a median rental, that's a rise from $580 to $760 a week. That means Hobart renters might have to pay a staggering $40,000 a year just to keep a roof over their head. It's more than $9000 over what most renters are paying today.

The Liberals have sat on their hands for the last decade as rents have continued to rise. It's time they gave renters real rights before things get worse, and instead of focusing on a billion-dollar stadium, we should be making sure that Homes Tasmania is funded adequately to build the houses that Tasmanians need. We need to manage unaffordable rent increases; we need to end no-cause evictions, and make sure that the houses we do have meet minimum standards. With so many whole home rentals being stripped out of the rental market into short stay hotels, we need a way to regulate Airbnb. The 5 per cent short stay that the Liberals promised two elections ago, is now not set to be introduced until next year. Meanwhile, we're losing houses along the way. That really should be stepped up, and that would have helped with a $22 million injection into the budget.

Report after report is demonstrating that the housing stress and the housing challenge in Tasmania is getting worse. It's projected to get worse still. We have 5336 applicants on the priority housing register. In Estimates, we heard that many of them have one, two, three or even more children, so we know that that 5000-plus applications actually equals 8000 plus individual Tasmanians who are needing to find a home. 

We must do better for vulnerable Tasmanians. We must build more homes. We must give renters real rights because it is a human right to have a home to call your own and we are currently dismally failing Tasmanians.


Emergency Services National Medal Presentation Ceremony

[9.20 p.m.]
Mr ELLIS (Braddon - Minister for Police, Fire and Emergency Management) - Honourable Speaker, hundreds of hours of dedicated service to keeping the Tasmanian communities safe was recognised and acknowledged over the weekend in Penguin in the north-west. I rise tonight to acknowledge the dedication of our fire and emergency service personnel right across the north-west region who were recently recognised at that National Medal presentation ceremony held at the beautiful Penguin Surf Life Saving Club.

It was lovely to see Her Excellency, the Governor and, of course, Fire and Emergency Services Commissioner, Jeremy Smith AFSM, celebrating the commitment of these extraordinary Tasmanians who serve our community with courage and professionalism. The purpose of the National Medal is to honour those who've served in the Australian Police Forces, Australian Fire services, Australian Ambulance services, Protective Services, correctional services and emergency services. The National Medal is awarded for 15 years of diligent service protecting our community, a significant milestone that represents countless call outs, hours of training and moments of bravery that many of us will never see.

A large cohort that was recognised on Saturday, including individuals such as Michelle (Shelley) Marthick of Irish Town and Lyndon Young of Port Sorell, among many others with recent recipients demonstrating enduring dedication in their service to their communities. 

I also acknowledge those whose service has extended beyond the 15 years of recognition. Individuals such as Norman Berkery of Hellyer, Paul Dowling of Devonport, and Craig Stirling of Somerset, who received their First Class, marking 25 years of dedicated involvement in the Tasmanian Fire Service.

Importantly, some of Tasmania's longest serving career and volunteer firefighters were recognised. These are people who've given 30, 40 and 50 years of service. I want to particularly pay tribute to Garry Morse of Riana, Philip McCormack of Kimberley, and Russell Barker of Hellyer, who each reached major long service milestones that reflect their unwavering leadership within their communities.

A remarkable group have reached the rare benchmark of 50 years who are honoured, including Paul Bugg of Penguin - Buggy, I hope we didn't embarrass you too much over recent days but you are truly a much loved part of the Tasmanian Fire Service, and serving in an operational support brigade, I know how many firefighters are grateful for your commitment to them.

Morris Hill of Sprent, Anne McCormack of Kimberley, and the amazing Mark McDermott AFSM of Strahan, who has played such a huge role on the west coast. Their half-century of selflessness and community care is deserving of the highest admiration of this parliament. 

I also want to acknowledge and thank Devonport Brigade Operational Support Member, Richard Kostic, and Stowport Natone Brigade Volunteer Firefighter, Fiona Tustian AFSM, who among others both received their 30-year long service medal and certificates.

Our SES personnel were equally represented and recognised. People like Anthony Dick who has actually been critical to delivering the Tasmanian Government Radio Network and I've seen him in flood events and a range of other emergencies, stepping up to support our community, Harold Deverell, and 45 and 40 years respectively for those two gentlemen. Rose Mitchell and John Mitchell of Kentish, both marking 35 years, who stand as pillars of resilience and reliability in times of emergency and disaster, particularly in that flood prone community.

Behind every medal recipient is a partner, a family, an employer, and a community that supports them to step away to protect others. Their contribution deserves acknowledgement in this place as well. What strikes me most at National Medal ceremonies is not only the years of service, although those are great, but the sense of pride, humility and camaraderie that defines both TFS and SES personnel across the north-west and right across the state. These are Tasmanians who never seek recognition but who deeply deserve it. I ask the House to join me in congratulating every medal recipient from this year's National Medal Presentation Ceremony and thanking all TFS and SES members, whether career or volunteer, who continue to protect Tasmanians in their greatest moments of need. Our communities are safer, stronger and more resilient because of them.

Members - Hear, hear.

[names tbc]


Raider's Guide - Tasmanian Greyhound

[9.25 p.m.]
Mr WINTER (Franklin) - Honourable Speaker, I rise tonight to talk about the Raider's Guide and Gary and Greg Fahey in particular. They are two names that most members of this place would be unfamiliar with. Within the Tasmanian greyhound industry, they are two legends of the sport. For over 50 years they've loved, owned, trained and bred many wonderful greyhounds and none better than their late champion, Raider's Guide. Raider's Guide, winner of 41 out of his 79 starts during a sparkling racing career, was lightly raced and carefully managed throughout his career.

Trained by a Tasmanian Hall of Famer, Gary Fahey, Raider's Guide will go down as Tasmania's greatest ever stayer, produced here in the state. His resume included the Devonport Chase, Ronald McDonald Charity Cup, RL Foley Perpetual Stakes, Richard Stamford Perpetual Stakes, both Tasmanian sprint and distance finals and the winner of the Billy Grice Memorial, a touching race to win.

The lack of distance races in Tasmania, though, meant that its owners and trainers had to take the dog elsewhere across the country. A start in the Hume Cup held at the Meadows in Victoria and competing at the highest level, Raider's Guide had an impressive heat win followed by a gallant second in the cup final.

Raider's Guide was selected to compete in the invitation-only prestigious Rookie Rebel in Victoria along with another star Tasmanian greyhound Fast Minardi, a remarkable achievement for two of Tasmania's own-bred greyhounds that were selected for the event - groundbreaking for Tasmania.

Raider's Guide found the front early, and the Rookie Rebel, and won convincingly, claiming its first Group 1 victory. Winning the Tasmanian Distance Final by a massive 16 lengths, Raider's Guide was set a challenge for all winners in other states to go to the National Final being held in South Australia where, in the lead up to the National Title, unheralded interest was sought from all around the country, especially for the 'Tasmanian', as they called him. Come race time, Raider's Guide did what Raider's Guide did best, found the front and beat its rivals by eight lengths - a massive achievement and a second Group 1 win.

A stellar 2024 racing season saw the dog crowned Tasmanian Greyhound of the Year. Later on, he went to the Sandown Cup, found the front after 100 metres, held off its rivals, winning its third Group 1.

An invitation to Queensland was received. In a highly anticipated event saw Raider's Guide journey up to Queensland and in a highly competitive race he defeated his rivals to claim his first Group 2 title.

He then returned back to Tasmania, lazed around the yard for a few weeks before he was set to resume racing. In the final following week, tragedy struck, with Raider's Guide falling after suffering a bump resulting in a fracture to his cervical spine. The race night veterinary surgeon, Dr Kim Barrett attended within minutes, immediately arranging for a clinic to open after hours and transferring the dog there, where he was placed in an anaesthetised state in an induced coma for further treatment the next morning. Instructions are on record from the trainer. They say, 'Save our dog at any cost. We will sell our home.'

The next day, the X-rays were taken and sent off to the expert greyhound surgeon for evaluation, but tragically, Raider's Guide passed away after having a cardiopulmonary arrest whilst underneath the anesthetised state. Later that day, Tasracing issued a written press statement indicating that Raider's Guide had been euthanised. It was reported widely that way. This was not true. The owners and trainers have asked me to put this on record as it's been misreported here and elsewhere that he was euthanised. The Fahey family loved that dog like their own family member. The idea that they would have just euthanised this dog has been emotionally distressful for the Fahey family who had just lost not only their best ever greyhound, but a loved family member that they'll never forget. No one ever picked up the phone to find out the exact circumstances. Whatever happened to the ethical conduct that they should have received? A complete veterinary report was available, but no one bothered to check it. Maybe the truth wasn't what people wanted to hear. 

Distraught, the family tell me they phoned and expressed their distaste to many people but the media coverage continued. They suffered. The 82-year old trainer was traumatised and heartbroken and had to be hospitalised himself, such was the grief and stress and suffering that he experienced following the death of his loved dog and family member but also the media coverage following that. 

The Fahey family are just one of hundreds of families who love their dogs and greyhound racing in this state. They would do anything for that dog and they would have sold their home for that dog. It wasn't euthanized for the sake of it. The dog died tragically and they will never forget a great Tasmanian athlete, bred, trained and owned by a wonderful Tasmanian greyhound family.

The House adjourned at 9.30 p.m.
